“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANCE.
Coventry Patmore.
f , , of-ofpd (which is published price with
On reoeint at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to I , ,
On receipt at Ihe Bulletin
Coventry Patmore is a poet who does not attract
the multitude. Yet his message is singularly
sweet, and for kindred souls he has an irresistible
esharm. Alice Meynell, who has made a selection
from his works which she calls “The Poetry of
Pathos and Delight” (ss. Gd.)—an admirable in-
troduction —declares that “ many poets are heard
because a chorus of contemporaries sings with
them and like them. Mr. Coventry Patmore’s
voice is single in his day, and single in our
literature. It makes part of no choir heard by
numbers, and so it needs an attentive ear. To
that attentive ear it sounds alone, as the divinest
voice of our time. ”
* * * *
Endorsement of this appreciation is plainly a
question of mood and temperament. The three
extracts which follow —chosen mainly for brevity
• —may help unacquainted readers to decide how
far they can follow the eulogist.
THE LOVER.
He meets, by heavenly chanoe oppress,
The destined maid ; some hidden hand
Unveils to him that loveliness
Which others cannot understand.
His merits in her presence grow,
To match the promise in her eyes,
And round her happy footsteps blow
The authentic airs of Paradise.
For joy of her he cannot sleep ;
Her beauty haunts him all the night;
It melts his heart, it makes him weep
For wonder, worship, and delight.
O, paradox of love, he longs,
Most humble when he most aspires.
To suffer scorn and cruel wrongs
From her he honors and desires.
Her graces make him rich, and ask
No guerdon ; this imperial style
Affronts him ; he disdains to bask,
The pensioner of her.priceless smile.
He prays for some hard thing to do,
Some work of fame and labor immense,
To stretch the languid bulk and thew
Of love’s fresh-born magnipotenee.
No smallest boon were bought too dear,
Though barter’d for his love-sickJife ,
Yet trusts he, with undaunted cheer,
To vanquish heaven, and call her Wife.
He notes how queens of sweetness still
Neglect their crowns, and stoop to mate ;
How, self-consign’d with lavish will,
They ask but love proportionate ;
How swift pursuit by small degrees,
Love’s tactic, works like miracle ;
How valour, clothed in courtesies,
Brings down the haughtiest citadel;
And therefore, though he merits not
To kiss the braid upon her skirt,
His hope, discouraged ne’er a jot,
Out-soars all possible desert.
THE SPIRIT’S EPOCHS.
Not in the crises of events,
Of compass’d hopes, or fears fulfill’d,
Or acts of gravest consequence,
Are life’s delight and depth reveal’d.
The day of days was not the day ;
That went before or was postponed;
The night Death took our lamp away
Was not the night on which we groan’d.
I drew my bride, beneath the moon,
Across my threshold ; happy hour !
But, ah, the walk that afternoon
We saw the water-flags in flower !
THE TOYS.
My little son, who look’d from thoughtful eyes,
And moved and spoke in quiet crown-up wise,
Having my law the seventh time disobey’d,
I struck him, and dismiss’d
With hard words and unkiss’d,
His Mother, who was patient, being dead.
Then, fearing lest his grief should hinder sleep,
I visited his bed.
But found him slumbering deep,
With darken’d eyelids, and their lashes yet
From his late sobbing wet.
And I, with moan,
Kissing away his tears, left others of my own ;
For, on a table, drawn beside his head,
He had put, within his reach,
A box of counters, and a’red-vein’d stone,
A piece of glass abraded by the'beach
And six or seven shells,
A bottle with blue-bells,
And two French copper coins, rang’d there with
careful art,
To comfort his sad heart.
So when that might I pray’d
To God, I wept, and sard :
Ah, when at last we lie with financed breath,
Not vexing- Thee in death,
And Thou rememberest of what toys
We made our joys,
How weakly understood
Tliy great commanded good,
Then fatheifly, not less
Than I whom ’lhoffihast moulded from the slay,
Thou’lt leave Thy wrath, and say,
I will be sorry for their ehdldishness.
Notes.
Marriott Watson says the just word of criticism
in a Bookman notice of William Watson’s new
poems:—
The eloquence of his melodies is almost the most per-
suasive part of his high talents. . . . The “ Hymn
to the Sea ” impresses one rather as a dignified and beau-
tiful exercise than as a real achievement. It is not the
offering of a faithful worshipper; it is the compliment of
a polite stranger.
Watson is not a lyric poet at all, says Watson.
Here is the only poem in the new volume to set
against this judgment:—
Oh, like a queen’s her happy tread,
And like a queen’s her golden head 1
But, oh, at last, when all is said,
Her woman’s heart for me 1
We wandered where the river gleamed
’Neath oaks that mused and pines that dreamed
A wild thing of the woods she seemed,
So proud, and pure, and free !
All heaven drew nigh to hear her sing,
When from her lips her soul took wing;
The oaks forgot their pondering,
The pines their reverie.
And, oh, her happy, queenly tread 1
And, oh, her queenly golden head 1
But, oh, her heart, when all is said,
Her woman’s heart for me !
Yet even here the beauty is rather of sound and
form than of spirit. As, perhaps, in this delicious
sonnet: —
I think you never were of earthly frame,
O truant from some charmed world unknown 1
A fairy empress, you forsook your throne,
Fled your inviolate court, and hither came ;
Donned mortal venture ; wore a woman’s name ,
Like a mere woman, loved ; and so are grown
At last a little human, save alone
For the wild elvish heart not Love could tame.
And one day I believe you whl return
To your far isle amid the enchanted sea, —
There, in your realm, perhaps remember me,
Perhaps forget: but I shall never learn 1
I, loveless dust within a dreamless urn,
Dead to your beauty's immortality.
His recently-published “ Letters ” show that
Matthew Arnold considered Swinburne verbose,
Carlyle’s message superfluous, Macaulay intellec-
tually vulgar, Tennyson, “with all his tempera-
ment and artistic skill, deficient in intellectual
power. ” Thackeray he did not think a great
writer. Some suggestion here —and a trifle of
acidity for a quietist philosopher ?
“ ‘ Beside the Bonnie Brier Bush,’ ” says a critic,
“ga*ve us the first-day-in-the-week Drumtochty;
in ‘ The Days of Auld Lang Syne ’ we have the
parish as it is six days in seven. ”
Henry Lawson’s “ In the Days when the World
was Wide, and Other Verses,” including all his
best Bulletin pieces, will be ready next week.
Post-free from Bulletin office, ss. Gd.
\Vm. Watson’s new poems (with a fine portrait
of the author) cover some 05 pages of print, at
an average of perhaps 14 lines to a page, and the
price is 3s. 6d. nett (4s. 3d. posted). To people
who like quantity for their money, this will seem
poor value. With a publisher's catalogue thrown
in, the small Bvo. volume is not half-an-inch thick.
Certes, the new poets know how to make profits.
Mr. W tson’s work must now be sought in eight
volumes ; and the whole could probably be packed
into the space of a single Shakespeare play.
David Blair’s two daughters have on the stocks
what is described as a very outr6 book.
Angus and Robertson are to publish in their
Australian series Dyson’s “ Rhymes from the
Mines and other Lines ” —mostly from The Bul-
letin.
Alexander Sutherland, of Melbourne—a more-
or-less historian, humourist, poet, and many other
things—has just finished an elaborate work on the
“Development of Ethics.” Much of it was
discussed with Henry Gyles Turner —an authority
on commercial ethics, certainly.
Pierre Cassee : One verse will show your parody’s
quality :
Now wades the dingo in the glimmering tank,
And all the solemn scrub seems locked in sleep,
Save where that bullock-bell goes clonk urn -clank,
And blended bleatings mark the drinking sheep.
Maoriland booksellers—ower Scotch and canny ?
—order “ The Banjo’s ” poems in twos and threes,
while one admirer writes from Rangitata
that “*had I had 20 copies I could have sold them
on this and adjoining stations ; and only yester-
day I was offered 10s. for my own copy.” Where-
fore would-be Maoriland readers are counselled to
order direct from The Bulletin Book Exchange.
By the way, £2 2s. is already offered for the large
paper edition, which was sold out immediately on
publication. The first sheets of Lawson’s poems,
also to be issued next week by Angus and Robert-
son, "justify the prediction that his success will be
equally great.
Recent Publicotions.
Justin McCarthy described George Eliot’s
“ Spanish Gipsy ” as the clever attempt of a
woman of genius who is not a poet to write poetry.
Edward Arnold’s “Light of Asia ” is the similar
attempt of a man of talent. It has two or three
effective passages, but is chiefly interesting tor
the view it gives of Buddhist philosophy, ine
new cheap edition (2s. Gd.) is illustrated, and
prettily issued.
A remarkably cheap book is the reprint of
Lane’s “ Arabian Nights ” (3s. 3d.), well bound
and printed, and running to 400 large Bvo. pages.
There are some omissions, but no expurgations,
and “ Ali Baba ” and “ Aladdin ” are added from
another source.
Lady Cook is a laborious New Woman who
writes with zest on sex -questions. Cnee an
energetic American girl (Tennessee C. Clafun !)
she yoked an English baronet to her conquering
car, and he sweats at her bidding for the regenera-
tion of Monstrous Man. Gordon and Gotch are
agents for the Lady’s third series of essays (9d.),
which are attractively compounded of sound sense
and sound nonsense.
Aimee Fabregue’s “ Crucifix ”-—the love-idyll of
a leper—i« in the original a clever study in delicate
emotional prose. I). Havelock Fisher's transla-
tion (Is. ?W.) —the second volume of the Towei
Cosmopolitan Library—is uncommonly well done.
Racey Schlank’s “ poems ”•—an Adelaide publi-
cation —are crude and commonplace enough, but
hint of coming capacity to do much bettor work.
The reprint of Langbridge’s “Poets, at Play”
(4s. Gd.) gives in cheap form one of the best collec-
tions of humorous verse in the language. It con-
tains not only the stock pieces, but many out-of-
the-way and admirable items, and many from
recent writers.
“The Stolen Bacillus ” (35., 45.), H. G. Wells’s
collection of rei>rinted short stories, shows the
scientific bent of the author of “ The Time
Machine.” The motives and treatment are fresh,
and there is—O marvel! —not a woman in the
bookful. Generally the fancies lack substance—
rarely interest.
Stanley Weyman seems to have found the limit
of his literary tether. His new work, “ The Red
Cockade” (.'is., 45.) maintains his standard—no
less and no more. Certainly, no one nowadays
has a better knack of historical narrative, or a
greater talent to invent and handle incident.
Given a little humor, a little pathos, a little emo-
tional strength, and Weyman might rank as a
classic. As it is, he tells a very interesting story.
“The Red Cockade” deals with the beginnings of
the French Revolution, and the hero is rather
less monotonously lucky than “A Gentleman of
France. ” However, he has desert enough to win
his fate in the shape of the shrinking yet spirited
<young lady who is Weyman’s stock female charac-
ter. The book is readable.
Grant Allen’s “British Barbarians” (4s. 6d.)
seems to plagiarise “ The Wonderful Visit ” idea
and spoil it for lack of humor. A young man
from the 25th century is made the medium for
expounding Mr. Allen’s now-familiar “ views ” on
sex-questions. The exposition is dull.
Periodicals.
The Album changes its issue from weeWy to
monthly. With the handsome Xmas No. (Is. 6d.)
comes a set of beautiful illustrations of orchids.
Percy Spence cleverly illustrates a story in the
second number of the Ludgate Magazine (new
series, Is. 6d.). A wealth of fine engravings dis-
tinguishes this magazine.
Dr G. L. Mullins, of Sydney, has a striking
article on “Cancer in N.S. Wales” m the Jan.
Aust. Medical Gazette. His generalisations
are interesting, though the period covered by his
statistics is too brief to make their foundation
certain. He claims to have already shown that
the Australasian-born population is not subject to
phthisis, at any rate in Queensland and Maori-
land ; ” and he is now led by a comparison of the
birthplaces of cancer victims to believe that
“cancer is not a prevalent disease among native-
born Australians.” His other conclusions are
1. Although New South Wales has one of the lowest
death-rates in the world from cancer, the disease is
undoubtedly increasing in the colony.
2. The cancer age is from 35 yearn upwards.
3. Cancer is slightly more prevalent among males than
among females. The proportion is as 1-035 to 1.
4. The deaths from cancer occur chiefly among natives
of Great Detain and Germany.
5. Climate appears to have little or no effect on the
production of the disease.
. The stomach is the organ most affected in males, the
uterus in females. The stomach is largely affected in
females.
/. Heredity is the chief cause of the production of
canctr. Chronic irritation is an important factor,
but its true significance ( i.e ., whether it can originate
cancer per se) is still a matter of dispute. _ Meat-
eating, alcohol, or tea-drinking, tobacco-smoking, &c.,
are not primary causes of cancer.
Conan Doyle’s new story, “ Rodney Stone,” the
scene of which is laid in England at the beginning
of the century, will first appear serially in the
Strand Magazine.
Stead announces that his Review Xmas No. —
“Blastus, the King’s Chamberlain is the first
of a series of similar annuals. It is tedious
enough to suit the stolid middle-class mind mar-
vellously. Key-romances want a dexterous hand
to be readable beyond their little circle, and Stead,
who is insufferably dull when he preaches, now
preaches continually. Yet he was a brig 1 t
journalist once. (It seems a long time ago.)
There is something in this, from the Speaker :
Whatever the newspapers may say, the peoples of
Europe hate the idea of war with a holy intensity of pur-
pose. If war should come, it will be the fault not of tl.o
nations but of journalists, diplomatists and statesmen.
“Last summer,” writes Paul Bourget in
“ Outre-Mer,” “I was passing through
Beverly, near Boston, at the time of the
death of one of the most distinguished officers
of the Federal Army. The corpse was to be
carried to Baltimore, and a funeral service
was first celebrated in the little village
church. In the midst of the ceremony a
young man entered, drew near to the coffin,
gently raised the pall, tapped the lid with
his finger, and said softly, ‘ Steel, not wood.’
Then he disappeared, in the midst of uni-
versal surprise ; it was a reporter.”

THE HAIRY MAN OF KOORAWTHA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There iow beneath whose savage scowl the
low land cowers m craven dread
A fierce black ridge that nature seemed to have
wu f °rmed ln a furious whim,
Where gibbers grey through the scrub show bare
a au Wart ? and wens on a woolly head
And th S P nned shepherds in bygone days as
a a 11 ,unt . a monster grim.
And all whom evil chance allowed that being’s
shape to scan 6
With white and shuddering lips avowed ’twas—
ugh ! —just like a man !
Well, years had passed in a hum-drum way since
vrri last been seen of men,
hen Mick Mulleary came along in search of a
vacant block,
And coolly put in his corner peg within a mile of
the monster s den
Quit * Pi irp J-i ndeed t 0 have dropped upon such
f( T a splendid run for stock.
if 1 must choose betwane yahoos and settlers’
tt ; sheep, said he,
‘ I’ll take my chance wid the hairy men,” and he
chuckled with churlish glee.
So he settled down like a pioneer and was well
content as a bear might be
ill he took a fit in the field one day at. the
sound of a splitter’s axe,
And he growled to his wife as he rallied to, “ Is
there no law for the like of me ?
Be the powers of war I’ll make him shift in
less than a brace o’ cracks.
I here s the rabbit-pest and all the rest of the
plagues we’ve got to face,
But there’s divil a nuisance among them all to
aiqual the human race.”
I hen he rigged himself from scalp to toe in a
a suit of dingo-skins
And stole away to the mountain side while
«rs muttering in undertone,
Oonsarnin’ that splitter beyant,” said he “if
he s annyways frisky about his pins ’
111 make him clare out of camp as fast as the
divil wmt out of Athlone.
And thin, maybe, they’ll lave me free whin they
hnd, as sure as they will,
d here are rale yahoos and bunyips and things
around Koorawatha still.
“ But hould on now; is it mad ye are that ye’ve no
regard for a loaded gun ?
Fwhat a splendid mark for a sportsman ! Och !
the saints be kind this day J ” .
He stood aghast, for fair in front was the thing
itself— the hairy one —
There, peering over a clump of rock not twenty
yards away, J
In nasty truth a face uncouth—all nose and mouth
and ears,
And a straggling beard like stringy-bark, and hair
all spikes and spears.
Limp as a rag with awful fright Mick felt just
then he’d lost his legs,
But soon re-action braced him tight and gave
him wings instead ;
And nearing home he came upon his wife on the
hunt for turkey-eggs,
Who, unaware of his wild disguise, let out a
screech and fled.
He chased her straight through the cow-yard gate
mid a racket of terrified howls
I rom freckled young savages, mixed with the yelps
of curs and the cackle of fowls.
Time kills romance. A candidate drove up one
day to Mulleary’s gate
And sought with greasy smile and speech a solid
vote to score,
But Mick could only gape and stare, for in the trap
before him there
Was the very face he’d seen amongst the rocks
three years before.
At length, in pique at non-success, the face began
to sneer.
“No doubt,” said he, “you hit it well among
your neighbors here,
Although a passing glimpse of one was quite
enough for me;
But p rhaps he was a friend of yours, a relative
may be ? ”
“It’s not the hairy-man,” said Mick, “ye need be
afther fearin’
As I belave he s had a shave— and gone elec-
tioneerin’. ”
TOM FREEMAN.
BOULIA ON SYDNEY.
Oh, I’ve been there ! You needn’t skite ;
I’ve been in Sydney !
Boulia for me—a town that’s quite
Another kidney.
In Sydney you must wear a coat,
And also collars;
In Boulia you can “act the goat,
If you’ve the dollars ;
Can strip stark-naked if you like,
And black your hide,
And even ask the landlord, Ike,
To “come outside.”
There’s not a pub. in all your town
Where, calm and peaceful,
A Western man can settle down
To fill his fleece full.
Your coppers, cabs, and ways all rile—
Act like a blister :
I loathe your darned soft-soapy style—
Your “sir’’and “mister”;
And rushing crowds, who hurry so
Their various courses.
Why don’t the white-faced beggars go
And catch their horses ?
The thousand shops, the miles of streets
In your “ big smokes ”
Sicken one like a box of sweets
Or “Book of Jokes.”
So many women, too, I say,
Are almost hateful—
It takes one’s appetite away,
Too big a plateful;
Against ’em all I’d any day
Back red-haired Julia,
And, blow of Sydney as you may,
I’d rather Boulia.
Q.
R.A.F.

The LADY or The Tiger

Mr. Frank R. Srockron.
A SOCIAL QUESTION.
Thera are carriages set
apart for ‘ ladies only,’ but
the other sex have to chance
their company. And that
this is no small risk has just
been proved by the incident
reported from. Tasmania,
where a lone man with a
pulmonary complaint found
himself the only fellow-
traveller of an hysterical girl.
As he was taking a dose of
cough-mixture her disordered
imagination smelt what she
thought was chloroform, and,
believing that ho meant to
drug her, she jumped out of
the train at the risk of her
life and laid a serious charge
•gainst him.”— Daily Paper.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—■
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One l ouna
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year. .
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney, in that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
Cheques, Draffts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
j> Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
I Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, SOOO words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
We are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in ltlack and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS. .

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, Queensland,
S. Australia, Westralia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales is now £l, with 2s. 2d.
added for postage. England £l, with 6s. 6d. added for
postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 1, 1896.
A Matter for State Interference.
Bather more than two years have passed
since the great bank-reconstruction epoch,
and the results, so far, have been by no
means up to expectation. Out of the 12
banks of issue which resurrected themselves
on that memorable occasion only two have
since released their deposits in full, and
resumed business on a thoroughly sound
footing. One of the 12 is now dead, and is
going through the process of “ revelations”
which follow decease in such cases. Another
has reconstructed a second time, and, having
obtained large concessions from its creditors,
it looks as if it might ultimately get through;
and a fifth is trying to do a second recon-
struction, though it is too early yet to form
any definite opinion as to its prospects of
success. Of the remaining seven, three are
regarded as safe, and the ultimate fate of the
other four, and of one or two little institu-
tions not included in the 12, is still more or
less uncertain. The shares of at least five
out of eleven survivors are almost wholly
unsaleable, and in three cases it would
probably be necessary to pay a handsome
sum to get them taken away. In one or two
cases things are growing so much worse that
the only apparent alternatives are more
smash or more reconstruction. And, as the
British depositor has not shown himself of late
in a very friendly mood, there is at least as
much chance that the end will be one thing
as the other. Under these conditions, cer-
tain of the Australian Governments should
consider whether it isn’t advisable to do
something, or prepare in good time to do
something as soon as it may be wanted, to
meet the danger. This suggestion is not
made in the interests of the managers and
directors, whose doings in many cases may
thereby escape the exposure they deserve.
Nor is it advanced in the interests of the
shareholders, whose speculations are strictly
their own private funeral; nor in the inte-
rests of the British depositor, about whom
The Bulletin cares very little ; nor even in
those of the Australian depositor. But another
bank crisis, even on a very small scale, would
mean more industrial paralysis, and much
indirect injury to thousands of people who
never had any connection with any bank
whatever, and in their interests there is fair
excuse for State interference.
* * * *
At the time of the 1893 re-construction,
some of the banks had already sunk their
capital and reserve-fund, and part of their
deposits, in investments where the principal
was doubtful, and the interest very doubtful
indeed. They reconstructed partly in the
hope that many of these bad investments
might become good again through a general
revival ; partly with the idea that they might
accumulate profits with which to gradually
wipe off their losses ; partly, it may be, to
get time to cover up some of the more
hideous evidences of their mismanagement
and make the revelations rather less awful
when they had to come ; partly to get time
to die in peace ; and mostly, it would seem,
on the general principle that it is always
good to postpone the calamity as long as
possible. Also, there was the hope, which
is never wholly abandoned, that even in the
most dreadful situation something may
“turn up.” But nothing did turn up for
those who most needed it. The doubtful
investments in most cases went bad, and in
the persistent depression many of those
which seemed good became doubtful, or
worse. Interest dropped instead of rising,
and the bank that had lost or locked up its
capital and reserves and part of its deposit
money, and could only invest what cash it
had left with difficulty, at low interest,
and yet had to pay high interest to its
depositors, found itself in a bad way.
Dividends stopped in many instances. The
earnings, even if they were all earned, were
barely sufficient in the worst cases to pay
working expenses and interest, and there
was practically nothing left with which to
write off past losses and prepare for future
repayments. The situation, in several in-
stances, is decidedly worse than it was in
1893, and there is serious danger that it may
become worse still unless something is done
to improve it very shortly.
* * * *
The only visible remedy lies in obtaining
a large reduction in the rate of interest on
the old fixed deposits. The interest is un-
doubtedly much too high under ordinary
conditions, but it was made high because the
security was decidedly low. And now the
banks which most need assistance can only
ask for a reduction on the ground that the
security is a great deal lower than it was
before. The case which they have to present
is something like this : ‘ ‘ The security which
we have to offer is much worse than hitherto.
The return of prosperity which we promised
hasn’t come, and for the present doesn’t look
at all like coming. You gave us a certain
amount of consideration before on our as-
surance that we could pay up principal
and interest with ease, and our assurance
proved to be wholly unreliable. We
now offer you our equally valuable
assurance that if you give us more con-
sideration we can pay up in full.” That
is the sort of case which any bank that wants
further re-construction has to lay before its
creditors, and the result, in England-at all
events, would almost certainly be a demand
for a complete and independent investiga-
tion of the accounts, which is the one thing
that is not wanted at present. So far as
common report goes, there are now several
Australasian banks which, with time and a
reduction of their interest-burden and no
dreadful revelations to burst up whatever
confidence remains, may get through all right
in the vend with decently honest manage-
ment, but even mild revelations just now
would probably mean panic, and panic would
mean a sudden end of everything.
* * * *
The most promising road out of the diffi-
culty which seems to be coming is by Govern-
ment interference, but on a much wiser plan
than that pursued by the Seddon Ministry
in the case of the Bank of New Zealand. If
the Government offered 2\ per cent. State
debentures in lieu of the present 4| per cent,
deposits they would, The Bulletin believes,
be gladly accepted, the increased security far
more than compensating for the reduced
interest. It would then have to guarantee
the security of all other deposits and
take over the management of the concern
until all its obligations were met in full.
If it charged the bank 2f per cent, it would
get a considerable sum per annum out of the
transaction towards covering its risk. The
enormous saving in interest would provide the
bank with a decent annual revenue, and, as
the State management would pay no divi-
dends to shareholders until all liabilities
were provided for, the whole net revenue
would go towards writing off past losses.
Moreover, the existence of a State guarantee
would make a panic and a consequent run on
the bank’s resources practically impossible,
therefore some of the present cash reserves
could either be invested in revenue-pro-
ducing securities or employed to pay off part
of the existing liabilities, and thus reduce the
interest oharges. The State could incur no
risk until the entire capital had been ex-
hausted and had proved insufficient to cover
losses, and even then it should be possible
for a concern which paid no dividends and
obtained its deposits at a minimum rate of
interest to gradually accumulate profits to
fill the gap. Even a rotten bank with
a State guarantee behind it could do
a large and profitable business, and might
slowly work itself back to a condition of sol-
vency. A rotten bank, in private hands,
is doomed just as soon as the public becomes
aware of its rottenness ; under the control of
a decently honest Government its original
condition would matter nothing at all so long
as the State guarantee held together. Ite
State could even put the management anc
the directorate in gaol without destroying
the bank’s credit —which no body of private
shareholders could do.
* * *
It would probably be a work of many
years to put some of the existing institutions
on an absolutely solid basis, but to the State
years matter very little — it goes on prac-
tically for ever, and it has all the time there
is in which to do the work. M liether in the
end the institutions thus treated became
public property and were amalgamated into
a State Bank, or whether they were handed
back to the control of the shareholders when
the work of rehabilitation was complete, is no
part of the present argument. There are at
least eight good reasons why no more Aus-
tralian banks of issue, good, bad, or indiffer-
ent, should be allowed to go into liquidation
for want of State interference. Australia
cannot stand any more financial collapses at
present ; it will take it many years to recover
from the amount of collapse it has
already got. It can’t stand having any more
huge masses of property thrown upon the
market at a time when a good part of the
continent is already for sale or to let unfur-
nished. It is weary of liquidation, which is
the slowest, saddest, and most costly .agony
yet invented. A banking crisis means the
bursting up of industries, widespread bank-
ruptcy, and general loss of employment, and
the unemployed difficulty is much too severe
already. The State can avert these things at
hardly any risk, and what risk there is will
be more than covered by the gain to the
public revenue, which always falls off woe-
fully in the presence of any great financial
disaster. It can also, by giving the support
of its credit, reduce considerably the exorbi-
tant interest which this country is paying to
English depositors, and the mere fact of
familiarising the British public with 2|- per
cent. Australian securities may help along
the next loan-conversion materially. And
further, there is every reason why the State
should do its best to keep Australian banks
afloat in order to prevent English institutions
getting a larger lien on the business of this
country than they already possess. Also,
there is a prospect of earning the gratitude
of a great many people, with a view to the
next general election, and of finding billets
for some deserving politicians, and various
other things.
* * * *
Even if the interference of the Seddon
Government in the case of the Bank of New
Zealand should prove none too successful,
that has no possible bearing on the scheme
herein outlined. In the matter of the
B.N.Z. the Government interference did not
procure any reduction in the rate of interest
payable on deposits, and that part of the
heavy load was not diminished one iota.
And, on top of this, the bank’s burden was
increased by the borrowing of £2,000,000 at
the ridiculously high rate of 4 per cent.,
which, considering that the State could easily
procure money at 3|, was a hopeless piece
of mismanagement. Therefore, the net
result of the State’s interference was that an
additional liability of £BO,OOO a year was
piled up on the already tottering bank ; that
the country assumed the responsibility of it;
and that it gave the bank’s creditors what
was practically a guarantee of all liabili-
ties without exacting any concessions in
the shape of reduced interest in return
for this greatly improved security. The
Bulletin’s idea is that the Australasian
Governments, where State support is neces-
sary, should exact the full price of the addi-
tional security, and the money thus saved
should be applied towards wiping out old
losses and putting affairs once more on a
sound footing—which is a wholly different
thing. Only a very strong bank could stand
much of the kind of support given by the
Seddon Government.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Voice of Parsons.
The very latest story which is going the rounds
of Australia, as a proof that this country should
walk cheerfully and without fear of danger into
the Japanese treaty, is that of one J. L. Par-
sons, of South Australia, who has lately
returned from a visit to the Land of the
Chrysanthemum. There will be no influx of
Japanese into Australia, says Parsons, because
the Japanese are not an emigrating race.
“ The Japanese can come to Australia now, but they do
not and will not in any large numbers. It is much to be
hoped that the decision with regard to South Australia
connng under the provisions of the Imperial treaty will
not be clouded by any bogus representations about
Japanese swarming into Australia. In my opinion there
is absolutely no need to fear that any such thin- will
happen.” &
This is the comforting sort of bosh which is
based on the theory that what has not hap-
pened in the past can’t possibly happen in the
future—in other words, Jamieson couldn’t
have invaded the Transvaal in ’95 because he
hadn’t invaded it in any previous year The
Japanese, as Parsons truly observes, can come
freely to Australia now, and they haven’t over-
run the country up-to-date ; therefore he
argues, it is quite safe for Australia to'give
away her present right of exclusion and bind
herself to let them come in freely for
ever. The obvious fact is that they
haven t come in any great numbers in
the past because there was no direct line
of steamers to bring them, and because for
want of means of communication, they knew
next to nothing about this country. In other
words, the Japs, didn’t come to Australia in
any very great numbers prior to, say 1895 for
the same reason that the Brit.sh didn’t come
before 1786—because they had hardly heard
about the place and couldn’t conveniently get
there The fact that the British didn t rush
Australia before 1786 is just as much a proof
that they haven’t come here since as the com-
parative absence of the Japanese m the past is
that they will always remain absent. Also,
the fact that there were practically no Italians
in Argentina 25 years ago, despite the fact that
they might have come there any time in the
previous two centuries, proves by the same
process of logic that there are none there now,
vet the Italians are fast becoming the dominant
race in the River Plate republic. And another
development of Parsons’ line of argument
proves that there is no British population in
the Transvaal, because there was none a while
ago; and that the Japanese haven’t raided
Hawaii in great numbers because the Jap. was
a stranger there until very recently ; and that
there are no English in England, for they hadn’t
come there in all the ages from the begin-
ning of the world until about 1500 years
ago ; and that the Spanish race is not in
South America, and that nobody is anywhere
unless he came there so far back that history
doesn’t record a period when he wasn’t there.
All which things are interesting as well as
logical. And, to follow out Parsons’ line of
argument, if the Japanese man doesn’t want to
come to Australia then there is no reason why
this country should sign a treaty giving him
special and extraordinary facilities for coming,
since the treaty would be of no use to him.
And if he is specially anxious to get that treaty,
then the natural assumption is that he does
intend coming. If anyone asked Parsons to
sign a deed by which he was authorised to
jump on Parsons’ head without legal conse-
quences, and assigned as a reason for Parsons
signing it that he wouldn’t make use of the
permission if granted, that gentleman would
probably see the point of the matter. His
natural reply would be that if the other man
didn’t mean to use the power he was quite as
well without it, and if he did intend using it
he ought not to have it. Which is the Japanese
case exactly.
Wanted, a Boom.
It is characteristic of the fact that the public
never really learns anything, that the Victorians
are now craving for a gold-boom with a very
great crave indeed. The province hasn’t yet
anything like recovered from its land-boom,
and it wants, if possible, to have another great
glare and smash on top of its present disasters.
Victoria, as the Age and other papers point
out in mournful tones, is producing three times
as much gold as Westralia, its yield is increas-
ing faster, its recent Gippsland finds are more
sensational than anything recently discovered
in the West; yet there is no boom, no inrush of
British capital, no big companies being floated
in London—nothing ! And Victoria ought to
be truly thankful for it, but isn’t. Because
the southern province has no boom, its recent
gold discoveries mostly go to enrich, in a mode-
rate way, a large number of individual diggers.
None of them make a big pile, with which they
can clear out of the country on a large scale,
and go to move in British sassiety and become
K.C.M.G.’s, but a lot of them make enough
to live comfortably, and distribute a
good deal of new-found gold among local
tailors, grocers, bootmakers, grog-dealers,
and other deserving and undeserving persons.
Also, they make enough in many instances
to pay some local firm to put up crush-
ing machinery, and thereby find work and
wages for some more people who want it badly ;
and their efforts are slowly opening up new
districts, and helping along various depressed
backblocks towns, and the result is that a little
more money is getting into the shire treasuries,
and a few more men find work in making roads
in the new gold districts, and these men
support more tradespeople, and so on ad
infinitum. There is no wild dazzle about the
movement, and consequently there is no big
burst looming at the end of it. If the British
boom that the Melbourne papers want so badly
were to come things would be very different.
The finder of a sensational little patch wouldn’t
take out the gold and spend it locally; he
would rush off to England with his find and
sell it in London for .£70,000 cash to a British
company. Then he would probablv stay there,
and be more or less of a British dude for ever
afterwards. A <£500,000 co. would be floated,
but the <£500,000 would go to the robbers
m London, and Australia would see none
of it. A dignified and high-toned British
manager would come out, instead of the
managerial billet being given to a local man,
and he would bring a stupendous 50-head
battery with him because the dem’d “colonials”
coulcln t be trusted to make these things, dont-
cherknow. When the gold was extracted it
would go to Hingland to pay dividends on the
Hinglish capital. The directors would be in
London, so the fees for their 1 services would go
there also ; and the only money which would
be expended locally would be the wages of the
working miners, who would be paid at the
lowest possible rate, and who would find just
as much employment under local management.
Also, the news of the boom would bring to
Victona the unemployed of the other six pro-
vinces, and the country would have to support
them somehow or other. A British gold-boom
aolrl fnU t Ung , for a P rovince which has no
wl ir.bß l 18 Sl m P y going 011 the hluff; for one
anS vw 8 ° C / ,S aU unm itigated disaster ;

Federation and Secession.
“C M‘Kay SmitiT” writes to the SM
whicl“ s UP boun a d V 1 ° f AuStralia “ Federation
sooner or later ° ° ome “ P f ° r dlsc ussion
moreoTthe colonies possi t?ility ? iIity that one or
tion may, after a time, o-eftired <§ thl . Australlan
draw from it, whether hv ntr-mv. f the buslness and with-
in that case what would become by force '
set aside as a federal oaniMl . th . e neutral territory
by a province eiecW"" ** to be bounded
status ?In other worH= e ec ,ted to revert to its original
otner words, what would become of the


hon. C. A. Goodciiap’s federal city of Hawkesburv-if I
may put a name to it—ring-fenced on all sides other than
its sea front by New South Wales, which has tried federa-
tion u ith the result of not finding it good enough V
lliis suggests a lot of things, but principally it
wakes up the whole secession problem. If there
is to be a right of secession—and The Bulletin
is distinctly of opinion, for reasons which will
appear later on, that there should not be—it is
tolerably certain that all the districts of any
particular province won’t be unanimous on the
point. Therefore, if a majority, say in New
South Wales, wanted to secede there is no just
reason why it should be allowed to force the
minority to secede along with it. A small
spot like Sydney, if it wanted to set up
again for itself, probably from motives
of pure selfishness, has no claim by sheer
force of numbers to compel the Barrier district
to go with it, simply that the Barrier trade
might be driven to Sydney by the Federal
tariff instead of going to its most convenient
outlet, Adelaide or Port Augusta; neither
should the Sydney majority be allowed to
coerce a minority in Riverina, if Riverina found
it more profitable to stay inside the Federation
and trade with its nearest port, Melbourne.
Or, again, if the Federal Government planned
a new railway policy which would enable the
far south-west of Queensland to find a short
road to the sea at Port Augusta, and the Bris-
bane majority decided to secede so as to
shut off this outlet and put the people
of the south - west on the wrong side
of the Federal Customs barrier, and thereby
make them trade at greatly increased expense
with the remote city of Brisbane, there would
be no semblance of justice in a constitution
which allowed it to do so. Neither should a
Melbourne majority be allowed to drag the
Wimmera district, whose interests lie in Ade-
laide, out of the Commonwealth for its own
selfish ends. Again, if the Federal Govern-
ment started to build the Esperance Bay rail-
way, to give the Westralian goldfields a short
road to the coast, the majority in and around
Perth would probably w T ant to secede so as to
shut down on the scheme and save that city
from practical obliteration ; but for the good of
Australia in general that majority should be
suppressed by the larger majority of the other
provinces. To put the question shortly, if a
majority in (say) New South Wales should
have the right to coerce a minority into
secession, then a majority in Australia generally
can claim an equal right to coerce a minority in
New South Wales into remaining in the Federa-
tion. If there is to lie a right of secession at
all the right should be vested in small divisions,
say in counties. Then, if Sydney and the country
whose port it is wanted to resume their
state of isolation the Riverina, whose natural
outlet is Melbourne, and the Barrier whose
natural outlet is Adelaide or Spencer’s Gulf,
would be at liberty to decide their fate for
themselves instead of being voted down by the
population of a far-off city with which they
have no natural trade interests. Similarly, if
South Queensland wanted to separate, it would
have no power to drag the North and far West,
which have few interests in common with it,
into the contract; and Perth could not coerce
Coolgardie and Esperance Bay ; nor Melbourne
compel the Wimmera to secede along with it
and send its trade again to the remote city by
the Yarra, instead of remaining in the Federa-
tion and seeking its natural outlet down the
Murray. And the difficulties in the way of
this arrangement are so many and so great,
involving a complete readjustment of debts
and many other things, that it would be a great
deal more simple and effective, and probably
quite as equitable in the end, to make the
Federal bond eternal, and have no provision for
secession at all. There is no use in having a
scheme of secession unless it is at least reason-
ably equitable, and to arrange an equitable
scheme seems practically impossible.
A Matter of Wills.
It is probable that the will under which the
Anglican Bishop of Riverina derives the major
part of his episcopal revenue will be submitted
toN.S.W. Parliament at an early date, with
the object of obtaining the cancellation of one
particularly obnoxious clause in the document.
It may be remembered that the will in ques-
tion, while setting apart a sum of money for the
support of the Bishop of Riverina, did it in
terms which appear to make it indispensable
that whoever is appointed to that see must be
consecrated in England. Dr. Anderson, when
appointed to the office, might have been conse-
crated in Australia and have thus entered on
his duties at once, but the fear that he would
by acting in this sensible way deprive himself
of the monetary advantages of the will was too
real, so he went to England, whence he re-
turned a couple of weeks ago, fully qualified
from a legal point of view to capture every
penny the tantalising testator dangled before
Holy Church. But the procedure is admittedly
obnoxious to Australian sentiment, covertly
offensive to Australian Anglican Episcopacy,
and, as a piece of practical business, a down-
right foolishness, and hence the words of the
will are forthwith to be submitted to legal
analysis and (for the conclusion is foregone)
afterwards to Parliament, with a view to
having the vexatious conditions expunged from
the document. So far, the matter is wholly,
or almost wholly, ecclesiastical and sectional
in its interest, but when it claims the assist-
ance of the courts, and especially when it enters
Parliament, it should create an occasion for
the performance of a much-wanted, long-
deferred and high public service. Unquestion-
ably the vexatious and foolish portion of
the will comes within the common-sense of
Parliament to annul: but only when Parlia-
ment is prepared to annul what is unjust,
vexatious and silly in wills in general.
Why should a will in which a hus-
band devises all or most of his wealth
to strangers, leaving his wife practically
a pauper, be considered an inviolable instru-
ment ? Why should a man who bequeaths to
a religious organisation or establishment the
bulk of all of his substance, throwing wife and
children practically on the world, be held up as
a model instead of being treated by the public
courts as a crank or a fraud ? Why should a
man who confers an allowance on his wife have
power to restrain her while she lives from re-
entering matrimony, or from living where she
nkes or with what people she likes? The
Kaverina Bishop’s demand for an alteration in
testamentary law as applied to his own see is
just but equally just is the demand of the
public that testamentary law all-round shall be
made amenable to the principles of fair play
and common sense.
Fetishes.
Bull Jingo is taking to himself immense credit
tor having demolished the fetish-houses at Coo-
massie, and claims to have ended for ever the
sacrifice of human victims in Ashantee. A
mission to civilise and enlighten the heathen
anywhere is as grand an excuse for buccaneer-
ing and looting and annexing territories as
was the plea for the “burst” of the
reformed Scotch drunkard that his “ whustlin’
canary was deid.” Religion and civilisation are
lc whistling canaries of the Maxim-armed
buccaneer Powers that want to spread— not
religion and civilisation, but themselves. It
may or may not be accidentally fortunate that
Bull Jingo has ended a reign of gory fetish
orgies; but —does not Bull Jingo himself
yearly, weekly, daily, and hourly perpetrate
to his particular fetish millions of human
sacrifices ? Human habits are matters of
training. The barbarian whose habitually
violent soul revolts not at the religious
sacrifice of and feasting on human flesh is
only a parallel edition of the so-called
civilised ” sheep who accepts a living death of
pauperdom, disguised slavery, toiling famine,
and caste domination, under the fetish of a
“ civilised religion ” that bids him “order him-
self lowly and reverently to all his betters,”
and declares to him, on a lying claim of divine
inspiration, that “it has pleased God to call
him to “ that state of life ” which means
groans and subjection to the Subjected Many,
and the swinishness of choice to the Exalted
Few. Ye who have eyes to see, ye who have
ears to hear, ye who have minds to understand
—Revise your Fetishes ! Say, ye logicians, ye
theologians, is the idolater who prostrates his
body and soul before Fat Man—Christendom’s
Jin-de-sidcle God—one grain less to be pitied,
one acorn more advanced toward freedom and
self-control, than the flat-nosed savage who
“bows down to wood and stone?” Revise
your fetishes? Powder up the latest Golden
Calf and build us another god.
Weights and Measures.
The Bulletin respectfully rises to ask all
people concerned if there is any valid reason
why Australia should adhere for ever and ever
to the stupid and cumbersome system of
weights, measures and money which it inherited
from its British forefathers, instead of adopting
the simple and convenient metrical system of
France and other countries. Under the metrical
system all moneys and all measures, whether of
capacity, weight or area, increase by
tens. The centigramme is ten milligrammes ;
the decigramme equals ten centigrammes ; the
gramme equals ten decigrammes ; the deca-
gramme is equivalent to ten grammes. There is
only one table of weights for all purposes, and
the system goes with unfailing simplicity and
regularity all through. The British system is the
most confused thing ever invented outside a
madhouse, and it almost doubles the burden of
education by its useless complexity. It in-
cludes three principal varieties of weights and
many of less importance, and none of them
have any semblance of connection with the
other. There is a separate table of weights
used by goldsmiths only. Another wholly differ-
ent one is used by apothecaries; another by wool-
dealers ; another by hay-dealers ; another by
most other kinds of dealers. A “ legal stone ”
is 141 b. ; an English stone in weighing meat is
81b.; a stone in weighing glass is 51b., while a
stone of wool is 161 b., or is supposed to be.
A hundredweight is not 1001 b. as its name
implies, but 1121 b., though in the United
states it is really 1001 b., which makes serious
confusion. Apothecaries' fluid measure has no
earthly connection with any other fluid measure.
There is considerable trouble in measures of
length through the link being7’92 inches and the
rod being 5£ yards; and the perch of 30. J square
yards is an insult to arithmetic and common-
sense. Neither in money, weights, measures
of capacity, measures of length or anything
else does British arithmetic go by any rule
whatever ; the fact that there are 12iu. in Ift.
is merely a reason why there should be 5£ yards
in a rod or 317 grains in a carat and
carats in an ounce ; and the square vards
which go to a perch are only another solid proof
of the wisdom of the system whereby an acre is
a square piece of ground measuring each way
208 ft. BJin. and about a speck over, and a mile
at sea should equal ITSI miles on land. For
many years past the press has risen at intervals
to ask why something isn’t done to amend all
this frantic confusion, and it is advisable that
it should keep on rising. The British weights
and measures were compounded out of the con-
cussion of many strange, wooden-headed
systems in the Dark Ages, and these systems
got telescoped together with the shock, and the
mass, being duly stirred up with a shovel,
assumed its present shape and remains there.
The "Well-Connected” Criminal.
The “ well-connected ” criminal that we all
know by heart now, and who is always let
down easily, put in a debauched appearance in
Melbourne the other day before Magistrate
Panton. He was alleged to be an English
8.A., a Bachelor of Medicine, and a solicitor
and barrister. Also, he was employed in the
office of a Victorian country lawyer, and during
his boss’s absence he forged a cheque for a small
sum and went on a great orgy with the pro-
ceeds. The prisoner was “ well-connected in
England,” wailed his counsel, and Mr. Panton,
after hearing the circumstances, gave him “the
rising of the court,” which meant about three
minutes’ imprisonment. As his offence was a
small one, this paper has no special objection
to the sentence in itself, but there is much
room for objection to the way it was arrived at
—or attempted to be arrived at. What all
decent people now earnestly desire to know is
this when will an honest, fearless, and abso-
lutely independent “ beak ” arise in Australia
who will commit for contempt the lawyer who
pleads that his client is well-connected ? The
said plea is a gross outrage on justice,
and the lawyer who presents it insults the
court, and the magistrate or judge who allows it
to be presented without promptly suppressing
the legal gentleman, insults himself. The plea
is always put forward in mitigation of punish-
ment, and the fact of it being put forward
distinctly implies that the magistrate is
expected to let off the well-connected person
lightly, and administer to him a different
variety of justice from that served out to the
prisoner who isn’t well-connected—in short,
that he is expected to corrupt the processes of
justice in favor of the man of “good family.”
Lhe statement, made almost in so many words,
is as follows : —“ Your Worship, there is one
kind of law for the common person and another
kind for the well-connected person, and we
want the other kind. You will please take a
crawlsome view of this case, because the
accused s friends and relatives move in good
society. A person whose relatives move in good
society should be let off where another
person would get six months or, perhaps,
two years. It is outrageous to suppose that
the law doesn’t give a certain amount of lati-
tude to a well-connected person ; it professes,
of course, not to do so, but we are behind the
scenes and know better. Your Washup, this
a well-connected person, and I claim that he
be let down easily in consequence.” .That is
not what the lawyer says, but it is just what
he means, and he means it so obviously that he
ought to be run in for contempt there and then
for meaning it.
Gruesome item from Albury (N.S.W.)
A small grazier was proceeded against at the police-
court to-day under the Diseased Animals and Meat Act,
tor disposing of a cow affected with tuberculosis. The
Government veterinary surgeon deposed that the beast
was badly affected with tuberculosis. Defendant called
several witnesses of lengthy experience among stock, who
swore that the beast was perfectly healthy. The cow
defendant stated, had been milked daily by him, and the
St. n SO U in town until a week ago, when he sold her.
The Bench, in view of the conflicting evidence, ordered the
beast to be slaughtered (O Solomonic beak !) and a post-
mortem examination made. This was done, when
the animal was found to be literally rotten with tuber-
culosis. On both lungs tumors of the size of emu-ee-irs
were found, filled with yellow matter, and the other
organs were affected to a hardly less degree The
veterinary surgeon states that the milk from such'a cow
would be a potent agent in spreading disease among
children. He is confident that many children died yearly
from disease contracted from this cause. Defendant will
appear before the Police-court again to-morrow. In
making the inspection of the dairies of this town the
veterinary surgeon condemned one cow, the milk of which
had been consumed by a number of families. The animal
was one mass of disease.
Moral: Always boil your milk.
Thus Bishop Anderson, of Riverina (N.S. VY.),
just arrived from being consecrated in Hing-
land : °
. * believe that a religious education which is ill-defined
indefinite, and undogmatic, is one which will not do for
the sons and daughters of the English people.
And voices present said “Hear, hear.” By all
means, if the Englishers like ; but so much the
worse for the sons and daughters of the English
people. For this is to say : Let all arts and
all sciences and all other branches of know-
ledge progress, evolute, develope, broaden,
fulfil themselves, but religious education, being
“defined, definite, and dogmatic,” remains
squared, and measured, and chiselled out, and
cannot be added to nor taken from. Man’s
soul may expand, his one-time rudimentary
soul-processes may become developed into new
limbs or organs, but these shall not avail him ;
his chunk of religious education is to be for
ever the same—“ defined, definite, and dog-
matic.” Monstrous tyranny of Anglicanic
hierarchy ! Still more monstrous that such a
monstrosity should scoff and jeer at and
denounce its brother hierarchy of Popery !
The men (outside Parliament) who run the
bush Labor Unions in New South Wales, and
the Labor men who represent bush electorates
in Parliament, are not, on the whole, a happy
family. Just now the quarrel revolves about
the Bourke agglomeration of Labor and Labor-
in-politics bodies and Sleath, M.P. for Wil-
cannia. Seeath expected a walk-over, but
suddenly (“ in order to circulate money,” as he
put it) a certain Murphy announced himself as
a candidate. Thereupon two prominent
unionists, friends of Sleath’s, wired to the
Bourke headquarters for money to carry on the
fight. Next day £25 came; and then there
wasn’t any fight. The money was handed to
Sleath, M.P., and he left. Presently the
executive committee at Bourke wanted
the money back, and asked for it ; the
acting secretary suggested at the same time
that Murphy’s sudden appearance and dis-
appearance didn’t look nice. Upon that,
Sleath, M.P., became offended—and his man-
hood would not permit him to refund the money
until the imputation had been withdrawn.
For the next nine months various secretaries
and Sleath, M.P., spent their hours in
writing “smart” letters to one another—or,
rather, to the public ; for very early in the
correspondence the probability of the letters
being published was mentioned. Published
now they have been; and they fill about a
dozen columns—about as melancholy an exhibi-
tion as Australian Labor-unionism has yet
furnished.
It is now announced that Johnson, imprisoned
for perjury in connection with the infamous
Butler-Rofe case, is to be liberated under the
First Offenders Act. This is about the meanest
compromise the Reid Govt, has yet effected.
If JoHNSONi. deserves to be released, then the
Government, when it refused to release him a
few weeks ago, did an act of brutal and wilful
injustice to a much-wronged man. If he doesn’t
deserve to be discharged and the Government
did its duty the last time the subject turned
up, then it is now perverting justice and show-
ing itself unfit for its position. Or, as a possible
alternative, it may be that the Government
only ventures to do a thing like this when its
lord and master, Attorney-General Want,
is away. Johnson, by the way, got three
years for trying by allegedly false evidence
to save his sister from ruin through the
conspiracy of which solicitor Rofe was a
proirunciifc part, and Rope, who was convicted
or being many times worse than Johnson,
i ion t go to gaol at all for his conspiracy, and,
though the Judge who convicted and released
mm plainly stated that he had committed per-
jury as well as conspiracy, the Reid Govern-
ment didn t even prosecute him for that second
onei ice. And why, morally speaking, Johnson
should get three years for perjury while Rofk,
tor a similar otienee in connection with exactly
the same matter, wasn’t even tried, passes the
comprehension of every fair-minded person.
I Tactically, of course, the reason is known to
every schoolboy in New South Wales.
Victorian Leg. Council is very pleased with
itself. All its own bills are becoming law, and
it keeps on putting all the Assembly bills into
the w.p. b. Last session its financial policy—•
more retrenchment and no land-tax—was forced
on the country. This session it has rejected
the One-Adult-One-Vote Bill passed by the
Assembly, and it is seeking excuses from
sundry sweaters and Fat Men to squelch the
democratic Factories Bill. It means, further,
to completely alter the aspect of the Federal
Hill. As agreed by the Tas. conference of
l rentiers, the bill passed by the Assembly
provided that the delegates to the convention
slum hi be the direct representatives of the
people. The Council proposes to make
1 arl lament appoint the delegates, pro-
bably reserving four appointments for it-
self—thus completely effacing popular interest
in the convention, and securing a powerful
representation of Fat Man in Vic.’s delegation.
Ihe idea that these 48 rich landlords are to sit
for ever on the chest of Vic. is satisfactory to
no one but themselves, and the Assembly will
presently have to make another fight to secure
its lost ascendancy. The problem is difficult
and harassing, but for desperate constitutional
diseases the desperate remedy of Black Wednes-
day is always available. The Tas. contract
was for a conference democratically elected,
and it would be absurd to hold it if the Vic.
delegates were merely the nominees of a clique.
+-
\ ic’s fatuous Opposition tried to hatch its
usual addled egg last week. Premier Turner
poes not ram his bills down the Assembly’s
throat, and the Opposition says it is a sign of
weakness. Once a fortnight, therefore, the
hatching of a new Government is attempted
but the incubators get the usual result, and the
smell of one more bad egg would annoy the
people if they were not accustomed to the inflic-
tion. When Attorney-General Isaacs pre-
sented his Companies Bill at the end of last
year, and kept a solid majority against all vital
amendments, MTntyre and his noble army of
three said it was Obstinacy. When Turner
withdrew his Railway Trust because the House
was against it, MTntyre, etc., said it was
VVeakness. Turner’s view is that the passage
of bills merely to maintain the Government’s
dignity is an effete scandal, and that the
Government is the instrument of Parliament—
not vice versa. The difficulty is that he is try-
ing to carry out the principle of elective
Government with a noil-elected Ministry, but,
in the meantime, he is affording an object-lesson
of much value, lhe present Vic. Assembly
was elected to oust Patterson, and perhaps it
will not be long before it has to seek more
detailed instructions from the country. In the
meantime, Turner is justified in treating the
Assembly as a General Committee, and not as a
House divided against itself.
Maloney’s (Vic.) notice of motion “ to restrict
security to the mortgaged property,” and the
Homes Protection Bill (on the lines “of the suc-
cessful Homestead laws of several of the United
States) are democratic steps in the right direc-
tion. Ihe Bulletin has always denounced
the system by which the mortgagee gets not
only the land, but usually everything else the
unfortunate borrower has. The bargain is with
reference to the land, and although the small
print, couched in highly technical terms, of the
mortgage may say more, the borrower, in 99
cases out of 100, is utterly unconscious of it
till he finds the bailiff in. Until the Home-
stead Law is passed, anything like permanent
home life for the Victorian farmer is practically
impossible.
Ihe practical importance of the movement
advised by The Bulletin to capture and
democratise the municipalities is illustrated by
the answer (if it is not a red-herring) given by
a Minister to Prendergast when he demanded
periodical analyses of the food used in the pro-
vince. He was told that the municipalities
had ample powers to do this, and that it was
inadvisable that the Govt, should interfere
with their functions. If this is so, every
municipal candidate should be questioned at
every meeting as to whether he will actively
work to secure the exercise of these powers, to
abolish adulteration in articles exposed for sale,
and to prosecute every person, whether a whole-
sale or retail dealer, however high and mighty,
who has on his premises any adulterated article!
Thus might the evil of adulteration be stopped
—that is, of course, if the Minister, when asked
for bread, has not given a stone.
Hear Bulletin, — Apropos your recent com-
ments on the Canally (N.S. W.) poisoning case,
let me draw attention to the gross injustice
perpetrated by the Justice Department in
bringing persons hundreds of miles on such
flimsy evidence as was adduced in the case
alluded to. When the accused has been
acquitted he is turned adrift to tramp his way
back probably without a copper in his pocket
—or otherwise stop in town and starve. lam
not sure whether the authorities did this in the
Canally case, but I know for a fact that it was
done in a similar case at Wentworth not long
ago. Such procedure seems to be the law, and,
despite the loss and anxiety sustained by the
legally-innocent man, he has no redress what-
ever. The least the authorities can do in such
cases is to issue a free pass to those wrongly
arrested.— Nixey.

Sydney, January 23, 1890.
Mr Dear Moorabinda—
This happened before the duties came off—
a iterm that always sounds to feminine ears as
if duties were back hair or a loose petticoat. A
China boat arrived with, inter alia, a packet of
silk for a local Hebe, but how to smuggle it ashore
was the question, for the C.O.’s keep a very sharp
look-out on tubs from the Flowery Land. The
lady, however, had another friend—a usually
smart detective—whom she sweetly asked to bring
her home from a musical party to be given on the
s.s-. Hookilah, and the ’tee gladly consented, and
proudly marched up the wharf with Hebe on one
arm and her innocent-looking roll of music under
thpotfyer, while the Customs men, who knew him,
looked!on in mute admiration of the “altogether.”
Arrived at the hotel, however, the ’tec felt rather
chilly down the back when ttlb music-case was un-
rolled and shown to contain a lovely symphony in
a dozen yards of pink silk !
Music-cases, however, occasionally fail to fulfil
their destiny—especially violin-cases. The mind-
apipearances maiden, who does the Block with
what appears to be her Strad in its coffin, very
frequently carries nothing more imposing than her
morning's shopping, which may range from chiffon
to' chops, Beople who won’t carry parcels, or
heavily-laden bags, frequently have recourse to an
empty fiddle-case, with which to go a-marketing,
ana, after all, it’s a harmonious deceit—l only
object to it because I’m afraid of its spreading.
A‘ slender girl with her violin husk filled with
ribbons and lace is all very well, but I am
frightened of fat women coming out with big,
booming ’cello eases, which they intend to fill
with pork and greens. Also, I dread the recur-
rence of. what once happened on the North Shore
ferry: a viol in-case carried by a perfectly-lovely
girl opened by accident and disclosed —a crayfish.
Apropos of smuggling, on Hit that some of the
officers who ply between Sydney and Melbourne
on local steamers occasionally have a bad time at
the hands of their lady friends this side who
generally come tripping down at the last moment
with dutiable goods neatly done up and addressed,
which they implore the flabbergasted sea-dogs to
“get ashore somehow” with the usual feminine
disregard of consequences (to other people). Some
of these packets are quite imposing, I am led to
believe, and must puzzle the ingenuity of the
mariners to dispose of—that is, of course, if they
undertake the job. But whether they do or not,
it’s not the fault of local femininity if the local
mariner isn’t a regular Baul Jones (boy’s-size) who
ought to have his vest examined ala Brough in
“ The Passport ” every time he goes ashore at a
protected port looking fatter than usual—l mean
the mariner.
Smuggling reminds me that in highly-protected
ports it is sometimes a most embarrassing thing
for a' woman to prove that her gowns are not duti-
able and.have been already worn. A moderately-
refined woman doesn’t leave ear-marks of wear on
a frock that perhaps she has only sported twice,
and it is sometimes a difficult matter to prove
previous wear to the satisfaction of ignorant
officials’Unless the frock shows some sign of it on
the collar or the binding. I have heard of a
travelleress whose dresses looked perilously fresh
and clean, and whu, in desperation, slightly
marked theinside collars with black lead-pencil to
satisfy.the morbid craving of the C.O.’s for some
evidence of wear and tear; and I also know of a
voyageuse who easily landed a brand-new gown at
Melbourne simply by sewing a couple of well-
worn arm-hol($ “ preservers ” into the brand-new
bodice. When the Gee-Gees landed in America
th6y had a great to-do to get their frocks in un-
taxed, and it was only after miich heated argu-
ment that the ladies proved that the dresses had
been worn, though how they proved it remains a
mystery, especially if the dresses did look fresh.
As a rule, a stage-costume that has seen service
betrays it by a half-mourning tinge all round the
floor-end thereof.
The latest way to read character is by the teeth,
but I hardly think it’s “deep” enough for any-
body but a school-girl. It’s so easy to read teeth
where you have both fact—real grinders—and
fiction—false ohes. But people with artificial
fangs have at least one coin fort—nobody can pos-
sibly read their characters aright, since the pro-
pensities shadowed forth in the purchased article
do not belong to’em Though occasionally they
may make tilings a little awkward. Some
advanced tooth-purveyor, for example, who doesn't
believe in having .an even set like two lines of
crockery tombstones, on tlie ground that Nature
is more or less irr gular, m.iv rig up a row like
Gabbett’s (as seen ;»,r .a recent theatrical production)
in some pretty woman s jaws mid -et all the tooth-
readers sizing her up as a sub-rosa cannibal iu eon-
sequence. •
But I believe in teeth-reading. ft's so true.
When you see a person with moss ad over her
chewing apparatus you can bet a hat that her
nails-won't stand investigation. Also, a woman
with all her front teeth set in a sort of gold post-
and-rail is likely to be careless how much money
she s|lends on herself; and a woman with vine
buck tooth and the other missing may safely be
summed up as having little personal vanity. And
the girl who grins at yon like a clown in order to
show that she has a real gold tooth in the back of
her head will probably jilt Adonis and marry
money with a skull like a hen’s egg.
I do not feel qualiti d to give any masculine
instances of the art ot tooth-readnig. Men’s
teeth do not play s:eh a forward game as
women’s teeth ; most men hide all possible dental
defects with a caretiilly-trained mo’, through
which their teeth are seen glittering like
tombstones in the starlight or esle gleaming
like rows of cut-out orange-peel—which are the
teeth tobacco generally bestows upon its most
devout disciples, I notice. To read any man’s
teeth intelligently the enthusiastic tooth-render
should wait until his barber has him by the nose.
And, talking of teeth, reminds me that a Sydney
shark—harbor variety—was caught the other day,
and a set of false-teeth, with the stamp of a
Sydney dentist thereon, was found firmly em-
bedded in its stomach. Whether they remained
as the only undigested asset in a first class tragedy,
or whether they were the long-lost fangs of the
lady who didn't feel well at sea —that is t .e
question.
Since the mercury has been fashionably engaged
in breaking the record, Sydney girls have taken
to going abroad without gloves, whether they
have rings or not, and whether they are manicured
or not. The heat is a most natural looking oppor-
tunity to show off a pink nail with a polish on ;
but when it comes to badly trimmed finger tips,
with odd-shaped nails and a suggestion of halt-
mourning in their depths and no polish to speak
of, I certainly think gloves are an advantage.
And it’s a “ bloomer ” (as the bad little boys say)
to suppose nobody notices. The world is cynical.
When it sees a bare-handed woman, the first thing
it expects is a ring, and the second thing it looks
for is a manicured expression about the finger-
tips. Therefore, maidens who wear their paws au
naturel should have either one or the other (both,
is better still) as a valid excuse.
I have always credited men with being rational
creatures. Heaven alone knows how my faith
was shaken in the dog days by the spectacle of a
youth proudly what remains of the block in
a pair of white duck trousers and a tall silk
hat after the fashion set long ago at
Sydney races by the Earl of Dudley. Apart
from the hideous incongruity of the thing,
the rule generally is to keep the feet warm and the
head cool, but this youth stood the old notion com-
pletely on its head. Imagine the conventional
soul of a man who emancipated himself as far as
cool pants, but had to rush into a shiny belltopper
to show he hadn’t quite gone astray ! What a
typical woman was lost in him ! By the-way, I
have discovered a new use for Johnuie sticks—as
bonnet-pins. Gussie, in the high wind, crooks the
top of his crutch over his hat, and there you are.
In “ Hide and Seek,” at Sydney Criterion, the
ladies of the co. were a heavy handicap on a show
that had many bright masculine points about it—
Wilfred Shine’s clever Character-sketch of a
“ laird,” and Frank Roberts’s “ waiter, ’ not for-
getting Mr. Dunbar’s decayed tragedian—but the
women were not even clotlies-props. Comedies
take a lot of dressing, and somehow, when a span
of alleged actresses come on attired in doll’s rags,
more or less (for what else does a bright-red frock
with a yellow fichu - like beef and mustard-
amount to?), the play loses its grip upon you on
the spot. One, with a fiat hat on overrun with
red flowers, like tomatoes on a soup-plate, had a
voice like a bassoon, and talked to reach an audi-
ence half a mile away; another, in a rose-pink
slip, had a bad attack of Australian accent, and
said that her hasty marriage was due to “tite.”
If in “ Hide and 3eek ” the ladies had only been
vaguely spoken of as a great force existing behind
the scenes, the result might have been different,
for the play went Smoothly and was adequately
staged, barring the window-curtains, which pre-
sented an appearance of having been nailed up in
a hurry.
Apropos theatres, the latest notion in America
is to have large armchairs placed under each
proscenium-box, intended for persons of “ un-
usual size ” whom, the ordinary chairs don’t
fit. I think it an excellent idea. A few of these
scattered about the Sydney shows would certainly
relieve the d.c. pressure on first nights when the
fat woman with the rattling bead trimming comes <
out of her chair with a pop like a drawn cork.
Rain played up with the opening of the well-
advertised cricket-match, N.S.W. v. Victoria, at
Sydney Association ground last Saturday, so far
as the crowd looking on went; but I couldn’t help
thinking it was good business for the cricketers
slopping round in the mud, as some of them expect
to go ’Ome, where that sort of remorseless drizzle
is an everyday occurrence. Therefore, a good wet
“pitch” (I think that isthe proper word) and a
slipper-slopper “crease” appear to me to lie the
very things to enable the choosing committee to
jnck their men with neatness and dispatch. But
rain spoils the game from a ladies’-pavilion stand-
point. Wet umbrellas are certainly a, novel and
uncommon sight these times, but they turn a
gathering of intelligent human beings into a mass
meeting of mushrooms.
A Sydney magazine has a long illustrated article
in a recent number on Sydney Hospital, from
which it would appear that the large, highly-
omamental building is a sort of Paradise bound.
But one great defect escapes mention, which is
th it all applicants, men and women, black, white,
yellow, or brown, are yarded up together on soni'
forms under the southern wing, and in the middle
stands a desk presided over by the officer who
issues orders to attend the doctor.
So as to determine what medico the patient
should attend, he or sht must rise up and describe
all symptoms in a loud ai d distinct voice, and the
feelings of a respectable man who has to explain
the leading features of his case lie fore a lumber of
strange women.
“ The days of Sairey Gamp are gone, says the
same magazine. Yet, the other day, in ail up-
country town the patient was prescribed poached
eggs. “ Mary,” said the nurse, “have you any-
tli.ng to poach eggs in?” After diligent search
nurse’s eagle eye rested on a spittoon on the
mantelpiece which was kept spick -and-span for
parlor use. “ All,’ she said, “ this must be che
thing.”
But nurses have a hard row to hoe, and their
life is by no means skittleish. lam told of a
blue-eyed sister Dora who thought that pro
fessional nursing mainly consisted of holding
on to intellectual foreheads varied by spooning
jelly into good-looking he-patients, and who came
as a probationer into a Northern hospital not long
ago. Her first job was to hold the candle while
Sawbones hacked the leg off an “accident” who
had just b'-en brought in, and when the butcher-
ing was over the surgeon calmly tossed the gory
limb into her arms for her to carry away some-
where. She did stagger with the gruesome burden
as far as the door, but fainted just outside, still
clasping the leg to her heart.
The fun ot Sydney Turf Club races is that
amateurs ride in some of the flat flutters, and, in
ease they should be mistaken for professionals,
they are always designated “ Mister ” So-and-So,
while the genuine jockey is billed as plain Tom,
Dick, or Harry. Tnat, however, is not the only
difference between them, though, of course, tlie
amateurs think it is. 1 can always tell a gentle-
man rider by the voluminousness of his riding-
pants ; you may also spot him with certainty by

Two society brides adorned the members’ stand

This sudden absence of collar isthe inevitable re-

celerity than even her elbows do. Another matron,

not say 1 liked the effect of two broad white

roses sprinkled all over had evidently gone out of
mourning up to her bonnet, which was demurely
black. Evidently it is the correct caper to shed
the weeds of woe feet first.
Apropos of mourning, I was surprised to see
