“ Patriotism is well enough, but one gets tired
of the ever-recurring Australian prodigy who
means to ‘ knock ’em iii London or know the
reason why ! ’ The first step towards fame, in
these cases, is paved with benefit-tickets, and
then the aspirant departs ’mid a flourish of
trumpets, to disappear, more often than not, in
the “ Big Smoke, ” never to be heard of afterwards
except through the medium of private letters,
extracts from which are published in local society
columns for the edification of “sympathisers.” He
who casts his bread upon the waters in the shape
of subscriptions, is compensated after many ('are
by the perusal of tit-bits, such as these, ‘That
gifted young Australienne, Miss .lew’s Harp
Jones, playfully termed ‘the Croajingolong
Canary,’ out of compliment to her birthplace,
intends,’etc., etc., or ‘the friends of the Bung-
bung baritone may be congratulated on the fact
that having conquered his chronic catarrh, he is
resolved ’ —and so on.
“ Fortunately for themselves, ‘the Old Folks at
’Ome ’ take most things pretty calmly, even the
advent of the‘typical New World Genius,’with
Ins cheek kissed by the South wind, eyes far-
gazing, as becomes a iKiundless bush-seer —and all
the rest of it. For one thing, it’s been going on
so long. Ages ago, when ■ Gats Gregory, ’ one of
the famous exploring family and afterwards the
first Bananaland Surveyor-General was tendered a
complimentary banquet on the eve of his depar-
ture for Europe, he told his hosts at Berth
(W.A.) that ‘some people sought greatness,
others had it thrust upon them,’ but for his part
he was determined to ‘ slip quietly info London if
he had to make a midnight job of it.’
“ * Ah, well,’ as old We.cralians always, i.
mr,er[)olate at this point, the Gregorys were-.a
plucky lot, though so rampagious as youngsters
that their widowed mother often sent a messenger
to a neighbour’s house with ‘ her compliments and
she would esteem it a favor if Mr. So and-So
would come over when dist ngaged and punish
two, three, or four of the boys, as the case might
be. Barents who seldom mustered tip e fcrgy
to discip ine their own olive-branches d.d ft it
welcome these invitations ; but in the sister pro-
vince, long, long ago, a gentleman could not refuse
to oblige a lady, so reciprocal compliments were
always returned to the effect that ‘Mr. X. would
be delighted,’ Ac., &c.

The Freetrade
Policy to which N.S.W. has recently reverted has
proved more than a nine days’ topic in the com-
mercial circles of Melbourne, and many specula-
tions are made as to how the new order will affect
intercolonial trade in the immediate future. While
there is a consensus of opinion that the non-
restrictive policy will to some extent benefit
Sydney at the expense of Melbourne, there are
isolated facts which point in quite the contrary
direction. For example : Messrs. Manning and
'Company, the popular ladies’ outfitters, of Coll ins-
street, Melb., have always had considerable busi-
ness transactions with the mother colony, but,
since the New Year, their orders from N.S.W. for
Costumes, Cloaks, and Millinery have been far
larger than usual. A drawback refund of duty is
obtained from Vic. Customs House, and credited
.to the buyer by Manning and Co., so that buyers
across the borders get their purchases duty free.
Thus, if it were not for the trouble occasioned by
the narrower policy of the southern province,
Melb. merchants should find the Freetrade pro-
gramme of N.S.W. very much to their advantage.
Howbeit, Messrs. Manning and Co. are sanguine
of now largely extending their business with the
ladies of N.S.W., who think, rightly or wrongly,
that Melb. modes are always in advance of those
of Sydney. Ladies !—Write to Manning and Co.,
on the Block, Collins-street, Melb. •

Looking far back at the Royal pantomime,
one cant help shuddering, even at this safe dis-

as a general thing, deliciously idealises the work-a-

Nothing more convincing could possibly be
imagined than their cheerful methods of romping,
kicking up their heels, yawning, biting their nails
- m fact, doing everything which they should not
have done and vice versa, m true child fashion.
“ Mist Florence Wilton, who, unlike most of her
colleagues, looked as if her clothes were really and
truly her veiy own, made ‘Jack the Giant-
killer ’ just as bright as a button, and worked like
a polo pony. Every time she bounded onto the
boards like an indiarubrier ball and kept the ball
rolling all the time she was there. Facially con-
sidered, a longish and sharpisri nose would seem
to be the thorn in Miss Wilton’s otherwise charm-
ing ‘ flesh. ’
“It would seem that Melb.’s stock ot female
loveliness is at a low ebb. One beautiful face fills
all the photographers’ show-cases, and on the Block
alone there are more than 20 pictures of the same
belle. It seems hardly fair to the lovely sitter
that her face should be made stale in this way.
Even the small street boys have got to know her
name now, and criticise each new photo, with
much discernment.

A new co. started on Saturday to fool with
Providence at Sydney Criterion—where Provi-
dence has gone back on many people of late—by
staging “ Hide and Seek, ” a comedy which is past
its first youth, and which consists largely of a
Scotch person trying to throw one Jones over a
balcony. The play consists mostly of Jones.
There are six persons, all more or less Jones, con-
cealed in three separate rooms in a hotel, and the
Scotch person, armed with a large, shapeless griev-
ance and a threepenny piece, hovers in the vesti-
bule and storms them as they come out.
Consequently, after a while, the Joneses don’t
come out any oftener than they can help,
and when they do emerge they run for
their lives. A pleasant feature of the case
is that two or three people call in at vari-
ous times and assault the Scotchman over
various imaginary grievances, or propose to him,
or accuse him of wife-desertion, or leave a black
infant with him. “ Hide and Seek ”is a violent
production where people skull-drag each other
round the premises on the slightest provocation,
and the whole affair would be over in five minutes
if the Highlander, instead of waiting in the vesti-
bule during three acts for his wife to come out,
would go into her room and fetch her. He is a
futile Caledonian, however, and, instead of adopt-
ing this simple expedient, he remains outside and
makes furious attacks on Jones. When he can’t
find anyone called Jones, he is willing to attack
any other jierson instead. Even when he is run
in ne simply gets bail, and comes back to sit in
the old place, and renew his efforts to cripple
Jones. Finally, 12 basketsful of broken Jones
are gathered up and removed, and the only other
very definite result is that the Scot nearly
Strangles the waiter in his efforts to make him
give back 3d.
*,* * *
The male element in the co. is decidedly
strong, and the female one lamentably weak.
Wilfred Shine is even a better Scotchman than he
is an Irishman, which is saying a good deal.
Ralph Roberts’s waiter is reminiscent in some
respects of Boucicault. Laurence Dunbar is a fine
crushed tragedian, and fills the part which Newton
Griffiths occupied when “ Hide and Seek ” was
played at the Opera House long ago—the only
admirable thing which the awful Griffiths ever
did in the course of The Bulletin's acquaintance
with him ; and Frank Hawthorne, as a valuable
idiot, has invented a walk with a peculiar stutter
in it, which is strikingly expressive of insanity in
the feet and ought to be worth a lot of money.
J. wo or three other people also shape well. As for
the ladies, the Pest that can be said for them is
that two of them are a good deal worse than a
third one, from which rash and ill-judging people
may rush to the conclusion that the third one isn’t
SO as Die other two. Also, a fourth one
—Miss May Burney—is a lot better than the third
one, which means that she is moderately good and
that the third lady aforesaid is very bad. The
Bulletin prudently omits~the name of this third
lady lest the first and second ladies should call
round.

Dan. Barry’s dramatic company, strength-
ened by a brass band, is still upholding Hingland’s
honor at Johannesburg, and the Russians continue
to perish ingloriously behind the scenes of Melb.
Alexandra. “The Roll of the Drum ”is a story
of some unknown period when the Czar bearded
the British Lion in his South African den. This
must have been pretty long ago, seeing that the
hero, wMo considers himself a freeborn Londoner,
gets mistaken for a Russian serf. Whereupon he
follows the Queen’s colors to the Transvaal, and
proves to be his colonel’s long-lost son, in conse-
quence of which he is allowed to rescue the Colors
from destruction, instead of being hanged as a
foreign spy. The curse of serfdom is then shifted
to a pale young lady in a black costume, at the in-
stance of a hairy Muscovite, her alleged father.
Luckily this interloper is only her uncle, so his
attempt to cast her into slavery is frustrated. Also,
the haughty colonel, and the hero who died of 10
mortal wounds in the second act, come back to

Johannesburg in the nick of time, pursued by the
victorious brass band, and at the same moment
the comic Irish sergeant shoots Augustus Glover
from the rear. Consequently the drama comes to
a happy end, the last invisible charge of the
British Grenadiers having earned the Czar by
storm and left President Kruger dead upon the
ramparts, or something to the same effect. The
fall of Glover is received with much rejoicing in
the Alexandra gallery, although he performs his
insane misdeeds quite admirably and supplies
the warlike drama with all its plot. Some
of the others, notably Miss Dora Mostyn, Dan
Barry, J. P. O’Neill, and Wilson Forbes deserve
a good word for their acting, but they don’t go
prowling after “ papers,” or keep innocent people
out of their property, as the stalwart Augustus
does. Besides, it is he who murders Dora Mostyn,
and so gives “ The Roll of the Drum ” a chance to
be pathetic. The spectacle of four supers walking
away, an inch at a time, with the corpse of that
chirpy little drummer-boy is a feast of sadness,
and when manager Barry gently lays Britain’s
flag upon the cold remains, one feels almost as
solemn as an amen in church.

W. S. Gilbert has come triumphantly
through a libel-action brought against him by a
gushing lady journalist known as the Comtesse
de Bremont (nee Dunphy). The “Comtesse”
wrote to the humorist, asking for an interview,
and when he replied that his terms for submitting
himself to the ordeal were 20 guineas, the lady
wrote back saying that she anticipated the plea-
sure of doing his obituary notice for nothing.
Bilious W.S.G. complained of the pen-woman’s
“cheek” in a letter to the London dailies, and
also dropped a note to her editor (Sr. Paul’s
magazine), suggesting that her future comments
on him (Gilbert) or This work would need to be
received with caution. These letters were the
grounds for action, and the jury found them
not good enough. But the lady will probably
make money by the deal. It came out in evidence
that she is the authoress of a spicy volume of
poems, some of which made the learned Judge
blow his nose embarrassedly when read aloud in
court. One poem in particular seems likely to
create a demand for the book—a sonnet entitled
“Love’s Consummation.” The comtesse dedicated
this warm, clinging little bit of female literature
to her husband, who, by the way, had been dead
seven years when the book was published. The
idea of dedicating such a honeymoon reminiscence
to the defunct partner seems slightly gruesome.

legislators ; he supervised the drafting of the
l nolle Service Act. Able, just, and knowing
full well the abuses of the N.S.W. Govtr. depart-
ments, he can do the State inestimable service.
He comes of a talented family; his sister, Miss
lza Coghlan, was one of the first two lady doctors
of Sydney University, and his brother, Dr. C. A.
Coghlan, is a leading Sydney barrister and Uni-
versity law-lecturer.

Mr. G. A. Wilson, who for the first year
"ill ho chairman of the board, is a big, brawny,
wary, unflinching, 50-year-old Scotchman, liked
and trusted by those with whom he comes into
contact. As a youth he did a four-years’ course
at Aberdeen University; lie did not graduate.
He travelled much in the States and Europe and
joined the B.N.Z. in ’74, became Melbourne
accountant in *B3, Melbourne manager in ’BB, and
Sydney manager ’9l. His bank salary was £l3OO
j*er annum; his accrued rights in the bank ] ension*
fund were £3OO per annum. He very level-
headedly forfeits these for £lOOO per year
under the State. At first he declined the
chairmanship, but after two interviews with
Premier Reid he accepted. Being a Strahget
to civil servants he should be ari impartial
man in reform. He says, “No relative of mine
is in the Govt, employ and none shall be.” His
business experience as a banker and the know-
ledge of human nature he has acquired-thereby
Will no doubt help his judgment m his present
duties. He has had many experiences of the bad
side of human nature, but “a banker tells
nothing.” Concerning Australians afiid' Britishers
he says : “ It is not fair to compare them, for we
get so few of the better Britishers out here.” Prom
his own personal experience he prefers Australian
youths as bankers or servants'to any'other ; “ the
prominent features of the Australian character
are self-reliance and power of initiative.— ,
* * * *
Mr. Joseph Barling, the third Commis-
sioner, is an Englishman, 56 years old, with 36
years of N.S.W. Govt, service. He first served
in the Audit office; next in the Harbors and
Rivers Dept. ; then in the Railway ; then in the
Harbors and Rivers again; and for Six or seven
years past lie has been U.-S. for Works. Mr] Bar-
ling, having been through the length and brie; dth
of the service, is one of the most familiar iof its
characters; his most prominent characteristics are
his clear-headedness, his enthusiastic love of work,
and his constant affectation of diplomacy. No one
knows better than Mr. Barling thatgreat reductions
must come upon the demoralised N.S.W. civil
service ; nor is anyone more apprehensive of the
fact that retrenchment in many cases means for
the confirmed civil servant—for the mature auto-
maton —penury and death.
The death last week of Mr. J. J.
Hinchy, of whom a portrait is printed in this
issue, caused a well-known figure to be miss-
ing from the members’ pavilion at the inter-
provincial cricket-match. Mr. Hinchy was for-
merly an enthusiastic and active member of the
Warwick cricket club, and, to the last, no big
cricket-match took place without his presence as
an onlooker. “Jimmy,” as he was best-known to
his many friends, was a musician of no mean order,
and for years was tenor soloist at St. Mary’s
Cathedral.
About once in every three months Melb.
Argus or Age discovers a plot to overthrow the
existing Ministry. The conspiracy, when found
by the Age, is mean and contemptible, whereas it
is said to be a matter for public joy when the other
organ brings it to light. In either case, the revela-
tion is followed by a paragraph announcing that
the plot collapsed for some reason or other, as
soon as the newspaper sjioke. Also, in either
case, the one newspaper congratulates the public
upon the disappearance of an inconvenient crisis,
whilst the rival organ indulges in a burst of scorn.
This periodica] occurrence grows very monotonous.
H. H. Champion intends to be his own
lawyer if attorney O’Halloran insists upon going
to court with his case, and will not have a fool for
his client. He distinguished himself by the
defence he made in 1886, when charged with
Burns, Hyndman and Co., for sedition. The bar
against him included Charles Russel], Poland, and
Charles Matthews. In discharging the prisoners,
the judge remarked that “the defendant Champion
deserved great credit for what he had done.”
Matthews was much struck with his ability, and
declared that “ he would make his fortune at the
bar. ”

SOCIETY
It is announced that the statue of Gov.
Phillip, ordered long ago in the good old loan-
boom times, will be erected on the site of the
burnt-down Garden Palace in Sydney Botanic
Gardens. Twelve thousand lovely sovereigns are
being spent on what will probably prove at best
only a middling sort of statue of Botany Bay’s
first boss gaoler—a commonplace and uncouth
semi-German naval captain, of whom the world
knows little. However, the deed is done, and
the bleeding country is saddled by that absurd
old spendthrift Parkes with a charge of, say, £1 a
day for ever or thereabouts, in order that the
world may be reminded that Sydney is a dropsical
pumpkin growing on an English dungheap.
* * * •
The alleged Tasmanian outrage makes a
curious story. The daughter of a Launceston
grocer was travelling by train from Hobart to
Launceston, in a compartment occupied by one
other passenger—a male. The lady alleges that he
offered her a rug, and accused her of bashfulness
because she refused it, adding that she would be
better pleased to have a young man with her than
an old married fellow like him. She said she didn’t
allow young men to travel with her at night, and
went to sleep. She woke suddenly, felt dizzy,
and smelt chloroform —at least, she thought it was
chloroform, though she never took chloroform
before. Then she said, “Oh, the wretch!”
and jumped out of the window. He re-
marked, “Oh, my God!” This was in
Tasmania, as before-mentioned, and the train
was a Tasmanian mail-train, so the lady was not
much hurt, and was able to tell her story fluently
when the guard, roused by the yells of the passen-
gers, came back and picked her up. Her alleged
assailant declared that he was dozing, and knew
nothing about the matter until he saw her feet

* * * *
A tragic disappearance is reported from
Melbourne. A fortnight ago two small boys
started for the baths, at Middle Park. They
never got there, and they never came back, and
for a good many days the most energetic search
cast no semblance of light on the mystery. Then,
one day, a bank of sand on a waste allotment
caved in slightly, and left a human foot sticking
out, and a little digging revealed the pair. They
had evidently taken shelter from the rain beneath
an overhanging mass of sand, and it had slid down
on top of them and smothered them both. An
almost identical tragedy took place many years
ago in the neighborhood of Moore Park, Sydney.
A young man took his best girl for a walk there
by moonlight, and they sat down under the lee of
a huge sandbank to hug each other, or talk about
how they would have the house furnished when
they were married, and what kind of glaring oil-
cloth they would have in the passage, or some-
thing, and just then the sand fell on them and
buried them. They were found a year or two
afterwards, one skeleton in the arms of the other
skeleton, and how they decided to have that house
furnished when they got married no one ever
knew not in this world, anyhow. Perhaps, where
their ghosts went to, they found a better oilcloth
than the one they reckoned on, and an angel laid
it down for them without any trouble.
* * * *
Judge Holroyd declares that a married
woman who has contracted debts she does riot
mean to pay cannot be sent to gaol on a fraud-
summons. Another grievance. Why is it that
the lowest larrikin in the land, spitting and
blaspheming on a corner, may be sent to gaol for
refusing to pay his just debts, whilst the privilege
is denied to woman ?
* * * *
Speaking of Albert Calvert (and valet) even
tradesmen may be over-advertised. A fashion-
able ladies’ tailor once received an order from a
Governor’s wife. The fact got into the daily
papers. The “society ” journals went one better,
and put the cheque down at £127, while, in fact)
the real amount was seven guineas. Landlord,
reading of larger amount, comes down for rent, and,
despite his protestations, tailor is obliged to go
insolvent. On going through his books the trustees
concluded that he had pawned goods bought on
credit—an offence against the insolvency law. He
was finally acquitted by the jury, but hot until he
had done some months of agony, suspense, adjourn-
ment and bail.

Summary of Deceased Wife’s Sisters’ Act
—a sister-in-law is not a sister-in-law.
Probably the most
God-forsaken corner of
the earth is the town-
ship of Boulia, Queens-
land back-blocks. The
local temperature ranges
from below freezing-
point in winter to
125 deg. in the shade in
summer. It is (says a
notorious Western liar)
possible to perspire freely
at Boulia, during Xmas-
time, in the ice - chest.
Civil-servants, exiled
thither, have been known
to bum down the pre-
mises and flee. Some
months ago, a China-
man, with true Celestial
grit, started a garden
there, and, after incredi-
ble exertions, succeeded
in raising a curious,
blighted, blasted-looking
vegetable reputed to be
a cabbage, together with
a wild, uncouth, withered
article of the melon
variety. The favorite
occupation of the
Boulians was to come
round and bet which
was which, and chaff
John on his productions.
The other day, John
was found dead—hang-
ing by the neck to a
beam, within view of
his two aborted green
stuff. He had probably
become confused himself
about the vegetables,
and, recognising the
absurdity of the situa-
tion, had poured forth
his soul in one wild
execration, and expired.
For him Hell has no
terrors.

The Westralian telegraphic service is slow
and not sure. When the Orotava arrived at
Albany, on Saturday week, the usual wire was
sent to Adelaide with the usual announcement.
The Orotava reached Adelaide three days later"
about four hours after the telegram, but, as she
did not leave W.A. till some time subsequent to
the sending of the wire, she might easily have
reached Adelaide in time to break the news of the
approaching ’gram. The remark of the Orotava’s
captain, when they told him of the incident, was :
“ Hope you don t blame my boat—that’s the wire
we sent last trip.”
* * * *
A leading Adelaide society dame, who has
been learning to ride a bicycle, ventured into a
leading street the other afternoon with disastrous
results. Madame got on a down grade, and in
endeavoring to avoid a tram-car collided with an
ice-cream .cart, and had to save herself by grab-
bing the ice-cream man, a swarthy Creek, round
the neck.
* * * *
In the mutton-bird season hundreds of
the eggs are disposed of to Vic. housewives as
respectable farm-reared duck eggs, which thev
much resemble. But an individual who deserves
a pat on the back from the author of ‘ ‘ Self-Help ”
went one better than that. He exhibited a set-
ting of eggs at a country show, where they took
first-prize, and afterwards sold them at a
fancy rate. The matronly biddy who had charge
of them was used to imposition, having mothered
wandering turkeys and amphibious ducks before,
but this was too much. Little mutton-birds !
Phew !

PUNCTURED ATTIRE.
She sat upon a log, and oh !
Her fair, sweet face was full of woe—
Her bike lay there beside her.
I went—the soul of sympathy—
To offer aid, at least to see
What ailed so fair a rider.
“ A punctured tire, Miss Blank?” I said.
She shook her fluffy little head,
But said naught at that juncture.
And then her blush the fact confessed—
’Twas her attire, I shrewdly guessed,
Had somehow suffered puncture.
Melb.
SNELL.


Judging by Appearances
Is always a risky and uncertain method of arriv-
ing at conclusions. It is notably about the worst
possible plan to adopt in the formation of opinions
regarding our fellow man, but it is to the last
degree deceptive in the matter of Photography.
Exterior polish or, professionally speaking,
enamel —does for the photograph what a new suit
of clothes does for the man ; it distorts the judg-
ment and conceals defects. The Platinotype is an
honest, durable picture, and, possessing a lasting
brilliancy of its own, requires no artificial gloss. A
study of the work done and exhibited at

Studios, 492 George Street, SYDNEY, and 114
Elizabeth Street, MELBOURNE, is conscien-
tiously recommended to all intending sitters.

• * * *
A Tasmanian lady-vocalist recently fiabber-
fasted the crowded audience of a very fashionable
lobart concert by appearing in gorgeous evening-
dress, pearls, and a pair of big muddy jack-boots !
Of course the lady was wildly encored, for the
audience naturally imagined there was a lot more
to follow. The explanation proved to be that
she had borrowed “hubby’s” big clod-hoppers
to walk to the Town-hall in—the night was very
wet—and had forgotten to change them !
* * * *
At Collingwood (Vic.) Police-court, the
other day, they suddenly found that the accused
person in the dock had a 21b. piece of road-
metal in each hand and was just taking aim
with one of these formidable “ gibbers ” at
the witness then giving evidence. The two
rocks were promptly taken from him,
whereupon he broke down and screamed
for his mother. Evidently they conduct
things in rather a loose fashion at
Collingwood when an accused is
allowed to take road-metal into
the dock with him, and the pity
is that he didn’t let fly with it at
the unpaid magistracy on the
bench. The Melbourne
magistracy badly wants
something or almost any-
thing to happen to it, and
a large “ gibber ” on the
head would do about as
well as a lot of other
things.

The moral of Jumbo, the late rogue-
elephant of Sydney Zoo., who departed his life
the other day full of years, rotten fruit, and
slavery. Eight years ago he tried to tusk and
trample a committeeman, his chief surface-fault
being that he failed. Had he been a mole-
skinned man, the Botany Bay Government
would greedily have got Nosey Bob to break
his neck ; but being a valuable asset —being pro-
putty—of the said 8.8. Govt., he remained an
honored exhibit. “His heart weighed 271 b.”
Fancy a thing with a heart like that enduring the
scorn of every grinning lout, being the butt of
every practical joker, and the especial slave
of ugly, undersized, underminded manhood ?
Wonder he didn’t jelly a crowd to death at nostal-
gic times ! Wonder, too, what Mumbo has got
the soul of Jumbo? Also, if “ the eye is the index
of the mind,’’ what sized mind has the ordinary
pig’s-eyed elephant got ?
A piece of picturesque bad luck 1 Out at
Cobar, on a recent sultry night, four young men
went to a tank to swim. After the swim they
commenced skylarking and got separated. Two
of them were seized with the idea that the smooth
grass was a good place for a race, and started to
sprint in the darkness. About the same moment
the other two, some distance off, had the same
idea and started to do likewise. In each case it
was a neek-and-neck race. There were, roughly
speaking, 400 square miles of empty land to sprint
in, yet they contrived to keep such an accurate
bee-line that the first information they got of each
other’s doings came in the shape of a fourfold
collision, and all four went down insensible on the
grass. After a while one of them sat up and took
stock of the disaster. It panned out one man with
a crippled arm and a bad shock; one with a
damaged leg, six cuts, sundry bruises, and a
shock; one man with slight concussion of the
brain ; and one with his forehead smashed in ap-
parently beyond repair. Moral: never run in the
dark, unless you have a whole continent to your-
self to do it in.

to the next man (a drummer), he said in a loud
voice, “ Will you try some mustard, mate ? ”

The troubles of a married sea-steward
lately pointed a moral in a suburban court. His
ma-in-law kept his wife-in-law secluded every
time he had leave ashore. Whereupon he shut
off the supplies. “ Check.” Wife interposes an
application for a maintenance-order for herself
and child. “ Counter-check.” Hubby his tale
unfolds, and the beak—a married man himself—
advises the pair to go and live together at more
than “a kmght’s-move ” from the ma. “ Double-
check.” Ma takes the infant son and heir!
“ Mate.” So soon has the New Woman produced
the New Mother-in-law.

and distinguished-looking woman, possessed of
most courteous and courtly manners, and far
ahead in style of any other Australian Governor’s
wife, completely “ runs ” the Tasmanian Governor-
ship, His Excellency doing simply what he is pre-
eminently fitted to do—nothing.
Dear Bulletin, —The experiment of graft-
ing the epidermis on humans has proved emin-
ently successful, and herein, I think, lies the only
solution of the ever-aggravating unemployed
problem. Upon the bodies of the workless thou-
sands let there be grafted sheepskins of the best
quality, and thereby they would be furnished for
life with reliable clothing and turned at the same
time into a source of revenue. They could be
fixed up either as merinoes or crossbreds, but I
think the merino unemployed would be most
profitable, as there is no present idea of eating the
animal when he grows old. A good merino who
didn’t drink and consequently lose the value
of his wool in police-court fines or get his
fleece damaged in free fights should be a profitable
spec, for forty years. The unemployed could
either form co-operative settlements and shear
each other, getting an advance on themselves
from the bank to supply the necessary capital to
start with, or they could let themselves out to
squatters. The advantages which they would
have over the ordinary sheep are obvious They
would require no dogs to round them up, and a
great saving of dog would thus be effected.
They would shear each other, which the sheep
(on do ‘ between shearing times they could
? ro P s f° r their own support, and pursue
mi g atlon works, while the sheep only stands
roun /• ’ a r so ’ . e.Y could look after cattle and thus
I ?, e wo u'dustries, whereas the sheep doesn t
oo alter anything. I erhaps the best idea, how-
ever, would be to graft wool on everybody ; when
C ° U ' ! >e a s , iee P and , wo , r c . lll a boot-factory
a t the same time he would obviously be a most
profitable spec. The wool off prisoners, girls in
reformatories, members of Parliament, and civil
servants could be sold by the State, and the pro-
ceeds put into a special fund for the e- ’ t! re-
duction of the public debt. —Yours, &e., Merino.

The Wild-Cat Column .
Tho Australian Widows’ Fund presents a report
(or the year ended 81st October, 1895, which has some
good features about it, and some other features. The
income from renewal premiums is £139,208, which is
£6OOO legg than last year and £13,000 less than the year
before. The income from new premiums is £12,030, which
is £2OOO more than last year. The total income for the
year is £206,800, which is £BOOO less than last year, and
£14,000 less than in ’93. Most life-assurance societies,
however, are suffering a very similar falling-off just now.
The funds have been increased by £60,719, whereas last
year the amount saved was £84,202, and in ’93 it was
£100,783, so in this matter things are not improving. The
worst falling - off, however, is in the rate of interest
realised on investments. In ’92 it was over 5| per cent.
It dropped to 5) in ’93, and to about 5 per cent, in ’94,
and this year it has gone down with a shock to a little
over 4J. The assurance-funds now reach the handsome
total of £1,251,266, but in ’93, when the funds were
£245,000 less, the income realised from them was £2600
more. These figures show that a lot of the glory and
dazzle have gone out of the life-assurance business of
recent years. The utterly objectionable feature, how-
ever, in the Widows' Fund accounts, is the expenses,
which are _ increasing at a truly dreadful rate just
as business is diminishing and interest growing smaller.
In ’93 the expenses swallowed up nearly 22 per cent, of
the premiums. In 1894 they amounted to over 23) per
cent., and in ’95 they are within a very small fraction of
27 per cent. The common explanation given in such
cases is that the increase is owing to the expansion of
new business, as new business is necessarily much more
expensive than renewals. In this case, as the renewal
business is less than last year, the increased expense must
be set down to the cost of new business, and on that
basis the Widows’ Fund has earned £2OOO more in new
premiums and spent over £4OOO more in expenses to do it.
Every separate item of expense shows an increase, and
the society badly wants to sit down in a secluded spot and
ask itself seriously how long this process can go on.
Intense competition has doubtless a lot to do with the
matter, but some societies, at all events, have contrived
to reduce expenses, despite the hardness of the times.
• * * *
One good feature of the Widows’ Fund balance-
sheet is that the death-claims are smaller than last year,
which shows that the society has a healthy anu well-
selected crowd of customers. Another is that the funds
seem, on the whole, to be well-invested so far as any
judgment can be formed from the information avail-
able, There are no shady-looking items on the list of
assets. The amount set down for “ properties acquired
by foreclosure ” is only £24,415, which is very moderate,
and the interest in arrear is returned at the low figure of
£2149. Interest in arrear, when it gets to a large
amount, generally means that a lot of the owners of pro-
perties mortgaged would rather the society foreclosed
than not, the properties being not worth redeeming at
the price, and when this item is low it usually signifies
that the investments are in a good condition.
• * * •
The second mass of awful revelations re the
dead City of Melbourne Bank has now arrived. It is
mainly to the effect that from 1891 onwards the weak
little institution was a living lie. The bank never faced
its losses ; it bolstered up insolvent customers to prevent
them filing their schedules and compelling their bad
debts to be formally acknowledged ; and, as it never
admitted that its advances were bad, it debited them with
interest and put down the interest as profit. In one half-
year of ’93, £32,286 of the alleged earnings were bogus,
being made up by charging interest which could never be
recovered on money that was hopelessly lost. Big
advances, muddle, neglect, and a lot of Worse things
ruined the City of Melb. Bank. It was a small concern
at the best, and it lent £1,563,000 on 10 accounts—an
average of £156,000 each. Directors lent themselves
£76,000, and they lent a late manager £75,000 ; and they
also lent to six companies in which they were interested,
£542,000. The committee of alleged inspection never
discovered these awful doings, or at least it said nothing
about them, and the auditors passed them each half-year
in dead silence. It is added that some even worse dis-
coveries have been made which are withheld for the
•present.
Tho moral of this is that the whole system of
audit is a ridiculous farce. In almost every bank and
insurance office in Australasia the auditors only profess
to guarantee that the figures of the balance-sheet are
correct in a strictly technical sense. The sheet says that
the securities and advances are so much, and the auditors
add them up and report that they are so much. In
hardly one instance do they profess to say that the
securities are good, and that the advances are recoverable
—they merely announce truthfully that they have added
up the pile of rubbish or otherwise, and that the total is
as alleged. They are not asked to do anything more.
Their appointment lies to a great extent with the
directors whom they are supposed to supervise; the
board’s own shares, and its friends’ Shares, and its proxies
and its friends’ proxies, and its influence generally, being
sufficient, in most cases, to decide who shall be appointed.
If there is anything to conceal the board secures the
appointment of men who can be relied upon to say nothing
about it; and as the average auditor’s bread and cheese
depend to a great extent on his saying nothing about it,
this kind of auditor can be found almost anywhere.
There is hardly an auditor’s certificate attached to the
balance-sheet of any important Australasian concern
which even professes to be worth the paper it is written
upon. ■£'"
• • * *
The balance-sheet of the Commercial Bank of
Australia is a pathetic evidence of hard times, and is the
worst dooument that concern has issued since its recon-
struction. The net profit for the half-year is exactly
£lOBO, or less than Is. 2d. per cent, per ann um on the paid-
up capital. Last half-year the profit was £5048 ; for the
six months ended Dec., '94, it was £13,353; the half ended
June, ’94, showed a profit of £7902, and at Deo., ’93, the
figure was £29,754. The £lOBO announced this time is as
nearly as possible the irreducible minimum. During the
past half-year the cash on hand has increased by over
£200,000, but still it only amounts to £1,174,338, which is
very moderate indeed for a concern of such magnitude,
and is nearly £200,000 less than it had two years ago, so that
the Commercial has practically made no progress towards
accumulating money to meet its reconstructed deposits.
During these two years its paid-up capital has increased by
£600,000, but apparently this money has all gone out
again in some shape or another. Another liquid asset,
“municipal and other debentures,” has decreased in the
two years by £61,000. Since the bank first began to
show its reconstructed deposits as a separate item, 18
months ago, it has reduced them by a little over £400,000,
and it has still £5,773,000 to repay. The reduction saves
it £lB,OOO a year in interest, but this big saving hasn’t
served in the least to avert the downward movement in
the profits. Also, a considerable amount of progress is
said to have been made at realising the old assets,
£1,600,000 having been wiped off the assets-realisation
account in two years, but any saving that has been made
in this way seems also to have gone into the abyss of
general depreciation. Even of the miserable £lOBO which
represents the half-year’s profit, all except £7 has been
made by further grinding down the expenses, and a good
part of every other half-year’s returns since ’93 has been
made the same way. The gross profits are barely £50,000,
whereas two years ago they were over £90,000, and the
falling-off has been continuous.
If the next half-year shows the same continuous
decline as the two last, the obvious fact is that the Com-
mercial Bank of Australia can’t pay the 4) per cent,
interest on its reconstructed deposits any longer
out of current profits. The big institution, by the
way, converted a large portion of its deposits into
preference shares, which now receive no dividend, so it
is really only called upon to pay about 3) per cent, on the
total of its old deposits, but even this it can’t possibly do
out of profits—should the process of depreciation go on
for another six months at the present rate. . Its cash
balance of less than £1,200,000 has to be set against
£1,700,000 of new liabilities, and for a concern of such
magnitude it doesn’t leave nmch margin for paying off
any part of the reconstructed funds, in order to reduce
the interest thereon. And when it is remembered that
the Commercial, which has just earned £lOBO for the
half-year, needs to earn £93,880 before it can pay a 5 per
cent, dividend, the shareholders’ prospects look too
dreadful for language.
A small side-issue in the Commercial Bank’s
accounts is that the process of liquidating the great mass
of assets taken over from the old Commercial Bank is
getting slower and slower every half-year. This may
mean a lot of things, but it certainly looks as if the insti-
tution had got through all the best and most realisable
assets with a rush, and the remaining £6,000,000 worth
were a tough lot which were being found hard to deal
with.
* * * *
The A.J.S. Bank has done a little better this
half-year than last. The profit (£25,825) shows an im-
provement of £9OO, which is something, though not very
much. A 4 per cent, dividend takes £23,353 of this, and
after putting £2OOO to reserve (making it £50,000)) there
remains £472 over. The £50,000 reserve has all been ac-
cumulated since 1893. During the past half-year the note
and bill circulation and current accounts have all in-
creased. The coin, bullion, British consols, Government
debentures, and other liquid securities are £200,000 more
than they were six months ago, and amount to £1,863,000,
and the reconstructed deposits have also been reduced in
the past half-year byabout £400,000-the A.J.S. has lately
fallen into the bad habit of muddling together the 1907
fixed deposits and the new deposits, so that the amount of
the reduction can’t be actually arrived at. But the result
seems to show that the A.J.S. is making rather better pro-
gress, and has an improved outlook for the future, if the
land-tax doesn’t come down with too big a shock on its
great mass of landed securities.
* .’’ * * *
The Queensland National Bank’s report is
chiefly remarkable, as usual, for the things which are not
in it. The profits for the half-year are set down at
£36,340, and, after paying the usual 3 percent, div. there
is £593 to the good. This has been arrived at mainly by
vigorous paring-down of expenses. There still appears to
Vie no attempt to build up a reserve-fund. The coin on
hand has diminished by £476,000 in six months and now
stands at £921,318. No remark is yet made about "any
attempt to release the deposits, and, as the balance-sheet
doesn't show how much the old fixed deposits amount to,
it is impossible to discover anything about them,
or to say whether the Q.N.B. gets any new de-
posits these times as a sign of public confidence,
or whether they are all Govt, money, and the recon-
structed cash of the public which remains in because the
public can't get it out. There is also no news as to how
those assets which were unrealisable at the time of the re-
construction are turning out, or what progress, if any,
has been made with the liquidation ; anyhow, the total
absence of any attempt to build up a reserve doesn’t look
as if any serious effort was being made to get ready for
repayment. The public opinion of the institution is set
forth in the fact that the last-recorded sale of shares (£5
paid up) was, so far as Tim Bullktin knows, made, some
four months ago, at Bs. 4d., at which price they would
yield 36 per cent, dividends to the investor.
» * ♦ *
The City Bank of Sydney has a profit for the
half-year just ended of £10,019 10s. sd. and as the 5 per
cent, dividend uses up the £lO,OOO, the £l9 10s. sd. goes
to strengthen the position of the bank. The most remark-
able thing about the City Bank is the consistently small
amount of the balance left over. In July, ’95, it paid a 5
per cent, dividend with £3O 9s. 3d. over, and in January
it paid the same div. and had £4 9s. 2d. left. There is
nothing else of a very surprising character in the ac-
counts. The deposits have declined by £32,000. The
cash on hand has diminished by £34,000, and the advances
have increased a little. Compared with things as they
were two years ago the reserve fund has been reduced by
£119,000, or more than one-half, and the amount at credit
of profit and loss has declined by £9O, some small sums
having been taken out of it to pay dividends with in the
old 6 per cent. days. There is no present prospect of any
attempt to reinstate the sorely diminished reserve. The
item “ bankpreniisesand furniture” has increased slightly,
but the City Bank’s premises and furniture have appeared
as a bigger item in each balance-sheet for a considerable
time past. Also, the sheet, as usual, contains as little
information as can well be put into such a document. It
doesn’t even show the amount of working expenses.
* * * *
The Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney has
put up another excellent record. During ’95, £6,000,000
of deposits have been set free, yet the cash on hand has
increased largely instead of diminishing, as might have
been expected, and now stands £2,500,000 with
£455,000 of Govt, securities to hack it up. The half-year’s
profits (£39,474) are larger than those for last half; note-
circulation and deposits have increased, and the reserve
stands at £1,010,000. An 8 per cent, dividend is declared.
The two secrets of the success of the Commercial of
Sydney are that it was moderate in its dividends in good
times, and accumulated a large reserve, and that it put a
strict limit on the amount to be advanced on any single
security. Most small banks, at all events, that have burst
have been killed mainly by lending a huge lump of their
capital to one customer; and one that is now alive and
doing well lived in terror for years because its whole exis-
tence then depended on the survival of an immensely
bloated and diseased person who, if he had attempted to
run after a tram-car, would have fallen down dead on the
track. He didn't run after any tram-car, as it happened,
and he lived to grow rich again and pay up his over-
draft and put tilings straight, but for a long and dreadful
period that gouty mass of meat was almost the only asset
the bank possessed.

That well-regulated pawnshop, the N.S.W.

The Sydney Harbor Collieries Co. has bought a
site on the shores of beautiful Taylor Bay whereon to sink
its shaft, and put up its huge unsightly machinery, and
its pile of coal-dust and grime. Our Sooty-full Harbor !
* * * *
More cablegrams continue to arrive to the effect
that the shareholders of the E.S. and A. Bank “ cordially
approve” of the proposed reduction in deposit interest.
Considering that the English shareholders simply propose
that they shall save from £30,000 to £69,000 a year off the
Australian depositor, and thereby probably avoid heavy
calls, the possibility of worse trouble, and give themselves
a chance of a dividend at the other fellow’s expense
couldn’t their cordial approval be taken for granted ? The
cordial approval of the Australian depositors would be a
lot more to the point.
Considering that the E.S. and A. Bank—now
trying to reconstruct some more—was subjected to a
rigid British audit at the time of its first reconstruction,
and there was a bowelless scrutiny of all the securities,
and everything was supposed to be written down to bed-
rock values what is the exact value of a British audit?
Castlemaine (Vic.) Brewery Co. reports another
good half-year, a great increase in the beer output, and a,
7 per cent, dividend. What an amount of accumulated
hiccough is represented in that 7 per cent. ! Tainworth
(N.S.VV.) Gas Co. pays at the rate of 10 per cent, per an.
(£500), and has £124 to carry forward. Sydney Freehold
Land, Building, and Investment Co. pays a dividend for
the half-year at the rate of about 22) per cent, per an. ,
it hasn’t enough money to pay the amount at present,
but expects that the necessary cash will come in by
February. Newcastle and Hunter River S.S. Co. pays 6
percent. ; Sydney Gas Co., 6s. per share out of a half-
year’s profit of £S2,(KM), which profit is one of the most
solid reasons yet advanced for a municipally-owned gas
supply. N.B.W. Shale and Oil Co. pays 6d. a share.
* * » *
The S.M.H. now reports the return of a number
of miners who left Newcastle for Coolgardie, some time
ago. They tell a gruesome story of the number of men
hard up in Perth; of the graves they had passed of men
who had perished ; of the want of work, and of the im-
pending stampede Eastwards shortly. The consolation
they get from the Sunday boomster is that these men
struck the field at the wrong time, and that repeated
warnings had been given not to go out there before the
exemptions expire. To speak of warnings in the face of
the sensational telegrams, reports, interviews, and every
other form of news appearing in all the papers is rich.
* * * *
N.S.W. Attorney-General Want lately decided
that married women living apart from their husbands
under protection orders could not hold publicans’
licenses. This meant ruin to a number of grass-widows,
who could not sell out on a falling market before renewal-
day. Owing to pressure, however, the Government has
conceded that, pending new legislation, the renewals will
no* be opposed.
* * * *
Insurance societies are exempted from the audit
provisions of the new Vic. Companies Bill!
» * * *
Though the fire in Block 11 of the B.H. Pro-
prietary has involved an outlay to date of under £20,000,
the half-yearly report shows that indirectly it has been a
severe handicap. In the previous half-year, 6,233,7200 z.
silver and 19,358 tons lead had been produced ; in the
half-year ended Nov. 39, the output amounted to but
4,158,5610 z. silver and 13,711 tons lead. Yet this wasi not
all due to the fire. The output had outgrown the mine’s
legitimate capacity, and there was consequently irreclaim-
able waste of labor, material, and metal in many direc-
tions. General-manager Howell had begun to ease off
before he Lglt; and Mr. Stewart has pursued the policy
further. The remarkable feature of the half-year has
been that, though the output has been restricted con-
siderably more than had been contemplated, and though
there has been this actual outlay of £19,886 on account of
the fire, the monthly shilling div. has been maintained.
But the revenue has only just permitted it; the
net profit has amounted to £292,162, and the divs.
have absorbed £288,090, leaving only £4162 to
carry to profit and loss. But for the sustained
advance in the selling price of silver and lead
one div. must have been dropped ; if only the average
quotations for the previous half-year had been realised
the income would have been £28,510 less. There have
been savings, too, in many directions. The co. used to
be bled pitilessly' by the European refiners and metal-
dealers. Now, by extensions made during the half-year,
it deals with the whole of its bullion at its own refinery at
Port Pirie, and besides saving on the actual process of
refining, ships its metals separately, sending some of its
lead (as well as much of its silver) direct to the East. The
open quarries have now got down to good ore, so that
the average value per ton of the ore treated in this half-
year was £3 13s. 3d., as against £3 3s. lOd. in the previous
half. The open-cut work has also led to a great reduction
in the timber bill. For the June-Nov. half of 1894 it
amounted to £51,570: for the Dec.-May half of 1895 it
was £49,221. About the highest point reached was in the
first half of 1892, when £78,743 was spent in this direction.
This last half-year it was down to £13,851. The coke bill
has also been very much lowered, and not only because a
smaller quantity has been consumed.
To Nov. 30 last, the B.H. Proprietary had paid
(in nine years) 101 divs., amounting to £5,752,000; cash
bonuses amounting to £88,000; cash received in payment
for the British, £576,000 ; and shares of the nominal value
of £1,744,000 a grand total of £8,160,000. In its ten
years of existence, the co’s works have treated 2,666,908
tons ore for a product of 75,774,7430 z. silver, 291,607 tons
lead, 1782 tons antimonial lead, 15,835)0z. gold, and 2603
tons copper. The quantity of gold saved has considerably
exceeded that stated, but the refiners give no credit for
it; so that, with a small exception, the only gold credited
has been extracted from the bullion at the co’s own
refinery, which was started on a very small scale only six
years ago.
During its last half-year the B.H. Proprietary
Co. obtained an average price of 2s. 8)d. per oz. for its
fine silver and £lO Bs. Id. per ton for its lead. This was
ljd. per oz. more for silver and 10s. Id. per ton more for
lead than in the previous half. The highest prices ever
realised (the average for a half-year) were in the Mav-
Nov. half of 1890. Silver then sold at 4s. 6jd. and lead
at £l4 16s. 7d.-thanks to the American legislation. The
Proprietary Co., by the way, is now shipping refined lead
direct to America.
Capt. Paul has resigned the post of consulting
engineer of the Junction North. This is one of the out-
comes of the efforts to twist the B. H. Lode at the north
end. Paul is manager of the B.H. North ; and it is dis-
puted whether the North or the Junction North gets
most of the Junction new lode as it goes northward. As
one man couldn’t be expected to “ barrack ” on both
sides, the Junction North had to be thrown up. Phillips
manager of the Junction, is willing to take up the Junc-
tion North work, upon the strength of which announce-
ment the latter stock has hardened.

for the operation. Parisians are heavy buyers. *

A good reef, carrying free gold, is said to have
been struck at a depth of 240 ft. at Wyalong-the lowest
level at which gold has been found on that field.
According to a London correspondent of the
Aroi's, Prof. Schnabel, having marie inquiry into Ash
croft’s sulphide process at his works, declares the dis
covery to be among the most important of the century.
It is really time something definite was known
as to the value of the Great Boulder lode at the bottom
level. Rumor says it is not only poorer than at any
other part of the mine, but that it is smaller is
it a lode at all, or a dyke formation with gold -rich’ it is
true, but in decomposed diorite and schist with a’verv
small percentage of quartz or other well-known matrix
for gold !

From London : “ Everything is quiet here, both
in African and Australian mines, but, If anything, the
Australian mining market is better than the African.
We are expecting Tower prices till after the holidays, when
things may pick up a little. Aside from the Great
Boulder, and two or three more at Hannan s, there docs
not seem any improvement in Coolgardie since we were
out there, and, some day, not far away, there must be an
awful shaking-up of old bones. It does not seem that
Menzies is anything near as good as they say. Some of
the papers here are going for Schmeisser because he
did not report favorably on that district. [How
quiet this has been kept!] Of course, as is always
the case -if the mine is worthless, blame the expert
who reports on it. From what I can learn, Schmeisser
has not reported favorably on many of the mines of
W.A. altogether. I am afraid his trip has been
a failure and a disappointment to a lot of London specu-
lators and promoters who look for big reports.” How
cheerful this news must be to the great W.A. army of
boomers who have been led to believe that the celebrated
German had described Coolgardie as the world’s boss
goldfield, and that his reports would pour millions of
foreign capital into the “ wild-cats,” which are all readied
up to receive them ! The. writer always regarded Herr
Schmeisser’s silence as suspicious. He wanted to get out
of the place alive. He saw enough of the Murchison in a
few days to satisfy him of its great richness - so the papers
put it. In mining always read the papers upside down.
» * * *
The last act of the Forrest Govt, to cause univer-
sal dissatisfaction is that compelling the registration of all
transfers in Perth instead of on the fields as before. This
has evidently been done in the interests of Perth lawyers
without the least regard to the convenience of mine-
owners or buyers.
» * • •
The unanimity with which all the Coolgardie
fields asked for an' extension of the exemption period is
remarkable. Six weeks’ enforced idleness by the miners
of W.A. is a consideration. Every excuse but the right
one has been pleaded - the two principal being the want
of miners and the want of water, But it is significant
that the Boulder, Hill End, and the few other mines on
good gold have not taken advantage of the exemption.
* • • •
So far, the only distinction The Bulletin can
see between W.A. gold booms and burglary is a legal
technicality.
* * * *
The Queen Margaret mine, 1.0. U., has been
taken over, after his own and other examination, by
Harvey Patterson, one of the Broken Hill directors. A
co. has been privately formed, and £9OOO working-capital
put up. Simpson, formerly mine-manager at Block 14, is
in charge. His last report appearing makes it look like a
Great Boulder. It speaks of drives in 5,6, and as high as
30 oz. stuff, and estimates the stone at grass at 400 tons.

The persons who have an option on the Croesus
mine, Hannan's, have asked for an extension of time, and
got it by paying £3OOO, which, with the £2OOO already
paid, is forfeitable failing completion of purchase. The
odds are : no revival of boom in London-no sale.

* * * *
A big attempt is being made to boom the
Norseman field in Adelaide, and S.A. investors, who
pretty well run the field, gave a banquet to themselves
recently. Chairman Harrold, a big Adelaide merchant,
alleged that already £lOO,OOO has been paid to Norseman
prospectors alone, and the capital invested in the field
amounts to £1,000,000. Then a mining captain, who
must be pretty hardened, said they had reefs on the field
for a length of 40 miles and 8 miles wide, and he didn’t
believe there were any wild cats there 1

in the field’s early d|ys, now return from £lOOO to £l5OO
annual rentals. But it is risky property to hold, the slump
being only a matter of time.

Croesus again. “ A discovery of rich stone has

Premier Reid’s reflections in May—Kruger
Caused My Gew-gaw ; Kaiser Created My Gran-
deur, &e.
Playford and Gillies are the only Agents-
General in England who have not been knighted.
Both have refused the bauble.
Blackwood says Phil. May does not seem
at home in Punch. As The Bulletin remarked
months ago—May and December.
George Dean’s step-father, Finch (supposed
bein Heaven), was recently forest-thinning for
N.S.W. Govt, on the Murrumbidgee.
tt A Melbourne paper remarks that Kipling
“ has raised the status of India as a place to be
born in.” Where was Thackeray born ?
Tas. Treasurer Fysh’s visiting-cards within
24 hours of his knighthood :
Hon. Sir Phillip Oakley Fysh, K.C.M.G.
Martin Treacy, Victoria’s pioneer boniface,
died at Wagga Wagga, the other day. He ran
the Daniel O’Connell Hotel, Collins-street, in 1840.
Colonel Mair, ex-Paymaster to Victorian
Police Force, has been drawing a pension of over
£4OO for upwards of 30 years. He is 93 years old.
The Sultan of Turkey, it is printed, is “a
total abstainer.” Like many other teetotallers,
he puts women, instead of whisky, into his soda-
water.
Madame Melba’s ex-friend, the Duke of
Orleans, has, it is cabled, met with an accident
“ while hunting in Italy.” What he was hunting
is not stated. Probably it was—as usual.
Bishop Harmer’s wife had a daughter on
Jan. 14. This is only extraordinary because it is
nearly 50 years since the last child was born to a
Bishop of Adelaide.
Artist Tom Roberts is engaged to marry
Miss Williamson, daughter of a former partner in
the well-known Melb. drapery firm (Craig, Wil-
liamson and Thomas).
Mrs. M‘Lean, mother of Vic. M.L.A.,
Allan M‘Lean, died the other day. She was the
first white woman to cross the Latrobe river into
North Gippsland in 1842.
The “father” of the Melbourne magis-
tracy, James Noonan, died the other day, aged 90
years. He was a member of the firm which built
the Vic. North-Eastern railway.
Of the little group present when Gov.
Hindmarsh declared S.A. a British province only
two survive: C. C. Wright, of Yankalilla, and
Mrs. Frances Merrick, settlers of —who are
both hearty still.
With the appointment of an independent
Public Service board, the drunken N.S.W. stipen-
diary magistrates should take the pledge and keep
it. Anyhow, they should refrain from being seen
very drunk in public.
Massive, metallic, strident Antoinette
Sterling is to be married again. Her intended is
a harmless and venerable Quaker, with a small
title and large means. It is indeed hard to imagine
Antoinette’s hubby anything but a Quaker.
The eminent Blake, Q.C., whose power-
ful personality made so great an impression
in Sydney, is described by those who knew the
father of the N.S.W. Constitution as being mar-
vellously like Wentworth in general appearance.
There is not a red-haired man in either of
the Vic. Houses of Legislature. There are several
whose hair has been red but has paid them the
reward which “ scarlet runners ” obtain in late
life, when their hair becomes a rich creamy white.
Howard Vernon (an Austra-
lian native, by the way) has now
been associated with the Firm’s
Comic Opera Co. for 15 years,
during which period he has only
missed one-and-a-half perform-
ances. The half was the first part
of “ Mikado ” one time in Christ-
church. Vernon overslept him-
self, after a week’s insomnia
caused by neuralgia, and arrived
at the theatre in great distress at
his remissness—which was, how-
ever, very easily forgiven. Ver-
non is another Thespian who
started his career in a church
choir. He was amongst the Scots
Church choristers 25 years or so
ago, and in church sat alongside
violinist Johann Kruse. After
that pious beginning Mr. Vernon
degenerated to minstrels, from
which he gradually ascended to
his present exalted position. His
first operatic engagement was with
Lyster. He once had serious inten-
tions of starring in Shakespeare.
It was only his tremendous success
as Bunthorne in “ Patience ” that
determined him to stick to opera.
The Tasmanian Governor
(Viscount Gormanston) simply
won’t know the local bigwigs—
so far as they are concerned, H.E.
is a perfect hedgehog tightly
rolled up. When old John
Watchorn was elected mayor,
the other day, for the third time,
he drove out to Government
House to pay his devoirs to the
Governor, and got a perfect
freezer. “ I bowed and he
bowed,” says honest John, “and
then I cleared out, while he
looked at me coldly through his
eye-glass—never even offered me
a whisky.” Which recalls the
time when Crouch, the blue-ribbonite, was Mayor
of Hobart. After the inspection of “the troops”
was concluded, on Queen’s birthday, his worship
invited a number of the most important per-
sonages to repair to the Town Hall and drink
her Majesty’s health. Of -course, they all went! —
and then the Deluge, for the “ fizz ” turned out
to be “ Vigorine,” and a' remarkably “dry”
brand, too.
Omen ! Justice Windeyer was taken on
board the American steamer, en route to Europe,
by the police-boat Nemesis.
N.S.W. Justice Stephen has returned from
Europe much improved in health. In his S.M.
Herald interview his Honor talks chiefly horse —
though the only N.S.W. Judge entitled to be
heard with respect on horsey subjects is Mr. Jus-
tice Innes.
Sir T. Gordon Sprigg, Cape Colony’s new
Premier, is a peace-in-our-time kind of individual
with strong Church leanings—the antipodes of
Cecil Rhodes. Try and imagine Joey Cook, of
the God-fearing Ministry, succeeding Damchicago
Dibbs. and you’ve got it exactly.

Speight’s return to the Vic. railways is
more likely than outsiders think, although not so
much so as the new firm of Hancock, Haddon,
and Co. think. If David Syme would only sell
the Age for twa hunder thoosand poond, and join
Nimmo in Scotia, Speight would vault in flying.
Symon, Q.C., the leading Adelaide lawyer,
got .£lOOO and costs as counsel for the Slug Hill
G.M. Co. in its litigation with the vendors.
The case was heard in Perth, and ended in a com-
promise. As Mr. Symon has a practice worth,
say, =65000 a year, no wonder he shirks Parlia-
ment.
The appointment of Statistician Coghlan,
himself a civil engineer by profession, as a N.S.W.
public service commissioner, is very acceptable to
the professional section of the service, which in
the past has often found itself treated with scant
consideration as compared with the merely clerical
division.
N.S.W. Premier Reid : “I joined the
Highland Society because I’m a Lowlander.”
Most members of it joined it because they were
thirsty. The writer knows one ex-member of a
branch whose father was Irish, mother English,
and who was himself born in India; and yet
passed on the lines that his grandfather was born
in Glasgow ! That humorist also joined a Hiber-
nian association, and only just failed getting into
a “ Lancashire club ” !
HOWARD VERNON, as Ko-Ko, in “ Mikado.
Photo, by Meets.
The endeavor to educate people into
pronouncing Gov. Brassey’s name “ Bracey,” has
failed absolutely. Only those “ who know, you
know,” do it now. People generally won’t have it
at any price. B-r-a-s-s, they say, spells Brass, and
e-y spells ey, and the name is Brassey without any
frills. Practically the propah pronunciation has
gone out, and His Ex. answers to the common
pronunciation most docilely.
JRe Ned Kelly’s baptism. Dean O’Hea
heard Red Kelly, living somewhere about the
Victorian Donnybrook, had a baby, but wouldn’t
have it christened. So the good Dane buggied
out and insisted on having the little candidate for
the kingdom of heaven brought forth and holy
watered.
Youl, the comic coroner, declares that an
alleged Chinese doctor, who fell dead in Melb.
recently, was really an Irishman with a sham pig-
tail. The Bulletin long ago predicted the day
when Europeans would have to masquerade as
Chinamen in order to be able to make a living in
Australia.
The Earl of Yarmouth is not at Rock-
hampton, but at the “Rocks,” a riverside mansion
near Mackay. There he spends his time and
expends his intellect in growing mangoes and hen-
fruit, and sells the proceeds. A more respectable
vocation than waving his hind leg to please the
female bigwigs of Melbourne.
At the time of the Beatrice-Battenberg
marriage everyone knew that Her Gracious dis-
countenanced the match. Beatrice, so runs the
tale, did not dare to tell her mother of her engage-
ment till some weeks after it was accomplished ;
then the old lady was offended, and would not
speak to her daughter for a week. “So sly of
Beatrice, ” she is recorded to have said.
Will Champion, in his coming criminal-
libel fight, keep up the good old English tradi-
tional claim that the jury is all-important in
deciding criminal libels —that the question of
libel or no-libel is wholly for the jury, no matter
how the Judge directs even in the matters of law ?
Fox’s Libel Act was passed to accentuate this
position, but Judges kick sullenly against it still.
Mr. Frank Hutchinson, bosom friend of
the late Plain Bill Dailey, and once very widely
known as a Sydney journalist, who has been
chosen to edit the new official N.S.W. Handbook,
has been little heard of during late years. In
days of yore his daily “ Town Talk ” column in
the Evening News was famous, as indeed it
deserved to be.
Melb. demise : Horatio Beauchamp, the
straight-as-a-die business-man, and Knight of the
Hammer, in the days of Stubbs, Tennent, Easey,
Fraser, Cohen, Gemmell, Lyons. Partnered one
time with Byrne, another with Rocke. A genuine
philanthropist, with a fanatic-hatred of the clergy.
He got up the Juvenile Traders’ Home and School,
which occupied a two-storeyed place, comer of
Russell-street and Flinders lane, and did a lot of
good.
The Bulletin prints the latest
photo, of Maoriland Premier Seddon,
which all who have seen the sturdy West
Coaster must vote the “ best yet.”
“ King Dick ” is here seen with his
chest right out and his “ boss ” look on
his frontispiece. From the very beginning
of R.J.’s years of discretion he ! s had
to go “on his own,” and with a clear
brain in his big head and good under-
standings beneath him, he has put his
foot down firmly wherever he has located.
Coming out to the golden West Coast in
his 20th year, he found that place adapted
to his constitution. The chief sport of
Kumara district was fisticuffs, and the
chief professor thereof an Irishman who
had wiped out all comers. Dick Seddon,
a stayer but not a “ scientist,” wiped out
that Irishman, after 20 lovely rounds,
and has been respected ever since ; also,
he has ever since been known as “Kumara
Dick.” That incident gave Seddon a big
start, but he was quicker with his fist
than his tongue. In those days, however
incredible it may now sound, he was as
slow of speech as Aaron. But by dili-
gent study he acquired a greater know-
ledge of words, and later on came into
public life as a member of the Road
Board, then into the County Council,
and eventually into Parliament, where
he talked to such purpose that John Bal-
lance chose him as Minister for Mines
and Works. In consideration of the way
he licked the Kumara Irishman, he was
also made Minister of Defence, and when
his leader died the Premiership fell to
Seddon. Although the women of M.L.
declare that Premier Seddon’s greatest
deed was advising the Governor to
sign the Act extending the franchise
to women, he will longest be remembered as
the man who squashed the great Midland Railway
swindle, rescued 6,000,000 acres of public lands
from that soulless corporation, and ended the
biggest incubus which has weighed upon the pro-
vince for years. When Seddon seized the Mid-
land line he was commonly supposed to have done
a ruinous and suicidal act, but the result of the
arbitration showed that he knew more law than
most of his critics, and was the one man in Maori-
land with courage enough for a great emergency.
At Maryborough, Q., long ago, a man
accused of horse-stealing challenged Mayor Booker
when the latter was called as a juror. Booker
was angry, and as prisoner had exceeded his right
of unquestioned challenge, he was called on to
show cause. “ This being a horse-stealing case,”
said lawyer M‘Devitt, who defended, “we don’t
care about a mare being on the jury.”
The diplomatic Reid ! Of the newly-
appointed N.S.W. Public Service Commission,
Mr. Coghlan is of Irish extraction, Commissioner
Wilson of Scotch, and Commissioner Barling, Eng-
lish. By the way, the list of Commissioners is
not, by any means, so Parkesian as many people
expected it to be. And the story goes that the
Premier had so many applications from M’sP. and
ex-M’sP. for Commissionerships that he (very
sensibly) decided to appoint nobody with a
political history.
"H. : “ A few skeins in the character of the
German Emperor. He hates England. With his
withered hand he is like crook-back Richard, re-
flecting on one branch of his parentage—not to say
he has any animosity to his mother, but he sees
the wisdom of the then young Bismarck, who
opposed the marriage. He imbibed his hatred of
England from Bismarck, and retained it when he
determined to be his own Bismarck.”
Rev. F. B. Boyce, a Sydney teetotal apostle,
says Queen Victoria has described strong drink
as “a great curse.” The old lady takes some of
the curse (Lochnagar is the brand) every night.
Old N.S.W. ex-Chief-Justice Stephen also des-
cribed strong drink as “a curse ” —and yet used
lovingly to hold up a glass of his favorite tipple
(red wine) to the light and ask his' Visitors to
admire its lovely color.
Col. Cameron, maternal grandfather of
Premier Charles Cameron Kingston, eloped with
Ludivina da Silva, daughter of a Spanish grandee,
during the first Peninsular war. The Colonel was
stationed in Sydney when old Sir George Kingston
married his daughter. This little family history
sets forth the S.A. Premier’s claim to have Scotch,
Spanish, Irish, and Australian blood in his veins,
for he was bom in Adelaide.
One Christopher Snowdon, of South St.
Kilda (Vic.), has applied at Sydney Patent Office
to protect “An apparatus usable either as a race
starting-machine, as a clothes-line, a combined
elevator and overhead conveyer, and a self-closing
gate for such as railway-crossings.” The next
invention may be a combination infant’s feeding-
bottle and tram-motor, convertible into a 40-acre
paddock, a street-organ, or a pair of bloomers.
Interviewed in Sydney the other day,
Albert F. Calvert, Esq., (and valet) was asked
“ whether he was the gentleman who is sometimes
described as a Westralian explorer ? ” “If I am,”
modestly replied the great man, “ I do not lay
claim to anything of the sort, but I have done a
good deal of pioneering work in that country.”
People who detest cheek are glad to notice that
Calvert (and valet) is being sent ’Ome by the
Westralian Govt, with a very large flea in his
ear.

Dear Sur,—On monday next lamto be made a Mare,

half a bag of oafs and a “ bran mash ” for the

Ail endeavor is being industriously made
in Melbourne to ridicule Protection because Labor-
leader Trenwith, who is suffering from cataract, is
going to England to undergo an operation. “Why
not patronise the local doctors ? ” the Freetraders
Photo, by Wrigglesworth [?] Binns.
Premier SEDDON, of Maoriland.
are saying. The plain fact is that Trenwith
wanted to be operated on in Melbourne, but he
was advised that the voyage would do his health
good, and, apart from that, his friends thought
that a trip to England, which would bring the
Vic. labor leader into contact with the labor
leaders of the old world, would be of political
advantage to him. Davitt strongly pressed him
to visit England, and he is going largely on account
of the Irish politician’s persuasion.
Gov. Brassey’s pictures, lent to Melb.
National Gallery and Ballarat Exhibition, count
strongly in his favor. There’s little or nothing of
what Mr. Sparkler called “ bigod nonsense ” about
them. Sea pieces that depict the ocean as it is,
cornfields and green landscapes which give fair
play to the imagination, and other works of honest
art rather than insincere mannerism. The
general public can see the point of the Governor’s
pictures without having studied culchaw and the
jargon of its apostles.
The usual story has gob itself attached to
Brassey. One day when the Sunbeam was
“thrown open” to the Tasmanians, a curious
visitor, mistaking the Governor in his nautical
rig for a small officer of the boat, ranged alongside
and asked : “Is Brassey aboard ? ” “ I believe he
is,” was the reply. “ Vou might point him out
to me, old chap.” Brassey led the stranger to
the nearest mirror, and,;, pointing to his own
reflection, said placidly,. “ There, that old chap
with the grey whiskers, he’s your man.”
