POLITICAL
POINTS
Rumors are rife of two coming vacancies
in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly.
Albury was the first proposed Federal
capital, Wentworth the second.
Premier Reid’s jingoism is another of his
fiscal inconsistences. A big war would do more
for Australian Protection than a dozen tariffs.
Description of two parties in Bananaland
Assembly: ‘ ‘ Men of some conscience and no
ability. Men of some ability and no conscience.”
In Queensland, some candidates don’t
stand for certain districts, but certain districts
stand for some candidates —like sea-beach donkeys
awaiting riders.
Both the Cairns political candidates—
Lissner and Givens—come from the Towers. They
hadn’t a white man available at Cairns—even for
a member of Parliament.
Maoriland is the only province in Aus-
tralasia which deals fairly with each of the British
kingdoms by proclaiming public holidays on the
days sacred to St. Patrick, St. George, and St.
Andrew.
The irony of it ! Reid, Carruthers and
Co. encouraging and subsidising N.S.W. State
technical education, and, at the same time, taking
away the work which alone could render such edu-
cation profitable.
Premier Kingston (S.A.) never appears in
any case in the criminal courts whilst Attorney-
General, nor will he allow his partner to do so.
This leaves him entirely free in exercising his
functions as a Grand Jury.
The Employers’ Liability Bill still awaits
its fate in Vic. The old Act expires shortly, and,
as the Upper House will almost certainly reject
the Bill, Labor-members had better be ready with
another, on the half-a-loaf principle.
Premier Reid reserves the sand-shifting
business as far as possible for the people of N.S.W.,
but he imports his Land-tax Commissioner from
S.A. The tender care which gives the local man
all the sand-shifting is very touching.
Lady Brassey is agitating to establish in
Melbourne a Society for the Prevention of Cruelty
(o Children. After which, we presume, State-
school children will be spared the pain of singing
the Unnatural Anathema for the edification of
imported Governors.
Sydney Daily Telegraph calls it “a
gratifying national enthusiasm ” when Englishmen
shout for war, but “ rabid jingoism ” when
Yankees indulge the same extravagance; and, as
if to balance the Hemispheres, the San Francisco
Bulletin calls the English indulgence “ rabid
jingoism” and the Yankee “national enthusiasm.”
Who is the solitary
exception from the Vic.
Parliamentary subscribers
to the Trey with testi-
monial ?
In Wesbraiia you are
still liable, if the police do
their duty, to be stopped
in the streets by an officer
if you are out after 10 p.m.,
asked your name and ad-
dress, whether “bond or
free,” and requested to ex-
plain your reasons for being
abroad at that suspicious
hour. Also, you may be
inspected all over by the
light of Robert’s lantern
so that he can identify you
again if need be. In old,
sleepy Westralia it is still
a cause for grave suspicion
to be out after ten, and
they still want to know
whether you are “bond or
free ” from sheer force of
habit. The old customs
are falling into disuse,
however, for the new
iniquities who have come
over by thousands from
the “ other side ” are a fro-
ward race who won’t go to
bed at 9.30, and who scoff
at the local policeman
when he wants to know if
they are ‘ ‘ bond ; ” and
when the officer demands
their names and addresses
he finds they are almost all
Bill and live in a tent;
and the hurry of the new
order of things sweeps the
Superannuated constable
off his feet, so to speak.
Mr. Callan has again reluctantly consented
to stand for the Fitzroy (Q.). In his speech appears
the neatly-disguised threat—thus: “He had been
caused great anxiety by the threatened closing of
the (Mt. Morgan) co’s works from scarcity of water,
but he was satisfied such a calamity might now be
averted. The co., he might say, had no intention
to reduce wages, as rumored, but would always
give a fair day’s wages for a fair day’s work. This
was away from the Subject; but he had mentioned
it to induce friendly feelings between employer
and employes.”
One of the most significant tell-tales re
the manner in which the Reid Government Party
has catspawed the N.S.W. Labor Party, is the
unblushing suppression, by Sydney D.T. —the
Reid organ—of the civil war within the N.S.W.
Political Labor Leagues, born of dissatisfaction
with the Parliamentary Labor Party. Whereas,
in respect of Reidophile questions, the D.T. con-
trives always to find three columns of space
for every two of the S.M.H., in reporting
the annual meeting of the P.E.L., the S.M.H.
gives a column to the D.T’s third. The Herald
is not tied to the chariot-wheels of Georgy-Porgey.
Since the
yearlB74there
has been a
great and con-
tinuous fall in
the prices
both of this
country’s ex-
ports and im-
ports, but the
prices of ex-
ports have fal-
len much the
faster and the
heavier of the
two. The wool
and other raw
produce which
fetched £lO2B
in London 22
years ago
realises £590
now ; while the
foreign -manu-
factured goods
which cost
£lO2B in Syd-
ney at the
same date now
cost ,£714. In
other words, a
country which
doesn’t manu-
facture for it-
self, but lives
by dragging
raw produce
out of the
ground and
sending it
away to ex-
change for
manufactured
articles, has
now, assuming values were level in ’74, to send
away 20s. worth of produce to buy 16s. 6d. worth
of foreign goods. God bless the great importing
policy !
Frank Madden is stated to be tired of
political fame, and to meditate retirement from
Parliament in favor of Wrixon. The latter tries
to be liberal, but is responsible for the flogging
and Sunday-paper-suppression Acts, which stand
always as a reproach against his capacity.
The alacrity with which men offer them-
selves as army volunteers all through the British
empire has probably more to do with the
plethora in the labor market than with
any zeal for maintaining England’s greatness.
William Pitt, in the old war days, was in the habit
of saying, when recruits came in slowly, “I’ll put
another ha’penny on the 21b. loaf, and that will
spur their loyalty. ” The hungry and unemployed
make the rank and file for kings.
THE GAMBLING CURSE.
SCHOOL-TEACHER (endeavoring to explain the meaning of the worn
“harness ”to a small boy): “ What does your father put
on the horse? ”
THE SMALL BOY (his face brightening) : “'E puts on all ’e can, if’e
thinks it'll win."
In England, during 1895, 513,000 acres of
wheat-growing land was abandoned ; the local
wheat supply diminished by 2,000,000 quarters ;
and 20,000 agricultural laborers and their families
were thrown out of work. To quote the report of
the National Agricultural Union, Britain is “be-
coming more and more dependent on foreign and,
possibly, hostile countries. ”
The departure of Gov. Lamington for
Queensland has been seized by Chamberlain and
Co. as an opportunity of “ tightening the bonds ”
between England and Australia. When the
Governor arrives he will speedily discover the
opinion held by the best and brightest of young
Australians—that the best thing between England
and Australia is the ocean.
“West Australia,” says the Queenslander,
“occupies the proud and unique {position of being
a colony without politics and without a policy.
There are, therefore, no political parties, and out-
side of a very narrow circle no one cares a rap
about public affairs. Everybody minds his own
business, and as a happy result everybody seems
contented and well-off.” And the same thing,
when one comes to think of it, might have been
said about the New Testament hogs which fell
overboard in Galilee. Also, it is one of the best
descriptions ever written of a snail on a cabbage-
leaf.
A SYMPATHETIC AUDITOR.
BARD'S WIPE : “I wish you’d read your new poem to yourself — you’ve made
baby cry.”
BARD: “I assure you, my dear, there’s not one more pathetic passage."
Mr. Charles Lyne, hitherto secretary (at
£7OO a year) of N.S.W. Public Works Committee,
has been appointed secretary to N.S.W. Civil
Service Board at £6OO a year. The diplomatic
Charles, seeing that the Works Committee
mightn’t last, whereas the Public Service Board
would probably be eternal, told the Colonel not to
shoot and came down. The new Works Com-
mittee secretary will probably get about £4OO, and
will be well paid at that. The billet has, up to
date, been the fattest and easiest in the N.S.W.
service.
Now that revenue is so much wanted in so
many places, The Bulletin respectfully suggests
that the minimum fine for ordinary curse-language
should be raised to 405., and for bad curse-
language, including one very loathsome expres-
sion in particular, to £5, and that the police should
have strict instructions to seize upon every person
heard saying “blanky” in a public place and
bring him under the 40s. section. Also, that
procedure should be simplified by allowing the
arrested person to acknowledge his guilt at once
and pay the price of his curse to the inspector
at the police-station without any publicity. And
further, that the proceeds of this enactment be
kept in a separate fund to build a particular line
of railway to be known thereafter as the Blanky
Railway. The average Treasurer, when he is hard
up, simply dumps another penny on the income-
tax. What he should do is to put another pound
on the minimum fine for bad language, and order
the police to listen harder than ever for the man
who says “blanky ” in public places, and to run
him in remorselessly.
Will some democratic M.L.A., when Par-
liament meets, move for a complete return of the
number of people whom the present Government
shoved into the N.S.W. public service during the
18 months or so that it has been in office, and
whom it was shoving in almost up to the last hour
before the political patronage era came to an end ’
Also, as Premier Reid has shown that he can’t be
trusted to tell the wholly unvarnished truth in
such matters ( vide the shamelessly garbled state-
ment of Dibbs’s political appointments which he
presented to the Assembly some time ago) will
that member also suggest that the Civil Ser-
vice Commissioners supply the return ? Further
it would be interesting to know how many soli-
citors who have been struck off the rolls for
disgraceful conduct the Government h*s provided
witj temporary or other billets at good salaries
And, lastly, the public wants to hear about a
recent case m which a hopelessly useless dude was
sent down to a public office to be employed at a
big wage, and how he was returned by the boss on
the ground that there was no work for him t-r. j
and he couldn’t do it if there was any ; and aboS

The “ special conditions ” now inserted in
N.S.W. Govt, contracts set forth that “if it shall
be represented to the Secretary for Public Works
that an undue number of men are being employed
by the contractor who have not been domiciled in
this province for six months previously to such
employment, and such allegation be proved to his
satisfaction, the Minister shall have power to cau
on the contractor to discharge any or all such
men,”&c. The Minister, it will be observed, is
the sole judge of what constitutes an undue num-
ber, and he may prohibit the contractor employing
any men from other provinces. How is this for
Free trade and a beautiful F ederal spirit ?
A well-known Sydney grocer of the
Foreign-trade persuasion publishes what purports
to be the cost of a week’s groceries to one of his
clients under the old tariff and under the new one.
Said list shows that sugar, condensed milk, pre-
served fish, rice, cornflour and salt fish have gone
down a whole lot in price owing to. the new tariff
—which tariff, strange to say, levies exactly the
same duties on these goods as before. Also, there
has been a big drop in sago owing to the present
enlightened Government taking the duty off,
which is still more remarkable, as there was no
duty on. And this production is headed “ a
good significance of the advantages of Freetrade.
Great Scott!
Cardinal Moran’s proposal to form an Irish
volunteer regiment in Sydney carries us back to
1879, when a Tory Home Ruler, the late King-
Harman, endeavored to extend the volunteer
movement to Ireland. The bill was hailed with
acclamation, for, in this respect, Ireland labors
under a genuine grievance. But in the Lords, one
of those individuals—rare even among Irishmen —
who knew Ireland, the Earl of Inehiquin (Smith
O’Brien’s brother), opposed it so stoutly as to
secure its rejection. A few months after the
Land League war started, and when the finest
peasantry in the world was potting landlords,
bailiffs and agents with obsolete blunderbusses,
J. Bull thanked Heaven that he had barely
escaped sending them Govt, rifles of the latest
brand.
At Sydney the week before last J. H.
Hellings (N.S.W.) won the 100 and 220yds. Aus-
tralasian swimming championships from N.S.W.,
Vic. and M.L. competitors. He deserves special
recognition because he is, in the strictest sense, an
amateur. His 220yds. win was the sensation of
the carnival, for the finish by which he beat
Gormly was a great exhibition of gameness.
Gormly, himself a man of rare pluck, remarked
immediately afterwards: “I was sure I had him
beaten ; fifteen yards from home I was a length
ahead. It all only proves what I have always
said—that Jack’s never beaten till the numbers
go up.” One feature of Hellings’s win is that he
beat A. Cavill, the champion 100yds. and 220yds.
representative of the great swimming family.
Hellings’s trainer is W. Brees; he gave his man
Photo, by Kerry.

THE ANGLO-JAPANESE TREATY.
Waste no time in debate, lest you leave it too late,
but join it as fast as you can,
This white-imbecility, yellow-ability, great-possi-
bility plan,
This specially-wonderful, startlingly-blunderful,
Foreign-trade-thunderful plan.
Our exports will heap, and our tea will be cheap
when we joyfully cast on the stream
Our utilitarian, Japanee-sharee-on, bust-up-the-
Aryan scheme,
Our cheap-tea-and-pepperee, Mongol-eum-leperee,
fall-down-the-steperee scheme.
Oh, think not at all of the issue, but fall forthwith
in the arms of the Jap ;
In his slip-from-the-rowlocky, stick - in-the-
mullocky, me-and-John-Bullocky trap,
His join-with-your-fellowmen, let-in-the-yellow-
men, Australia-to-hell-amen trap.

All hands have been discharged from Bunker’s
Hill, being replaced by a selected staff from the Thames.
Coromandelites are confident that with deeper sinking
the Hauraki golden lode will be brought to light. This
la on the cards, but several calls may be required to
make the prediction good. Bunker’s at latest were at
6s. 6d.
Waiotahi private claim, Thames, is a steady
gold-producer. For the past 25 years the same share-
holders and staff have run the mine, and the regular
monthly return has been 200 oz. and over. The greedy
practice of picking the eyes out of the property has not
been adopted—on the contrary, the ground has been
systematically opened out and* several payable leaders
exposed, giving moderate but certain divs. If other
mines followed suit the whine for John Bull’s capital
would not be heard so often, and the best ground would
not be held in London as is the case now.

S.A. railways only earned .£855,802 last year,

Launceston (Tas.) municipality provides electric-
lighting for citizens at a reasonable cost. But the Gas
Co. has much influence with Ministers, and the electric-
light is not to be adopted in any of the public buildings.
So great is the power of the gas-man that Tas. Govt,
could hardly be expected to bolster up new-fangled
notions respecting the munioipalisation of industries,
under the circs.
* * * *
Premier Braddon locally publishes a disclaimer,
setting forth that he does not now and never did own a
solitary share in the Anchor tin-mine, but that he lent his
name to the promoters of the company on the recom-
mendation of the Secretary for Mines, and for purely
philanthropic and patriotic reasons.
• * » •
On Woodlark and Horn Islands things are
pretty lively just now. Some 70 men are working on
Woodlark, but the gold-bearing belt is small, three-
fourths of the island being coral. On Horn Island the
diggers are requesting the Warden to prohibit employ-
ment of S. Sea Islanders.
The Cheque Bank, Ltd., has been established
over 20 years, and its cheques are taken as cash all over
the civilised world. The whole of its assets are vested in
trustees for the security of holders of cheques. Its
cheques, compared with ordinary money-orders, shew a
saving of over 50 per cent. All the leading banks
throughout the world, with the Bank of England first on
the list, cash the cheques, and the said Bank of England
allowed its name to be used in connection with the pros-
pectus of the Cheque Bank, Ltd. These cheques are of
great value to travellers, for those who have them can pay
hotel, steamboat an 1 railway charges with them, since
they are issued in all amounts. They are as well-known
over “ the Continent,” America, and other parts of
civilisation, as ordinarv money.*
• • • •
The latest is a secret partnership between a (say)
big-hotel manager and a labor-agency. A. applies to 8.,
labor-agent, for a billet as cook. “ Well,” says 8., “ I've
got the very thing for you. A capital vacancy at X.’s
hotel, £3 per week. You're the very man for it, and I’ve
got the giving of it away. But billets such as that don’t
go begging every day, and I must have a special bonus
from the man who gets it. I think that’s only fair.”
“ Well, what bonus do you ask?” (Here follows a demand
actually made by a S.A. labor-agent in re a billet at £3 a
week.) “ Well, supposing you get the billet, just from
week to week, without any agreement, but just taking
your chance, I want one week’s wages, £3. If I get
you a three-months’ agreement (that will stand you
m, say, £4O), I want £l5; if a six-months' agreement
I want £25; and, fora 12-months’ agreement, £3O is my
fee.” 1!! Mind you, the official-registration fee of 5s
had previously been paid ! The writer’s informant took
the billet “ from week to week,” and paid the
labor-agency extortioner £3. At the end of the
first week, he was told that there were “no
wages" for him, as the hotel-manager understood that
the first week's wages were to go to the labor-agent. “ But
I paid him £3.” “What? Paid him in advance? Just
wait a minute.” (Goes to telephone. Comes back.]
“Oh, yes; it’s all right; here's your wages.” Another
week slipped by, and then the employe got “ a week’s
notice,” on the broad ground that he “ didn’t suit.” Yet
the manager gave him an excellent “ reference.” From
enquiries made, it was found that the hotel was always
changing its cooks, its waiters, its barmen, its barmaids,
its pantrymen, and even its boots ! The solution? The
manager was “ halves” with the labor-agent!
At the end of ’B5 the S.A. Savings Bank had
86.968 depositors. The total population of the province is
only 350,000.
• • * •
Now that there is more money lost than made
over “pubs.,” brewers and liquor-houses are turning
their attention to groceries holding grog-licenses. The
wine and spirit houses are first in the field, and in one pro-
vince they have bought several grocery-groggeries that
have faced their creditors. Bung simply puts in his
nominee, who makes a pretence of being a legitimate
grocer by carrying a very small stock in that line, whilst
the bottle-trade is pushed vigorously by house-to-house
canvass. Bung gets weekly settlements from his man,
and runs no risks of moonlight flits as in the old days.
No monetary advances are necessary, neither are bills-of-
sale, and the principles of J. Barleycorn are diffused
throughout hundreds of households by means of whisky
charged in the bill as tea and sugar.
• • • •
Apropos the bung-trade, in some of the Aus-
tralias a third conviction for Sunday trading means the
cancellation of the license, so far as the licencee
is concerned. But a relative of the offender
simply takes up the running, gets a license, and in his
turn shares the fate of his predecessor, who then puts in
yet another relative, and so on until at last a distant con-
nection of his wife, fifty times removed, acts as his nomi-
nee. But this seldom happens, as the police “ sling ” the
job and come to terms. Remedy :To cancel the license
of the house.

Dear Bulletin,— Mrs. Brough states to your inter-

N S.VV. tastes, that “Sophia” ran but three weeks in
M lbourne, whereas it went for six in Sydney. The history

A pleasing feature in the Sydney panto, as compared
with both Melbourne productions is the paucity of Jin-
goistic gags, with which the Southern shows fairly bristle.
Another feature, still more pleasing, is that these puerile
threats are received in Sydney with marked apathy, while
in Melbourne they are cheered to the echo. The avowed
intention of wiping the floor with the British by means of
the next Auitrdian Eleven excites a real solid ovation at
the Sydney theatre, while the reference to nigger-butcher
Jameson as a hero is received with a shout of scorn by the
gallery.
There are few males in the Pollard Opera Co. Manager
“ Tom ” contents himself with male comedians only, and
assigns light tenor and baritone roles to the girls. He
knows the tenor Lothario, and' will have none of him.
Also, the public somehow prefers its Lothario to be a
shapely soprano.
“ Good taste must be the music-hall guide nowadays,”
Harry Kickards virtuously informs a Melb. interviewer,
and then you go and see Henry in the evening, cavorting
in petticoats, ami singing a blue song about a lady and
her medical adviser, with slabs of yellow patter let in,
and you understand that tastes differ.
R. S. Smythe was the first acting-manager of Melb.
Opera House. He has a pet anecdote of the first night.
The building (not the present structure) was not unite
completed, and the then not-much-travelled was hanging
about below, keeping an eye on the pay-box, when a
heavy and very dignified swell, with a tall, handsome and
austere daughter upon each arm, who had just taken
tickets for the stalls, was stopped by an attendant making
for the pit-door. The attendant directed him with a word
and a motion of the hand, and the haughty and exclusive
personage turned away like a man whose feelings have
been outraged, and, with an imperial stride, swept his
bouncing daughters into an apartment discreetly labelled,
“ Eor gentlemen only.”
“ A pitiable sight,” writes a Ballarat paper, “ was wit-
nessed at the Exhibition this morning, when the lioness
Psyche, the pet and the best-trained pupil of Mrs. Russell
(a Melb. lady, wife of a well-known dentist—who hits
adopted lion-taming as a profession), was at the point of
death, the immediate cause being inflammation of the
lungs. Mrs. Russell was found by the visitors to be sob-
bing bitterly, with the head of her favorite companion
resting upon her lap. Death occurred shortly after noon.
The distress of Mrs. Russell was most pronounced. She
purchased the animal from the management of Melb.
Zoo three years ago, when it was a ‘ kitten cub,’ and had
trained it as a household pet at her residence in Mel-
bourne, and regarded it as one of the dearest members of
the family, owing to its faithful attachment to her. The
death of the animal came quite suddenly, owing to the
varying temperature of the Exhibition building ; and the
death of Psyche came as a most painful surprise to Mrs.
Russell, whose regret is expressed in her own words : ‘ I
would willingly part with all my worldly belongings than
that Psyche should have died.’ The animal was a
splendid specimen.” Which recalls a memorable painting
shown in the foreign loan-collection at the recent Hobart
Exhibition. It was entitled “The Death of Brutus”—and
represented a lion-tamer and his wife mourning the death
of their splendid lion, whose dead body bad just been
taken from its cage. Eor some utterly inexplicable reason
this picture (by a French artist) was, though very low-
priced, allowed by the controllers of the various ‘public
galleries to return to Europe.
They have got a surprising double for Miss Florence
Young in “ Djin-Djin.” As far as appearances go, Miss
Bertha Place, who plays the Prince at matinees, is hardly
distinguishable from her prototype, and it is only her
voice which would undeceive three-quarters of the audi-
ence.
The Quong Yick Waii Bo (Chinese Sydney newspaper)
on the pantomime at Her Majesty’s Theatre, Sydney
“To please the eyes of youth are dancing-girls with
sha. e.ier limbs than ever one dreamt of. One peculiar
object, which is new to those who have not seen these
things, is to see girls who, to the sight, are without
clothes, dancing behind the shelter of umbrellas. They
are not really naked, but the representation excites the
idea that they are so. Such things would not be allowed
to happen in China ; but we are only men, and we like to
see bare arms, bare legs, and bare bosoms, and it pleases
us to hear a pretty girl so exposed exclaim, ‘I have just
come out of a nice bath,’ making one believe that she is
quite unclothed. The pictures are just like seeing fairy-
land, or what we see in our dreams, and are well worth
the price charged to see them. Do jou wonder that
every Governor and great personage who comes to this
city should exercise bis influence to take the l>est seat in
this theatre for himself and those be favors the most! ”
By the way, what does a Chow see “ in his dreams ”?

Dr. Neild, the veteran Melb. medico and ex-theatrical
critic, was remarking lately to a Bulletin man that he
finds it hard to convince people of the occasional magni-
tude of show specs, in the old days. F"or instance,
Catherine Hayes was a really famous operatic “star”
w hen she came to this country, and sang to prices ranging
from 12s. for the dress-circle. Di Murska, a much more
modern instance, started on the same high terms, but
soon lowered them.

Pollard, of the Liliputian Opera Co., says : “ It is only
to the gallery that the players can effectually submit a
connected story. The gallery comes to watch, to drink
everything in, to be pleased, to he swaved. But the d.c.
people come to spend the evening, to attend now and
then, and to chatter now and then.” This is why the
gallery is the applauding part of the house.

From Princeton, U.S., comes the story of a show mes-
merist who kept a string of half-witted’ subjects to turn
up on the platform and be knocked about, sewn through
the nose and otherwise maltreated for the edification of
the alleged human beings who thronged his halls. The
wage paid the miserable beings so used, as proved after-
wards in a court of law, was - nothing a week. The pool
wretches were fed on the roughest fare, clothed in uIJ
cast-off suits picked up anywhere, made use of ifi every
way possible “in front, and then told that they were re-
ceiving a princely salary. So completely did the mesmer-
ist have their battered faculties under his control that the
poor devils actually believed him, and went elated away
on salary-day.

The late Battenberg is to be buried in the
Isle of Wight “ in accordance with the terms of
his will.” l’oor henpecked Henry was allowed a
will of his own after he was dead, it seems. He
never dared (>ossess one when alive.
Sir George Elliott, the most distinguished
man in the British Parliament, died lately. The
Bulletin gathers that the deceased statesman
was famous for always wearing his hat tilted over
his nose, and his trousers turned up. “Fine or
wet, ’ says a London weekly, “ there is no man
living who can remember to have seen 6ir George
Elliott with his trousers turned down.” In the
other world, no doubt, he is already achieving a
reputation for wearing his halo differently from the
otlier angels.
At a Sydney police-court last week a
perfect “ lydy ” was accused of pulling a large
Quantity of hair from the head of another female
uring a disagreement which took place in the
gutter. Plaintiff produced an extensive handful
of hair as corroborative evidence, but defendant
alleged that it wasn’t pulled out but “came out
in a natural way,” and the case was dismissed.
Query : What is “ the natural way ? ”
Vic. Education Department this year
follows the example of S.A. by issuing a monthly
journal to replace the higher grade school reading-
books, price The “copy” will consist of
articles of Australian interest from books, maga-
zines, and newspapers—including, of course, Bul-
letin poetry. Later on, the department intends
to issue a new series of reading-books wholly
produced in Australia.

MR. DUANE, OF AUCKLAND.
We shall print the whole of Mr. Isaac Duane’s
letter, only regretting that it is not longer. He
might have mentioned that June, the month in
which he wrote, is the coldest month in the year
in his country, and January the hottest. He might
also have reminded us that New Zealand is almost
as large as Great Britain, with a much more
desirable climate. He might further have said
that it is a wholesome place to live in, as it con-
tains few physical sources of disease, and possesses
no insect so noxious as the English wasp. Yet on
reading his letter we shall find that those attractive
islands in the far Southern Sea are not free from
an enemy we are called upon to fight here in Eng-
land, as elsewhere throughout the world.
“ I have much pleasure in writing you,” says
Mr. Duane, “ as to me it is a privilege as well as
a duty to describe an experience in which I am
confident you will be interested.
“ Ever since I was a boy I have not only suffered
from indigestion in its worst form, but I have been
a martyr to it. Such success in life as I have
achieved has been in the face of the constant
opposition set up by this miserable complaint.
All its symptoms are familiar to me as the smoke
of London is to a dweller in that rather grim old
city. The bad taste in the mouth, the fitful
appetite, the distress in the stomach after eating,
the pains in the chest and back, the dull headache,
the sense of weariness and fatigue, the depression
of spirits, the want of ambition to take hold of
any labor, the weakness resulting from lack of
sufficient nourishment, &c.—all these were part
and parcel of my life from my youth to a time I
am going to speak of in a moment.
“ I can only account for it by assuming that I
must have inherited a tendency to this disease.
At all events it cast a gloom over my whole his-
tory up to the date of my recent happy deliverance.
The record of the sleepless, wretched nights I
passed would make almost a volume by itself.
Times beyond counting I have arisen from my
bed in the morning, glad the night was gone, and
yet in no frame of mind to welcome the day. To
the chronic dyspeptic rest does not bring strength
as it does to others.
“You will hardly need to be told that I made
every effort to obtain a cure. I tried medicine
after medicine—now something I thought of my-
self and then something advertised in the news-
papers. And as to doctors (against whom I desire
to say not a word), I tried one after another, and
faithfully used the prescriptions they gave me;
but nothing more than temporary relief came of it.
“ About four years ago a friend spoke to me of
the great reputation of Mother Seigel’s Syrup in
curing all ailments of the digestion, and urged me
to make a trial of it. I might as well say frankly
that I had little faith that it would do me any
good; but I was in such pain that I was in a
mood to try anything that offered the remotest
chance of a cure. So I bought a bottle, and the
very first dose made me feel better. This was so
cheering and hopeful that I continued taking the
Syrup, and to my surprise I grew better and
better until I was cured. All the symptoms which
made my life a burden for so many years are now
gone, and lam a different man. If ever, from
any cause, I have a temporary recurrence of
indigestion, a few doses of Mother Seigel’s Syrup
produce immediate relief, and leave me in good
health. You have my full consent to publish this
letter. lam well known in Auckland, and always
glad to tell anyone by word of mouth what a
wonderful cure your remedy worked in my case.
(Signed) Isaac Duane, coachbuilder, Karangahape
Road, Auckland, New Zealand, June 25th, 1895.”
Our home readers will perceive that not in
England alone but in far distant quarters of the
globe this celebrated medicine is known, and is
successful where others do not avail. It is the
most commonplace of truths that its praises are
sounded wherever civilisation extends, and almost
in every written language the name of Mother
Seigel’s Syrup finds an acknowledged place.*
Self-preservation is the first law of funk,
and the best foot-motor yet invented.
“The worst fix I was ever in,” said
Algernon, the drover (his real name was Bill, but
The Bulletin prefers to call him Algernon—for a
change), “was when I was stuck with 5000 sheep in
the middle of a salt plain six miles from a river.
We reckoned on watering them at the half-way
soak; but every drop was dried up. The sheep
looked as if they couldn’t move another yard.
What did Ido ? I rushed off to the hut-keeper
on the river-bank, put his tank on the dray, filled
it, and started back. My mate Eustace (his real
name was Jim) thought I was crazy, but I told
him to call the dogs and muster up the jumbucks,
and then I backed the cart into the mob and
opened the tank tap. Didn’t they just wake up,
tumbling over one another for a drop. But I
didn’t give ’em much time, for I whipped up the
horse, and away I rattled in the cart across the
plain with the thin stream running from the tap
and the sheep running after it. One after another
scented it, and soon the whole mob were following
at my heels, the whole 5000 all in single file. They
made a line three miles long. Some dropped out
and died, of course, but the rest kept on till we
reached the river. And the dogs soon got the
hang of the dodge : they ran up and down
like corporals with militia-men, and made ’em
keep in line. And you see the advantage of the
single file was this : they were not tumbling
over one another when they did come to the river,
but those on ahead had had enough by the time
the tail came up, and they moved away, so there
was no crowding. ’ ’
Dear Bulletin, —The Yic. Dental Act is
not really so foolish as the confusing par. referring
thereto in a recent issue. The Act provides that
any young man of 21 who has been “continuously
engaged during a period of not less than four years
in the acquirement of professional knowledge and
has passed an examination before the Board ”
shall be entitled to be registered as a dentist. Of
any new-chum who applies for registration on his
merits without passing the local exam., the Board
merely requires that he shall have practised as a
dentist for not less than ten years outside the pro-
vince. If he isn’t a foreign practitioner of ten
years’ standing, he must pass the exam. Four
years’ preparation is not too much to ask of any
young Victorian ; nor is there anything incompre-
hensible in the Board’s desire to prevent local
aspirants to dentistry from going abroad for
their education. The new Vic. Dental College
started last year is, on the authority of a
Sydney tooth-doctor who knows ’Murka well, a
better-equipped institution than can be found in
most of the States. The case you refer to was that
of a Vic. son of wealthy parents who airily
intended to evade the Act. He tripped over to
Philadelphia and returned within 33 months,
brandishing his degree and asking permission to
practise in Melb., where four years’ “ acquirement
of professional knowledge ” is indispensable to
‘ ‘ pass. ” Well, the young magnate suffers no hard-
ship. After putting in the balance of 15 months’
work, he can go before the Vic. Board for exam-
ination, and if his ’Murkan degree be a real testi-
monial to his ability he will have no difficulty in
passing. What’s the matter with the Act ? — Cum
Tree.
Dear Bulletin, — Re aboriginal (?) sten-
cilled hands. The only reason for attributing
these to an earlier race than our present aborigines
is that no aborigine yet questioned by those inte-
rested can say that he saw them being made or
ever heard of them having been made by his race.
Pity “ Creeve Roe ” does not look into some of the
scanty literature on the subject or Carefully
examine some of the imprints before suggesting
that the hands were stamped on in blood. The
hand of the primitive artist (and foot, too, for the
writer knows of stencilled feet) was placed on a
smoke-begrimed cave wall and then stencilled
round with a watery whitish-grey pigment, or the
same pigment in a powdered state on a previously-
dampened or oiled cave surface —the wet stencilling
lasted better. Caves in Wollombi district show
the splashes made with a wet pigment. Some of
this the writer has just as the cave-painter left
it—it comes from Yanco Creek, near Wollombi.
These black hands fade rapidly on being cut out of
a cave and exposed to sunlight and weathering.
Whether the custom had any tribal, religious, or
mythical significance yet remains to be seen—the
writer’s opinion is that of a black he questioned :
“Long time ago, plenty old-feller blackfeller,
plenty play about there when it wet.” Does
any Bulletin reader know of these hands else-
where than in caves of the Hawkesbury sandstone,
or in caves which face other than from E.N.E.
round to W.N.W. (approximately)?— Mindoo-

Barney Bamato’s real name is Isaacs, and
he is the son of a Whitechapel old-clo’ dealer. He
and brother Harry were trained as pugs., and
were reckoned clever with their fists. Barney is
also a brilliant billiardist. “ Mrs.” Barney Bar-
nato was known in South Africa as Fanny Bees.
She is the handsome daughter of an Africander
resident at Simon’s Bay, where, before she met
-tsarney, she used to keep a coffee-stall.
Two females who have run a finished
swindle for some years past, were bowled out
lately in a Maoriland town. One of them—an
exceedingly pretty girl of 25 or so—used to get a
billet as governess or companion, and her modesty,
culture and Christian frame of mind would endear
her to everybody. Then, in a few weeks, she
would be taken seriously ill and go to the hospital,
where, by a judicious arrangement of symptoms,
it appeared that she was an incurable case of
kidney disease. She would, acknowledge re-
luctantly that she had been discharged as incur-
able from other hospitals, but she had concealed
her state from her late employer because (here
she gave way to tears) she might not
otherwise have been engaged, and she wanted
to earn her own living to the last. Also,
she would add that the Lord would provide.
This beautiful state of soul always made her a
favorite with fashionable visitors, and she would
receive more presents than all the rest of the hos-
pital put together. Then one day a humble but
stdl pretty widow would come to the hospital on
some errand of mercy, and be so impressed with
the touching patient that she announced her in-
tention of adopting her, and sharing her modest
crust with her, and soothing her last hours generally.
Beautiful girl adopted accordingly and removed in
tears and a. cab by her benefactress, who would be
last seen kissing her because she was so like her
dear lost daughter. All the benevolent ladies who
haunted the hospital would call on the pious pair
with donations for the bedridden Christian. Also,
the parsons and others would be roused to sympa-
thy and cash. When the cash and the sympathy
were used up the sufferer would go away to die
beautifully to soft music, and start the business all
over again elsewhere. The scheme worked well
for a time, but at last it broke down through
the recklessness of the invalid. Her adopted
parent had gone out in her widow’s-weeds
to look for work, shedding the tear of a
lonesome but resigned female as she went. A
little later the kindly landlady went up to say a
few pleasant words to the dying sufferer, and found
her trying a new corset on before the glass and
doing a riotous high-kick with her golden hair all
hanging down her back. The remainder of the
proceedings consisted mostly of dashes and
hyphens and hysterics and parentheses, and the
invalid fled that night and hasn’t been heard of
since. A beautiful but resigned female with a
hopeless disease should begin to strike the Austra-
lian country hospitals shortly.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Harry Musgrove’s management of the cricket
tour is likely to hurt the feelings of many English gate-
keepers, who have hitherto regarded the Australian
matches as joyous financial picnics. When H.M. comes
round enquiring into the takings and making multiplica-
tion sums which tot up 20 per cent, higher than the
“ returns,” he will astonish a lot of old cormorants at the
game. No previous manager has had anything like a
wide-awake notion as to how much money the “ house ”
i.e., the ground ought to be worth when crowded, or
where the leakages would be most likely to happen.
Musgrove is pretty certain to sniff some cash escapes.
Also, he will call himself in to stop them, as though he
were a plumber, and the check-takers will pass a hasty
resolution to the effect that Australian cricket has de-
generated somethink hawful.
* * * *
Nicknames of last Australian Eleven : H. Trott,
“Joe”; H. T rumble, “Little Eva”; 11. Graham,
“Grummv”; G. Giffen, “Giff”; A. Bannerman, “The
Rat ” ; A.' Jarvis, “ Affy ” i Syd. Gregory, “ Tich ” (after
an English music-hall singer, same size as Syd.) ; Lyons,
“ Blon lin ” (Lyons used to say “I’m Blondin-Im a
doer, not a talker ; I can make 100 runs any time I like.”)
P. C. Charlton (N.S.W.) was always known
among cricketers as “ Frizzy ” ; S. Aus. Jones is “ Jonah ” ;
Iredaie (N.S.W.) is “Noss,” “Nossy” and “King”;
M'Kenzie (N.S.W.) is “ Bronchitis.”

Of the N.S.W. team playing against Vic.,
Garrett (cap.) is in the Registrar-General's Dept.: Donnan
is in the office of the Colonial Sugar Refining Co ;
M'Kenzie is in the Audit Office ; lredale in the Lands ;
M'Kibbin is becoming a solicitor : Howell is a fanner ;
Kelly an employ.: of the Cricket-ground Trustees ;
Walters is an "hoivikeeper ; Turner is an agent ; W. A.
Richardson is in an insurance-office, and Syd. Gregory' has
a cricket and sports depot.
* * * *
Australian cricket hasn’t done much for its
G.O.M. Giffen. He gets £3 per week as a clerk in the
Adelaide Post-office, which is very bad business compared
with what he might have done as a professional cricketer
in England all these .years.
One of the applicants for managership of the
Australian team wanted £5OO, an equal share in the pro-
fits, and all expenses paid. Declined. Another applicant
would “ manaige the trip for half, the gross prophets atid
pay my own expences.” Also declined.
R. M‘Leod, the Melb. cricketer: “It is not the
amateur player that runs away with the money, hut the
professional manager. I think some of the managers
would find considerable trouble in accounting for sundries.
Thev walk about -as big as a house,- and - have a beautiful
time of it whilst we are out on the ground running our
legs off for the honor and glory of the lovely Vic. Assoc.
Do they consider whose money they spend when they
nominate four of themselves to attend the cricket council
in Sydney or Adelaide ? They vote £5O for themselves
and have a picnic. The council is the most useless body
ever formed.”

Published that in a match, Botnadery v. Tome-
rong (Canulen district), the former scored 8 runs in two
innings. One run only' was got off the bat: the rest were
sundries. One bowler got 14 wickets fori run. Tomerong
may have done it, but it looks like tommyrot.

“Thank God !” fervently exclaims H. Trott,
“Thank God ! the next won t be a team of wives.”
* * * •
When the rain last Saturday stopped the play
some of the Vies, read the papers to pass the time. Some-
body found a bit of Latin and wanted a translation.
Bruce, the team’s classic, undertook it. Said A. E. Trott:
“ Isn’t the Latin language dead?” “ Yes,” said Bruce.
“ Well, let it rest in peace; let’s have a game of cards.”

could onlv make 35 runs, on a good wicket, against S.
Adelaide, Jones getting six wickets for 17, and clean
bowling Giffen, Darling and Scrymgour. “ Jonah ” also
made 4tf, top score tor his side.

Wicketkeeper Blaokham was recently nearly
killed by lightning. He saw a great ball ot fire descend-
ing and instinctively caught it and threw it up in the air.
“ How s that ?" said Jack.
• • * *
Dear Bulletin, —Are Zimmerman’s “ last words
in Australia” worth quoting? Because the young man
can sprint his nubbly legs taster than other cyclists, does
it follow that he is a good judge of their relative “ form,”
any more than a good trainer or jockey is necessarily a
wise tipster ? Zim. isn’t a little bit clever, as far as I can
make out. His bike-manufacturing business threatens to
swallow up all his money. When he ran his own show in
Melb. he managed to spoil his game by getting beaten
twice in one evening. vVhat’s the value of his opinion
that Parsons is as good as Harris, and which Harris does
he refer to, any way ? The one in condition who put up
the mile record in England, or the one out of condition
who has performed erratically in Australia? I make out
that Harris is quite as neat a rider as Parsons, and half as
strong again. In the matter of common sense, Martin,
the strongest of the three, beats them both. —F.
The defeat of Harris by Mystery recalls the
Melb. match between Woods, the English champion
cyclist, and five trotters, turned on one at a time at the
end ot each mile. Woods was ingloriously beaten both in
this and in a similar match at Christchurch, M.L., some
months later, but his performance on a hard-tyred ordi-
nary over five miles ranks far superior to that of the
Leicester lad last week. Harris's defeat is inexplicable ;
the day was perfect and the track not so lumpy as to ac-
count for the absence ot any sprint at the nnish. The
writer would like to see Mystery and any racing cyclist
around Mein, have a go on st. Kilda road.
* * » »
The moral of the Mystery v. Harris match is
that a “ onampion ’ cycl.st deserves to be beaten by a
horse. The Englishman rode without an ounce of brains.
He was supposed to be capable of getting over a mile in
less time tnari the trotting-mare comd record. Also, he
was cocksure that he could beat her in a short sprint.
Tnen all he had to do was to take the lead at the start,
thereby forcing the trotter to come along and get in front
of him, and so make a pace, or otherwise ensuring his
own victory by staying in front until it was time to ueat
the animal for speed in a sprint home. Instead of which
he hung behind the sulky for seven furlongs in both
heats, failed to come to tiie front at the finish, and leit
the course with the plaintive cry that he could have won
easily if the}’ had allowed him a tandem to take him
along. It's time that a buck nigger, or a Chow, became
cycling champion of the Universe.

The match was farcical and uninteresting.

The way Porta won the mile Intercolonial
Scratch Race shows that the Italian long-distance wheeler
is beginning to spurt. The race was a crawl for three laps,
and when the racing did start Parsons, Martin, and Lewis
had to finish behind the Italian.

Martin’s disqualification, after beating Lewis by
three-quarters of a lap in the Live Miles Track Champion-
ship, at A.N.A. meeting, was the hardest of luck, and the
surprise is that Lewis should have lodged a protest.
Seemingly Martin could have had a nap on the track and
then beaten Lewis.

At Mary vale Station, Upper Burnekin, two
spotted deer were turned 1 >ose some years ago by Mr.
Hann, a well-known North Queenslander. They have
vastly increased in numbers, and live mostly in the tea-
tree scrubs along the creeks. They are exceedingly wary
and fleet of foot, and cannot be approached—either or»
foot or on horseback.
* « * •
The paucity of first-class horses in Australia—
animals capable of carrying weight and getting over a
long journey in good time—has of late evoked consider-
able comment. Though the fact is obvious, little thought
is given the subject until big handicaps come round. Take
the principal cash-spending event of the year. Three-
quarters ot the entrants are sifted out by the talent as
rubbish ; a reservation is made as to three-year-oids ; the
top-weights may be regarded as overburdened ; which
leaves the middle division, most of whom are selected as
havi -chances—if they can stay. Then Australia, young
and old, spends months studying “ form ” books, figuring
out if they can stay, or are likely to stay, though sup-
posed to be unable to. The average backer calculates
thuswise, unless he has a special fancy for one of the
leading division who can stay, or has been put on to a
nonentity, which eventually turns out to have as much

Mr. A. L. Baird writes that at the carnival held
at Sydney Natatorium the will-registering machine
could not be properly w'orked on account of the great
crush around it constantly interfering. The heats, too,
were run very quickly. He is prepared, he says, to prove
that under proper conditions the machine is ail that it is
claimed to be.

Trotting, in Sydney, is as dead as Julius C’fesar’s
step-brother. Never under proper control, or given a
decent show all to itself, there’s no cause for wonder.
The clubs that gave trotting events a place on their pro-
grammes discontinued them on the alleged grounds of
audacious swindling and unutterable rascality generally
which is regrettable from a sporting and general stand-
point. But if they were worse than some ot the pony
“ races ” they must have been very, very bad.
In M.L. wealthy trotting-elubs flourish in every
part ol the country. They own well-appointed tracks,
give rich stakes, and provide good sport. Men ot stand-
ing, breeders ol horses and others, patronise and control
the sport. This, combined with the tote, is responsible
tor the elevation ot trotting above the level of magsmen s
races. The machine is the only legitimate betting agency
that conduces to the advancement and encouragement of
honest, sport,. It doesn't own horses, or nobble those who
do own them. Nor does it whisper to the jockey as he s
going out. L'ntd its arrival, trotting will continue to be
dead and festering in Sydney.
The marked improvement in the breed of the
trotter in M.L. during the last few years is a matter of
note. The importation of strains from America and else-
where has effected great alteration, and breeders find a
good market there. Mr. Sam Hordern yearly, sends a
batch across for auction, and has endowed two events of
the Auckland Trotting Club with good stakes for com-
petition among aniitials of his own raising.

The Argus berates Turner and Co. for
yielding to the opinions of Parliament instead of
fighting tooth-and-claw for a hard-and-fast pro-
gramme. “ The old practice, ” snorts the Argus,
“ is for a Ministry to have a policy, and to tight
that policy for all it is worth.” The Argus prin-
ciple in politics is “win, tie, or wrangle;” any
kind of Conservative confusion being preferable to
Democratic progress, however limping.
Sydney Telegraph, in its commercial
column, January 23 :

If he can ! Think of the Telegraph beginning
to totter in its faith like this, and to wonder if the

not be “ more burdensome “ in this case than the

Poor Battenberg’s bad luck stuck to him
to the last. He was the son of a small German
prince by his morganatic marriage with a high-
kick wtress, and the potentates of Europe wouldn’t
recogi. se him. Then he was a Prussian officer of
a low j,rade, and had an income of JffiO a year all
told, and legend asserts that he had to clean his
own boots. He married a British princess,
Victoria’s favorite daughter, who threatened to
develope into an old maid, and, having no means
of his own. he lived on his mother-in-law. The
whole royal family kept him in order, and made
him sit consistently on the hard chair next the
door, and he was put into kilts and other
humiliations. Then his wife became lf>-stone or
thereabouts, and took to riding a bicycle. And,
finally, this fine figure of a man grew weary of it
all, and went away to earn some laurels and seek
peace in the prospective Ashantee war, and he
caugh.t the first fever which came along and died
without once getting -into action. Poor Batten-
berg ! The so-called French Prince Imperial
poked his nose into the Zulu shambles, and stayed
there. Fighting Burnaby “waggedit” into the
Soudan troubles, and a common Arab spear was
Fate’s reproof. Moral: Lay to your book.

The women are said to have shown the
men an example of coolness and courage on the
occasion of the fire on the Cintra :
The dames stand on the burning deck
Whence all the men have fled,
And do not seem to care a speck
Although the smoke rolls red.
What makes the women seem so cool
When manly bosoms fail '!
Their confidence is in the rule
“No smoking aft this rail.”
Re the Cintra fire, it is most wonderful
that more fires don’t break out in the steerage
quarters of the Western steamers. Most of the
W.A. boats have reserved a slice out of their
cargo-holds, fitted up four tiers of bunks three or
four abreast, and called the result a “steerage.”
There are no portholes, and the only air entering
the apartment is drawn from the open hatch
(closed in rough weather). As this is also the only
source of light, the gloom of the rear bunks may
be imagined. All day and night candles and
lanterns in these quarters'consume nine-tenths of
the air which enters, and, after one steerage trip
West, the average man would prefer to sleep in
the engine-room or the stoke-hole.

An £asy Interview.
The way of the interviewer—like that of the
transgressor—is oftentimes hard, but this was not
the experience of the writer who interviewed Mr.
John Lawton, of Brunswick Cottage, Brunswick
Street, Brisbane, Queensland. In fact, Mr.
Lawton seemed willing, even eager, to relate his
experience, which we give below m his own words :
“Having suffered severely from rheumatism in
my legs and feet for many years, being at times
for a month together a complete cripple, and un-
able to put my boots Cn or walk about the floor,
and also having tried Allopathy, Homoeopathy,
Hydropathy, and goodness knows what other
‘ pathy, ’ I was induced by a friend to try Warner’s
‘ Safe ’ Remedies, and I must say I was very
sceptical about it at first. In fact I had begun to
think I was doomed to continual suffering for the
rest of my life. However, as ‘ perseverance re-
moves mountains,’ so Warner’s Safe Rheumatic
Cure removed my mountain of misery, and here I
am now perfectly free from it, having had for the
last five years nothing beyond a gentle reminder,
on receipt of which I immediately fly to my ‘ Safe ’
and obtain almost instant relief. This being so, I
feel it my duty, as well as a great pleasure, to
give this entirely unsolicited testimonial in the
hopes that by doing so I may be instrumental in
assisting some other sufferer.” *

Professor Loisette continues to announce his readi-
ness to impart his system of memory training to
those who desire to leam it through the medium
of the branch he has established in Sydney, the
said branch being charged with looking after his
interests throughout all the islands of the Pacific
Ocean, including Australasia. The professor was
incorrectly reported some time since as having
forgotten his umbrella on a recent visit to a young
ladies’ college on the occasion of his lecturing
there. Yet, if the young ladies had been as fascina-
ting as was supposed, the professor could scarcely
have been blamed for such a small slip of memory.*

lous. Over and over attain we see a horse, a certainty for
the race m which he is entered, “ stiffened ” in lavor of
some second-rater which, more often than not, is beaten,
lfie stilt is kept tor some big* event, backed heavily, and
runs second or third. Then the uisgusted owner puts him
up tor eteile, and gets about a third of his proper price tor

to a long-priced chance, enters him for some small race,
and the horse romps home. That’s the difference between
cunning and common-sense.
As a' rule pony-punters, unless well-informed,
deter backing until the pomes come out for their prelim-
inary gallops ; they then enquire of the jockeys, “ What
do you like, Micky 7 " “What's your fancy, Billy V”
When Micky, riding Doormat, tells his pals to back Boot-
scraper, everybody knows that Doornmt is “ dead ” (un-
less Micky is doing a “ double cross ”), but the stewards
never seem to notice it, and the iai ce of racing is kept up.
I lie proprietors of the pony courses are greatly
exercised in their minds at the latest development of
bookmaking. At nearly every course around .Sydney a
number of “ books congregate outside on some eminence
and bet “ thripinnies ’ and “ sprats” and “ deeners ” with
people who can t a.lord to pay admission-fee. One reason
way this class of “ bookies ” gets so many patrons is their
comparative ignorance of the merits of the horses en-
gaged., A jockey-boy in the paddock knows a “good
tiling ; he can t get better than even money in the pad-
docK or Leger, so he goes to the “ books ’’ outside, and,
finding that they know nothing about the aforementioned
• good thing,” puts a few shillings on with each of them
at five or six to one, and makes a naul.
At one or two of the little courses the trot still
flourishes, and is invariably the most amusing item on the
programme. Seldom, indeed, is there more than one
“ trier, hut the antics of the others are instructive.
Sometimes the “dead” trotter finds himseif dangerously
near winning, much to the alarm of owner and jock.
Thereiore the latter makes his mount break into a canter,
when, according to the rules, he must pull up and start
again, this proceeding loses him a lot of ground and
may be repeated when necessary.

An Australian bookmaker went to ’Frisco to
make a bonk. He made it. He came to a bank one
morning with lOOOdol. in bills—winnings. He tendered
those bills. There was a low whistle, and instantly the
bookmaker was down, choked, with two men over him.
We ve got the forger this time,’’ they said. Then the
Australian bookmaker explained that he was a young man
from the country called Australia, and he had just won
those bills from a client. Having satisfied the' bank
authorities, he w'as allowed to depart

The very oldest inhabitant of Port Fairy
district went into Belfast (Vic.) during the recent
holidays to see a locomotive for the first time.
His son, aged 50, went with him for the same pur-
pose. The oldest inhabitant —the person with
one reliable tooth who gibbers about turnips by
the fireside—is always breaking out like this.
His chief distinction, in most cases, is the number
of things he hasn’t seen, and the enormous length
of time during which he has never removed from
his own district, and when one meets him he
inspires a problem that is akin to a nightmare—
what would a district exclusively peopled by oldest
inhabitants be like ? Such districts can be found
in England—or they could 30 years ago. The
present writer remembers one. It was an agricul-
tural hamlet, and every soul in it had been bom
and lived his whole life within three miles—
except old George Smith. George Smith
was an irreproachable person aged 60, who
earned 12s. a week by gardening. He went to
the Baptist chapel and the prayer-meeting
regularly, and every Saturday evening he scrupu-
lously polished his boots and read the Christian
World—he never read anything else except the
Bible, and he never drank or smoked, and hadn’t
been in a theatre or a cab or a train all his life,
and he had a plain, irreproachable daughter named
Sarah. But in his youth he had removed—by
road—from exactly such another village 15 miles
away, and he never quite got over it. People
viewed old George Smith as a person of a roving
disposition who was liable to break out at any
moment and be an extensive traveller, or an ex-
plorer, or even go to Wolverhampton and attend
the Baptist Conference. They felt there was a
want of stability and deep-rootedness about him,
and old, sound, agricultural persons didn’t like
their sons to call round and borrow Smith’s
Christian World for fear they should become
unsettled in their minds—which was quite need-
less, for they hadn’t any. Finally, Smith did
break out, for he shifted into another cottage a
quarter of a mile down the road, and there he died
from rheumatiz, his wages having been reduced to
10s. a week in his 64th year.

In all the big European maternity hospi-
tals, incubators are now used to keep up the vital
heat in prematurely-born children, and those of
weak physique. Some of them are specially
designed for the purpose ; others are simply
ordinary poultry-incubators. They have saved
hundreds of lives.

vice. The effect is at first very stimulating, but
the results of indulgence are extreme nervousness,
insomnia and breakdown.

HEAT-DRINK.
When the heat-wave overwhelms the land;
when the sun devours the day and the moon
mocks the burning night; when the bleating
of sheep, the carol of birds and the insects’
hum are stilled in the hush of deatli ; when
the leaves struck dead by the sun’s fierceness
make their graves around the doomed troes ;
when pastures are deserts; lagoons and
billabongs Golgothas of bones and festering
carcases ; when the swamp grasses are dust
and the river reeds ashes ; when men whose
brothers are dead see the heat-rent
earjdi yawning graves on every side ;
when the carrion crow flies from the
scene of nature’s crimes southward on the
wings of fear ; then, and not till then, does
general humanity form a conception of the
agonies of thirst.
& * * * *
And everyone is thirsty ; thirsts with a
great thirst. The pop of alcoholic corks is
mixed with the fizzing of teetotal wash and
the thud of the heat-stroke corpse. And,
say many leading Sydney doctors, with the
more frequentthud of the drink corpse. Heat
kills a few ; thirst, several ; drink, many.
Sydney Sunday Times, having suffered like
the rest, sent out its special to several
eminent medicos to get a prescription for a
parched throat. Sydney Daily Telegraph
also interviewed the big men of small cures
and large bills. * *
The medical testimony which is condemna-
tion may be summarised thus. Intoxicating
beverages are bad—very bad ; non-intoxi-
cating are also bad—also very bad. Here
are the points of detail.
BEERS, WINES AND SPIRITS
produce the bladder and kidney complaints
so prevalent in Austraha. They also de-
velop the unwieldy and wrong-thinking
belly, the red, inhuma u snout ; the apathetic
brain. Says Bismarjk : “ Beer makes men
stupid, lazy, impotent.”
MILK !
Menacing drink ! Fraught with probable
dreadful disease ! Sydney’s every week sees
some distributor of milk brought up at the
police court and fined for selling impure or
adulterated milk. Were the dairyman always
the cause he should always be hung. But he
is nearly always not to blame. It is the
milk ! the milk ! The real criminal is the
cow. Look not upon the milk when it is
white lest you issue direct challenge to tuber-
culosis and slow, agonising death. Tempt
not Providence, for Providence, though
omniscient and omnipotent, is, on evidence,
not beyond temptation.
LEMON SQUASH !
The name suggests the man consumed with
bellyache, with dysentery, with death.
Besides, the human race is sour enough
already.
SODA !
Who hath shaky legs 1 Who hath bended
knees ? Who hath the spine of the jelly-
fish T He that dallies long with soda and
the enfeeblement thereof.
• * • *
TEA !
Alas ! it is the cup enclosed by the serpent
of scandal. Tea is indigestion, which is the
devil, which is the tomb, which is the devil
again and for ever.
* * • •
COFFEE!
Ends in coffee poisoning which injures brain,
nerve and stomach worse than alcohol. A
man hasn’t time to get D.T.’s from coffee —
it flattens him out too soon.
• * . • •
iERATED WATERS AND TEMPER-
ANCE TIPPLE !
Contain the curse alcohol ; often contain as
many bacteria as ordinary water.
FRUIT-FLAVOURED essences
As a rule are rank alcoholic poison.
* * * *
WATER !
God preserve the idiot who has water on the
brain. Water ! hydatids ! bacteria ! fluke !
microbes ! dysentery ! typhoid ! thirst!
death !
• * * *
ICE !
Lets all these circumstances into the human
craw, with the additional ill that they are
frozen. Ice has made America synonymous
with chronic dyspepsia.
* * * •
FILTERS !
With one or two rare exceptions, are breed-
ing - grounds for microbes, seed - beds of
disease. Not long ago, iir India, a very fatal
epidemic was only cheeked by the abolition
of filters.
* * * *
Thus do the doctors take away from us in-
toxicants and non-intoxicants ; beer, wines,
spirits ; serated waters, milk, tea, coffee, ice,
water, soda, &c. What shall we drink I
Hire’s Root Beer ! It fulfils the conditions
pointed out by the doctors ; The water in
it has hern filtered scientifically, and sterilised ;
the other ingredients are roots, bark, herbs,
scientifically prepared.
* * ,* »
If the Sydney doctors recently emphasised
anything, they emphasised the value of
sterilisation. But, in addition to having the
negative virtues that the doctors asked for,
it has the positive virtues of being strength-
ening, gently stimulating, sparkling, effer-
vescing and appetising. It was introduced
from America to Australia some 12 months
ago. In America, it is no uncommon thing
for a railway trait' of 30 cars to travel from
Philadelphia to San Francisco with nothing
but Hire’s Tmot Beer. The Australian
agents are Shirley Clayton & Co., of Pitt-
street, Sydney. 1000 cases, representing |
ot a million of gallons are now on the way
from America, and millions more are to
follow. Thus is the great question of thirst
solved for ever.
The Read just meat of the Ecliptic.
Dear Bulletin’,— Let us put all the special-
prayer weather-workers out of their agony. Some
want to supplicate the Almighty to moderate the
furnaces of the sun, and to turn off the “ heat-
wave.” Others are frightened because of infeli-
citous experiences after the recent Brunker-
Saumarez-Bavin prayers for rain—some fellows
having had their crops swamped ; others being
still in drought. Now, I have a plan to cure all
these inequalities mundane, and to fix things up
evenly and everlastingly. I have long been per-
fect ing an invention, unparalleled in human his-
toiy, but resting on simple natural laws. Only I
need Capital, and I give The Bulletin the chance
to scoop that capita] in for the benefit of the
afflicted earth. Here’s the

Professors of Natural Physics, Metaphysics, Psychics, and
Omniologies,

Invention for the Readjustment of the Ecliptic, whereby
the Earth’s Axis shall he made Perpendicular to the Plane
of the Earth’s Orbit; whereby—
1. There shall be equal day and equal night all over the
Earth all the Year round.
2. There shall be one permanent, equal, benevolent
season all the year round.
The advantages of this enterprise are obvious.
All men will then be able to sow or plant or
harvest just when they darn well please.
Special advantages to squatters: You can postpone
Lambing until you’re ready for it.
Attractions for the Eight-Hour Party : You will have
12 hours day and 12 hours night all the year round, and
can arrange your fixtures with greater than present con-
venience.
Notice to Tee-Companies: There will be no more
natural ice except at very high altitudes, and the Arti- :
ficial Ice-Companies must boom. Now’s the time to rush |
these shares.

THE DIPSOMANLAC.
Maoriland Anti-gambling League wants to
put down betting in all forms because it leads to
“improvidence, extravagance, living beyond one’s
means, and a general disregard of thrift and
prudence.” And the very first item on the
League’s balance - sheet reads —“ Overdraft at
bank, .£187.”
A song that knocks “patriotic” Austra-
lians in Melbourne describes how brave and
noble Jameson with his band of heroes went
forth to fight the Boers, which proves “ that
Britons will be conquerors ever,” and “British
subjects never will be slaves.” The fact that
Jameson and his heroes were complacently mopped
up by the vulgar Boer is ingenuously disregarded,
and the fatuous audience uproariously encores the
shrill and breathless singer.
Melb. women cyclists are now almost all
clad in full dress—“ Guvment ’Ouse” having
frowned on bloomers. All the same, there is
amongst the wheelwomen a decided hankering
after breeches, and it is rumoured that the majority
wear them in their evening spins, with a thin skirt
above at starting. When away from town the
skirt is discarded. Some bloomered Melb. Uni-
versity girls have been riding astride through the
backblocks of Gippsland. They were generally
taken for portions of a stray circus.
The cable states that “ the German
Emperor, addressing the leading statesmen and
officers of the army, waved the German standard
and swore to defend the empire.” This kind of
pomp and frill grows rather tiring. Why
should the military department of the State
always conduct itself in this childish fashion and
go waving things, when the other branches can
get on without it? The Treasurer doesn’t wave a
big banner now and then and swear to keep ac-
counts straight, and the Fite Gibbon office doesn’t
flourish a garish standard and cheer and put on a
rainbow uniform, and take a great oath in public
that it will see that large beasts don’t get into the
water-supply. Have the military no sense of
humor that they play the goat in this fashion ?
Swallow and Ariell, of Melb., have just
introduced a new line of fancy biscuits, in boxes
decorated with pictures of girls so charming that
people who buy the biscuits will have the greatest
difficulty in refraining from devouring the box.
This firm’s tinned plum puddings, desiccated
soups, compressed vegetables, and canned fruits,
not to mention their cakes, biscuits, and preserves,
are famous all over Australia ; and in the wildest
recesses of this great continent the man who
fancies himself an explorer will find a beer-bottle
and a Swallow and Ariell tin. Such vogue can
only be due to one thing—Al quality.*
The portentous progress of Smith is the
most mournful thing about John Sands’ Sydney
Directory for 1896. Smith welters over nine
closely-printed columns of Directory, and his off-
shoots, Smyth, and Smythe, and Smithers, and
Smithson, and Smithwick, and Smitherick help
him to fill a column more. This is not counting
Smish, who appears this year for the first time,
but whose device is so original and impressive that
it is bound to be widely initated. So far as Sydney
is concerned, most of Smith are named John, and
the remainder of him are chiefly labelled George.
The prejudice in favor of attaching John to three
out of every four little Smiths who reach this
planet seems to flourish rather than decay. Leav-
ing out the overwhelming fact of Smith, John
Sands’ Sydney Directory for 1896 is only a model
publication of its kind—full, accurate, up to date,
and indispensable.

In round figures, Tasmania has one lawyer
for every 900 of the population, a magistrate for
every 240, a doctor for every 1500, a chemist for
j every 1600, and one Chinaman to every 150 whites.
There are nearly 1000 Chinamen in Tasmania and

other thing.
. The true opium-poppy can be well-grown
m N.S.W. _ The plant flowers about 15 weeks
after planting, when the he ais can easily be
gathered by women or children. Poppies run
about 40,000 heads to the acre, which, at the usual
£1 per 1000, means a return to the grower of some
£4O per acre, and a substantial addition to an
enterprising farmer’s income.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long."
Dear Bulletin, — Re black - trackers : I
was in Coonamble Courthouse, some years ago,
when there was a cattle-stealing case on big
enough to have brought barrister David Buchanan
from Sydney. A lame, full - blooded darkey
described how he tracked the stolen (fat) bullocks,
and the horses of those who drove them from the
creek, where they were joined by a lot of working
bullocks ; after travelling together a further dis-
tance the working bullocks separated and went
down the creek, while the fat ones took another
direction, and he described their subsequent pro-
ceedings till they (or a portion of them) were
found by him and the police. Buchanan saw his
chance, and, when his turn for cross-examination
came, led off with some harmless preliminary
questions ; then, suddenly changing his tone, he
thundered out, with a ferocity calculated to make
an aboriginal turn pale: “Now, you’re a very
clever black-tracker, and you’ve learned your story
well. Will you tell his Honor and the jury
how you knew the working bullocks went down
the creek and not the fat bullocks ? ” With a
look of pity for the ignorance of the town man,
the darkie explained : “You see, fat bullock, he
bin strong, walk smart, this way (imitating),
make it clean track. Workin’ bullock, he bin tired,
drag his foot, make it mark longa ground, this
way.” The tracker’s “hoppy ” walk heightened the
amusement caused by his pantomime and the dis-
comfiture of Buchanan, whose subsequent cross-
exam. was only a spiritless “ do-something-for-
your-fee ” business. His client “ fell in ” —even a
Coonamble jury of that day failing to raise a
reasonable doubt for the benefit of a fellow-worker
in the moonlighting industry. Another instance :
Two kangaroo-shooters, at Ulimambir Station,
reported to the Coonabarabran police that 200
scalps had been stolen from their camp. Unluckily
for the thieves, there was a private mark on
each scalp. ' Sergeant Ewing, with Peter
Murphy, a black-tracker, nosed about the
camp till the tracker got on the trail, and
reported that the thieves were two persons
on horseback, one leading a pack-horse with a
large pack on the near side, and that they
were travelling towards Coolah in the dark. They
tracked them close to Coolah, where their tracks
were crossed by a large mob of travelling sheep,
and obliterated. They found one of the horses,
however, dead-beat and abandoned (a stolen Ulim-
ambir horse). After long delay they again
picked up the tracks beyond where the sheep had
been and followed them to a house somewhere
beyond Cassilis, where they found the horses
hobbled out, and two men whom they arrested on
suspicion. Enquiry showed that some of the
scalps had been sold at Merriwa. They were
identified by the private marks, and the buyer re-
cognised the prisoners as those who sold them to
him. The men were convicted. The scalps had
been carried in two wheat-sacks, one on each side
of the horse. Peter Murphy explained in evi-
dence that he knew one was a led horse because
the tracks were always parallel. There was evi-
dently a large pack on the side, because the two
horses travelled wide apart and never came
together or jostled, the pack keeping them asunder.
It was dark, because sometimes they got off the
road, and more than once they ran into a wire-
fence where it was parallel with and near the
road. The blacks’ wonderful tracking feats are
due as much to close and subtle reasoning as to
keen, trained eyesight.—Bush Native.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-out No. 1,” “Cameo ” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“THREE CASTLES” and “ CAPSTbA-HsT.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
