HIS ROYAL HIGHNESS THE
of
“Billy,”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The subjoined narration—in respectful
contravention of “ Banjo’s ” statements in last
Bulletin Xmas number that the sheep is not
ferocious and “ no one could ever say that a sheep
attacked him without provocation is a humble
offering upon the altars of natural history and
sacred truth.
* * » *
The British Army in India mollifies itself
with pets—apes, cheetahs, Bengal tigers, and
cognate foundlings. But there is no beast undtr
the sky that so completely fills the British soldier’s
aching void for unadulterated cussedness as a
ram. In heaven above, the earth beneath, the
water under the earth, or below that, there is
nothing more broilsome, nothing of a more demo-
niacal pugnacity than a fighting ram. On
“ J ndia ’ s coral strand ” Nizams, Nabobs, Mahara-
jahs, and Rajahs compete for the possession of
rams of attested extraordinary bellicose capacity.
* * * *
Several years ago, when H.M.’s 3rd
Dragoon Guards lay at Mhow (E. 1.), thev had
namcrfl^nv^ 11 !? 6 * a pecul j arl y profligate ram
i Was exceed ingly beloved, while
he lasted, because he was so wicked. He was the
means of disseminating unjust suspicions and of
shattering friendships. He nearly killed the
senior-major. Also, he was grossly unprincipled.
Did a provident dragoon place a pannikin of arak
convenient for night consumption, Billy drank it
and the innocent were blamed. Bellicose in his
most sober moments, Billy, after half-a-pint of
rum, would have unhesitatingly attacked a
hundred tons of mad elephant. Failing elephants,
he would, if not too drunk to find his way, sally
to the horse-lines, and there gratuitously and
brutally attack a certain splendid man-eating
stallion. This horse, the terror of the ghora-tvalas
and of the regiment, had killed at least three of
the former and eaten a good deal of them, and had
mangled several of the latter. He was picketed
alone, with special pegs and double head-and-heel
ropes. Even thus he could reach effectively with
his teeth to his second rib on either side, and to
either flank with his hind legs; he could do as
much devastation with his forelegs a,s a Nasmyth
hammer in full working order. But, always sup-
posing the ropes held, there was a space between
ribs and flank which he could not negotiate or
* Billy had studied out this fact, and, when
fighting drunk, would stagger down, take the
bearings of the defenceless spot, and then butt the
horse in the stomach until he (Billy) had no more
butt left in him. Then he would lie down, re-
cover his wind, and go swaggering and guffawing
back to barracks like the debauched old Silenus
he was. The entire, frightened of nothing else on
earth or elsewhere, was terrorised by Billy and
fairly shrieked whenever he approached. One night
the former, while punishment was proceeding, got
loose. The natives, gating from a safe place, pre-
dicted Billy’s demise in one lightning act. As a
matter of fact the man-eater bolted, yelling with
rage, pain and fear, while Billy sat down and
laughed till the tears ran down his face. So
knocked-about and scared was the stallion that he
was easily secured next morning in an adjacent
and hf \ ha J, with the exception of making
little mischief * Ben * ah baby or two > done
* * * *
Like many habitual drunkards, Billy was
immoderately addicted to tobacco, which he would
beg borrow, or steal-steal for choice. “ Tokey ”
Willmm and “Spud” Murphy “cut the loaf”
entirely owing to Billy’s malign influence. They
had occupied adjacent cots and had been sworn
comrades for years, until the former was re-
peatedly robbed of the tobacco he cached
beneath his pillow, wherewith to soothe him-
selt in wakeful watches of the night. He
accused Spud of larcenies, arid the repose of the
REMOVABLE.

troop was often ruptured by lurid language
and two hairy veterans prancing around in abbre-
viated night-wear. Spud climaxed his offence
by—so Tokey affirmed—seizing the latter when
asleep by the heels and violently bashing his head
against the bungalow wall. There was pugilism
en deshabille at dead of night between the antique
warriors, and compulsory retirement to the guard-
room. Which things might have been obviated
had “ Foxy ’’ Smith (awake and enjoying trans-
actions) explained that it was Billy who stole and
ate the tobacco, and Billy who, incited thereto by
“Foxy,” accepting Tokey’s uncovered toes
waggling in the moonbeams as provocations to
combat, reined back and butted Tokey’s feet.
After Billy dis-
covered the
amount of holy
joy there was to
be snatched from
this kind of re-
laxation, he rang-

A King in Exile,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
0, the Queen may keep her golden
Crown and sceptre of command !
I would give them both twice over
To be King of Babyland.
Sure, it is a wondrous country
Where the beanstalks grow apace,
And so very near the moon is
You could almost stroke her face.
And the dwellers in that country
Hold in such esteem their King,
They believe that if he chooses
He can do—just anything ;
And, although his regal stature
May be only four-feet-ten,
Think him tallest, strongest, bravest,
Noblest, wisest, best of men.
Ah, how fondly I remember
The good time serene and fair,
In the bygone years when I, too,
Was a reigning monarch there !
But my subjects they discrowned me
When they’d older, colder, grown,
And they took away my sceptre,
And upset my royal throne.
Yet, although a King in Exile,
Without subjects to command,
I am glad at heart to think I
Once was King of Babyland.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Saturday Review considers that no Judge
of English High Court should be allowed to sit
after 70 years of age, rarely after 65, and that
ordmardy Judges should be compelled to retire at
60. How would this work in Australia ?
Some years ago, when the British terri-
tories began to touch the Transvaal, the Cape
Government suggested that a certain degree of
longitude should be fixed as the Boer southern
boundary. The line, as marked on the map, was
inspected by the Boer authorities, and a commis-
sion sent out to view the country. It came back
!JT a weeks, much excited, and reported that
it had carefully examined the locality, but there
was no line there, and the existence of such lines
was evidently a lie invented by the English. The
Boer politicians imagined that a meridian was a
black streak on the earth’s surface, and they had
been looking for it.


Hume Nisbet paid a flying visit to Aus-
tralia recently, and reports that he has inspected
the present generation of Sydneyites, and finds
them a tired, degenerate race, out of which all
pluck and energy has departed—a weary, played-
out, jaundiced crowd, without mind and with a
thin, weedy body, which is used chiefly to make
an acute angle against a lamp-post outside a
public-house. From which The Bulletin assumes
that Mr. Nisbet’s books aren’t selling well in
Sydney just now. You can almost always tell
how any author’s books are selling in a city by the
way he cusses the inhabitants thereof.

The Master’s Mistake.
[For The Bulletin.]
William Spencer stayed away from school
that day, and the “master” wrote a “note” to
William’s father, and gave it to William’s brother
Joe to carry home.
“ You’ll give that to your father to-night,
Joseph.”
“Yes, sir.”
Bill waited for Joe near the gap, and walked
home with him.
“ I s’pose you’ve got a note for father.”
“ Yes,” said Joe.
“I s’pose you know what’s in it ? ”
“ Ye—yes. Oh, why did you stop away, Bill?”
“You don’t mean to say that your’e dirty mean
enough to give it to father ? Hey ? ”
“ I must, Will. I promised the master.”
“ He needn’t never know.”
“ Oh, yes, he will. He’s coming over to our
place on Saturday, and he’s sure to ask me to-
morrow.”
Pause.
“ Look here, Joe !” said Bill, “ I don’t want to
get a hiding and go without supper to-night. I
promised to go ’possuming with Jimmy Nowlett,
and he’s going to give me a fire out of his gun.
You can come, : too. I don’t want to cop out on it
to-nigh G-if I do I’ll run away from home again,
so there.”
Bill walked on a bit in moody silence; Joe, in
troubled silence.
Bill tried again : he threatened, argued, and
pleaded, but'Joe was firm. “The master trusted
me, Will,” he said.
“ Joe,” said Bill at 1; si, after a long pause, “I
wouldn’t do it to you.”
Joe was troubled.
“ I wouldn’t do it to you, Joe.”
Joe'thought how Bill had stood up and fought
for him only last week.
“I’d tear the note in bits; I’d tell a hundred
lies ; I’d take a dozen hidings first, Joe —I
would. ”
Joe was greatly troubled. His chest heaved
and the tears came to his eyes.
“I’d do more than that for you, Joe, and you
know it.”
Joe knew it. They were crossing the old gold-
field, now. There was a shaft close to the path ;
it had fallen in, funnel-shaped, at the top, but
was still 30 or 40 feet deep; some old logs were
jammed across about five feet down. Joe suddenly
snatched the note from his pocket and threw it in.
It fluttered to the other side and rested on a piece
of the old timber. Bill saw it, but said nothing,
and, seeing their father coming home from work,
they hurried on.
Joe was in trouble now. Bill tried to comfort
and cheer him, but it was no use. Bill promised
never to run away from home any more, to go to
school every day, and never to fight, nor steal, nor
tell lies. But Joe had betrayed his trust for the
first time in his life, and wouldn’t be comforted.
Some time in the night Bill woke, and found
Joe sitting up in bed crying.
“ Why, what’s the matter, Joe ? ”
“I never done a mean thing like that before,”
sobbed Joe. “ I wish I’d chucked meself down
the shaft instead. The master trusted me, Will;
an’ now, if he asks me to-morrow, I’ll have to tell
a lie.” ~
“ Then tell the truth, Joe, an’ take the hidin ;
it’ll be soon over —just a couple of cuts with the
cane and it’ll be all over.”
“Oh, no, it won’t. He won’t never trust me
any more. I’ve never been caned in that school
yet, Will, and if I am I’ll never go again. Oh !
why will you run away from home, Will, and
play the wag, and steal, and get us all into such
You don’t know how mother takes on
about it—you don’t know how it hurts father !
I’ve deceived the master, and mother and father
to-day, just because you’re so—so selfish,” and he
laid down and cried himself to sleep.
Bill lay awake and thought till daylight ; then
he got up quietly, put on his clothes, and stole away
from the house and across the flat, followed by the
dog, who thought it was some ’possum-huhting
expedition. Bill wished the dog would not be
quite so demonstrative until at least they got
away from the house. He went straight to the
shaft, let himself down carefully on to one of the
old logs, and stooped to pick up the note, gleam-
ing white in the sickly summer daylight. Then
the rotten “timber’’gave way suddenly, without
a moment’s warning.
» * * *
They found him that morning at about
nine o’clock. The dog attracted the attention of
an old fossicker passing to his work. The letter
was gripped in Bill’s right hand when they
brought him up. They took him home and the
father went for a doctor. Bill came to himself a
little just before the last, and said : “ Mother ! I
wasn’t running away, mother—tell father that—l
—I wanted to try and catch a ’possum on the
SHE DOESN'T LIKE HIM NOW.
SHE (off to Marchesi): “Do you think I can ever do anything with my
voice? ”
HE: “ Well, it may do in case of fire.”
ground. . . Where’s Joe ? I want Joe. Go
out, mother, a minute, and send Joe. ”
“ Here I am, Bill,” said Joe, in a choking,
terrified voice.
“ Has the master bin yet ? ”
“No.”
“ Bend down, Joe. I went for the note, and
the logs gave way. I meant to be back before they
was up. I dropped it down inside the bed ; you
watch your chance and get it; and say you forgot it
last night—say you didn’t like to give it that
won’t be a lie. Tell the master I’m —I’m sorry
tell the master never to send no notes no more—
except by girls—that’s all . . . Mother ! Take
the blankets off me—l’m dyin’. ”
HENRY LAWSON.
Hungering and Thirsting.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ever since she could remember, the
mother-love had been the ruling passion of her
life. As a child, filled with that pathetic faith
which a man had better be drowned in the depth
of the sea than destroy, she had clasped her rag-
and-sawdust doll to her breast, closed her eyes,
and prayed to God to turn it into a baby for her.
Once, in a burst of ecstatic delight, it seemed to
her that some responsive thrill answered the beat-
ing of her heart. When, at last, she dared to look
down, it was
with an agony
of disappoint-
ment that she
met that fool-
ish waxen
smile.
As a school-
girl she had
worshipped
each baby
that toddled
across her
path, and had
thought glad
thoughts and
dreamed sweet
dreams of her
own time that
was coming.
Clearly pray-
ers alone could
not help her,
so, with what
patience she
had, she wait-
ed until she
should be
grown up
enough to
marry.
* *
She had no
passion in her,
only tender,
motherly love.
A little of this
she gave to her
husband, and
all the rest she
stored up and
waited—wait-
ed at first
eagerly and
hopefully, and then with a restless uncertainty
hard to bear. , ~ „ A
And the months passed and then the years, ana
still the yearning agony of would-be motherhood
was unsatisfied. In dreams she quivered with the
rapture of the touch of baby fingers and hot, moist
little lips. Then poverty came and her dreams
by night and day were of the delight of denying
herself, of starving herself if need be, that little
mouths might be filled and little forms kept
vigorous. These remained only dreams.
“ I’ve sometimes been awfully down to think we
had no kiddies,” said her husband, worn with the
grind of poverty, “but we’ve both of us cause to
be thankful enough now.” But his wife said
n °Then"came better times, and still she waited
and waited and tried to hope, though she knew
that the thirst of her life could never bo quenched.
To the last the man did not know.
* * * *
He was brought home with a dislocated
spine and but a few hours between him and the
shadowy land. Sometimes he spoke, but more
often lay motionless thinking with an over-active
brain and counting the moments as they passed.
“I wish I could have left you better off,” he
said once ; “ but, thank God, there is no child to
burden you. Queer that I should have all my
wits about me when I am so smashed up.”
She pressed his hand and stooped to kiss him.
She walked to the window and leaned against the
frame. Those words rang through her brain
where all else was numb. ‘ ‘ Thank God there is
no child. Thank God there is no child.”
A sturdy, independent baby escaped from the
house opposite. With a face filled with the glory
of successful wrong-doing he staggered out, bare-
headed, into the hot sunshine. “You naughty
boy,” called the mother running after him, “ what
can mummy do with you ? ” She caught him up
with a little shake, then clasped him close and
kissed his face all over as she ran with him back
to the verandah. The shrill baby-laughter echoed
across the road. “ Thank God there is no child!”
The woman bowed her head upon the arms that
rested on the window-ledge, and the tears dropped
through her fingers slowly as if keeping time to
the man’s last lingering moments, dragging pain-
fully to an end.
Brisbane.
M.A.B.
